<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>3. The Vertebral Column</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center">(Columna Vertebralis; Spinal Column).</DIV><BR><BR>The <B>vertebral column</B> is a flexuous and flexible column, formed of a series of bones called <B>vertebr&#230;.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The vertebr&#230; are thirty-three in number, and are grouped under the names <B>cervical, thoracic, lumbar, sacral,</B> and <B>coccygeal,</B> according to the regions they occupy; there are seven in the cervical region, twelve in the thoracic, five in the lumbar, five in the sacral, and four in the coccygeal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;This number is sometimes increased by an additional vertebra in one region, or it may be diminished in one region, the deficiency often being supplied by an additional vertebra in another. The number of cervical vertebr&#230; is, however, very rarely increased or diminished.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The vertebr&#230; in the upper three regions of the column remain distinct throughout life, and are known as <B>true</B> or <B>movable</B> vertebr&#230;; those of the sacral and coccygeal regions, on the other hand, are termed <B>false</B> or <B>fixed</B> vertebr&#230;, because they are united with one another in the adult to form two bones&#151;five forming the upper bone or <B>sacrum,</B> and four the terminal bone or <B>coccyx.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;With the exception of the first and second cervical, the true or movable vertebr&#230; present certain common characteristics which are best studied by examining one from the middle of the thoracic region.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>3a. General Characteristics of a Vertebra</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>A <B>typical vertebra</B> consists of two essential parts&#151;viz., an anterior segment, the <B>body,</B> and a posterior part, the <B>vertebral</B> or <B>neural arch;</B> these enclose a foramen, the <B>vertebral foramen.</B> The vertebral arch consists of a pair of <B>pedicles</B> and a pair of <B>lamin&#230;,</B> and supports <B>seven processes</B>&#151;viz., four <B>articular,</B> two <B>transverse,</B> and one <B>spinous.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus82.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=143 SRC="/107/Images/small/image82.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i82"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>82&#150; </FONT>A typical thoracic vertebra, viewed from above. (<A HREF="/107/illus82.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;When the vertebr&#230; are articulated with each other the bodies form a strong pillar for the support of the head and trunk, and the vertebral foramina constitute a canal for the protection of the medulla spinalis (<I>spinal cord</I>), while between every pair of vertebr&#230; are two apertures, the <B>intervertebral foramina,</B> one on either side, for the transmission of the spinal nerves and vessels.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Body (<I>corpus vertebr&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The body is the largest part of a vertebra, and is more or less cylindrical in shape. Its upper and lower surfaces are flattened and <!-- PAGE NUM="97" -->rough, and give attachment to the intervertebral fibrocartilages, and each presents a rim around its circumference. In front, the body is convex from side to side and concave from above downward. Behind, it is flat from above downward and slightly concave from side to side. Its anterior surface presents a few small apertures, for the passage of nutrient vessels; on the posterior surface is a single large, irregular aperture, or occasionally more than one, for the exit of the basi-vertebral veins from the body of the vertebra.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Pedicles (<I>radices arci vertebr&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The pedicles are two short, thick processes, which project backward, one on either side, from the upper part of the body, at the junction of its posterior and lateral surfaces. The concavities above and below the pedicles are named the <B>vertebral notches;</B> and when the vertebr&#230; are articulated, the notches of each contiguous pair of bones form the intervertebral foramina, already referred to.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Lamin&#230;.</B>&#151;The lamin&#230; are two broad plates directed backward and medialward from the pedicles. They fuse in the middle line posteriorly, and so complete the posterior boundary of the vertebral foramen. Their upper borders and the lower parts of their anterior surfaces are rough for the attachment of the ligamenta flava.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Processes.&#151;Spinous Process (<I>processus spinosus</I>).</B>&#151;The spinous process is directed backward and downward from the junction of the lamin&#230;, and serves for the attachment of muscles and ligaments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articular Processes.</B>&#151;The articular processes, two superior and two inferior, spring from the junctions of the pedicles and lamin&#230;. The superior project upward, and their articular surfaces are directed more or less backward; the inferior project downward, and their surfaces look more or less forward. The articular surfaces are coated with hyaline cartilage.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Transverse Processes (<I>processus transversi</I>).</B>&#151;The transverse processes, two in number, project one at either side from the point where the lamina joins the pedicle, between the superior and inferior articular processes. They serve for the attachment of muscles and ligaments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure of a Vertebra <A HREF="/107/illus83.html">(Fig. 83</A>).</B>&#151;The body is composed of cancellous tissue, covered by a thin coating of compact bone; the latter is perforated by numerous orifices, some of large size for the passage of vessels; the interior of the bone is traversed by one or two large canals, for the reception of veins, which converge toward a single large, irregular aperture, or several small apertures, at the posterior part of the body. The thin bony lamell&#230; of the cancellous tissue are more pronounced in lines perpendicular to the upper and lower surfaces and are developed in response to greater pressure in this direction <A HREF="/107/illus83.html">(Fig. 83</A>). The arch and processes projecting from it have thick coverings of compact tissue.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus83.html"><IMG WIDTH=224 HEIGHT=137 SRC="/107/Images/small/image83.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i83"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>83&#150; </FONT>Sagittal section of a lumbar vertebra. (<A HREF="/107/illus83.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>3a. 1. The Cervical Vertebr&#230;</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Vertebr&#230; Cervicales).</B></DIV><BR><BR><The <B>cervical vertebr&#230;</B> <A HREF="/107/illus84.html">(Fig. 84)</A> are the smallest of the true vertebr&#230;, and can be readily distinguished from those of the thoracic or lumbar regions by the presence of a foramen in each transverse process. The first, second, and seventh present exceptional features and must be separately described; the following characteristics are common to the remaining four.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>body</B> is small, and broader from side to side than from before backward. The <B>anterior and posterior surfaces</B> are flattened and of equal depth; the former <!-- PAGE NUM="98" -->is placed on a lower level than the latter, and its inferior border is prolonged downward, so as to overlap the upper and forepart of the vertebra below. The <B>upper surface</B> is concave transversely, and presents a projecting lip on either side; the <B>lower surface</B> is concave from before backward, convex from side to side, and presents laterally shallow concavities which receive the corresponding projecting lips of the subjacent vertebra. The <B>pedicles</B> are directed lateralward and backward, and are attached to the body midway between its upper and lower borders, so that the superior vertebral notch is as deep as the inferior, but it is, at the same time, narrower. The <B>lamin&#230;</B> are narrow, and thinner above than below; the <B>vertebral foramen</B> is large, and of a triangular form. The <B>spinous process</B> is short and bifid, the two divisions being often of unequal size. The <B>superior</B> and <B>inferior articular processes</B> on either side are fused to form an articular pillar, which projects lateralward from the junction of the pedicle and lamina. The articular facets are flat and of an oval form: the superior look backward, upward, and slightly medialward: the inferior forward, downward, and slightly lateralward. The <B>transverse processes</B> are each pierced by the <B>foramen transversarium,</B> which, in the upper six vertebr&#230;, gives passage to the vertebral artery and vein and a plexus of sympathetic nerves. Each process consists of an anterior and a posterior part. The <B>anterior</B> portion is the homologue of the rib in the thoracic region, and is therefore named the <B>costal process</B> or <B>costal element:</B> it arises from the side of the body, is directed lateralward in front of the foramen, and ends in a tubercle, the <B>anterior tubercle.</B> The <B>posterior</B> part, the true transverse process, springs from the vertebral arch behind the foramen, and is directed forward and lateralward; it ends in a flattened vertical tubercle, the <B>posterior tubercle.</B> These two parts <!-- PAGE NUM="99" -->are joined, outside the foramen, by a bar of bone which exhibits a deep sulcus on its upper surface for the passage of the corresponding spinal nerve.<A NAME="txt15">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/21.html#note15">15</A></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus84.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=126 SRC="/107/Images/small/image84.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i84"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>84&#150; </FONT>A cervical vertebra. (<A HREF="/107/illus84.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus85.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=98 SRC="/107/Images/small/image85.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i85"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>85&#150; </FONT>Side view of a typical cervical vertebra. (<A HREF="/107/illus85.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>First Cervical Vertebra.</B>&#151;The first cervical vertebra <A HREF="/107/illus86.html">(Fig. 86)</A> is named the <B>atlas</B> because it supports the globe of the head. Its chief peculiarity is that it has no body, and this is due to the fact that the body of the atlas has fused with that of the next vertebra. Its other peculiarities are that it has no spinous process, is ring-like, and consists of an anterior and a posterior arch and two lateral masses. The <B>anterior arch</B> forms about one-fifth of the ring: its anterior surface is convex, and presents at its center the <B>anterior tubercle</B> for the attachment of the Longus colli muscles; posteriorly it is concave, and marked by a smooth, oval or circular facet (<I>fovea dentis</I>), for articulation with the odontoid process (<I>dens</I>) of the axis. The upper and lower borders respectively give attachment to the anterior atlantooccipital membrane and the anterior atlantoaxial ligament; the former connects it with the occipital bone above, and the latter with the axis below. The <B>posterior arch</B> forms about two-fifths of the circumference of the ring: it ends behind in the <B>posterior tubercle,</B> which is the rudiment of a spinous process and gives origin to the Recti capitis posteriores minores. The diminutive size of this process prevents any interference with the movements between the atlas and the skull. The posterior part of the arch presents above and behind a rounded edge for the attachment of the posterior atlanto&#246;ccipital membrane, while immediately behind each superior articular process is a groove (<I>sulcus arteri&#230; vertebralis</I>), sometimes converted into a foramen by a delicate bony spiculum which arches backward from the posterior end of the superior articular process. This groove represents the superior vertebral notch, and serves for the transmission of the vertebral artery, which, after ascending through the foramen in the transverse process, winds around the lateral mass in a direction backward and medialward; it also transmits the suboccipital (first spinal) nerve. On the under surface of the posterior arch, behind the articular facets, are two shallow grooves, the <B>inferior vertebral notches.</B> The lower border gives attachment to the posterior atlantoaxial ligament, which connects it with the axis. The <B>lateral masses</B> are the most bulky and solid parts of the atlas, in order to support the weight of the head. Each carries two articular facets, a superior and an inferior. The <B>superior facets</B> are of large size, oval, concave, and approach each other in front, but diverge behind: they are directed upward, medialward, and a little backward, each forming a cup for the corresponding condyle of the occipital bone, and are admirably adapted to the nodding movements of the head. Not infrequently they are <!-- PAGE NUM="100" -->partially subdivided by indentations which encroach upon their margins. The <B>inferior articular facets</B> are circular in form, flattened or slightly convex and directed downward and medialward, articulating with the axis, and permitting the rotatory movements of the head. Just below the medial margin of each superior facet is a small tubercle, for the attachment of the transverse atlantal ligament which stretches across the ring of the atlas and divides the vertebral foramen into two unequal parts&#151;the anterior or smaller receiving the odontoid process of the axis, the posterior transmitting the medulla spinalis and its membranes. This part of the vertebral canal is of considerable size, much greater than is required for the accommodation of the medulla spinalis, and hence lateral displacement of the atlas may occur without compression of this structure. The <B>transverse processes</B> are large; they project lateralward and downward from the lateral masses, and serve for the attachment of muscles which assist in rotating the head. They are long, and their anterior and posterior tubercles are fused into one mass; the foramen transversarium is directed from below, upward and backward.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus86.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=117 SRC="/107/Images/small/image86.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i86"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>86&#150; </FONT>First cervical vertebra, or atlas. (<A HREF="/107/illus86.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus87.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=175 SRC="/107/Images/small/image87.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i87"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>87&#150; </FONT>Second cervical vertebra, or epistropheus, from above. (<A HREF="/107/illus87.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Second Cervical Vertebra.</B>&#151;The second cervical vertebra (<A HREF="/107/illus87.html">Fig. 87</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus88.html">88</A>) is named the <B>epistropheus</B> or <B>axis</B> because it forms the pivot upon which the first vertebra, carrying the head, rotates. The most distinctive characteristic of this bone is the strong odontoid process which rises perpendicularly from the upper surface of the body. The <B>body</B> is deeper in front than behind, and prolonged downward anteriorly so as to overlap the upper and fore part of the third vertebra. It presents in front a median longitudinal ridge, separating two lateral depressions for the attachment of the Longus colli muscles. Its under surface is concave from before backward and covex from side to side. The <B>dens</B> or <B>odontoid process</B> exhibits a slight constriction or neck, where it joins the body. On its anterior surface is an oval or nearly circular facet for articulation with that on the anterior arch of the atlas. On the back of the neck, and frequently extending on to its lateral surfaces, is a shallow groove for the transverse atlantal ligament which retains the process in position. The <B>apex</B> is pointed, and gives attachment to the apical odontoid ligament; below the apex the process is somewhat enlarged, and presents on either side a rough impression for the attachment of the alar ligament; these ligaments connect the process to the occipital bone. The internal structure <!-- PAGE NUM="101" -->of the odontoid process is more compact than that of the body. The <B>pedicles</B> are broad and strong, especially in front, where they coalesce with the sides of the body and the root of the odontoid process. They are covered above by the superior articular surfaces. The <B>lamin&#230;</B> are thick and strong, and the vertebral foramen large, but smaller than that of the atlas. The <B>transverse processes</B> are very small, and each ends in a single tubercle; each is perforated by the foramen transversarium, which is directed obliquely upward and lateralward. The <B>superior articular surfaces</B> are round, slightly convex, directed upward and lateralward, and are supported on the body, pedicles, and transverse processes. The <B>inferior articular surfaces</B> have the same direction as those of the other cervical vertebr&#230;. The <B>superior vertebral notches</B> are very shallow, and lie behind the articular processes; the <B>inferior</B> lie in front of the articular processes, as in the other cervical vertebr&#230;. The <B>spinous process</B> is large, very strong, deeply channelled on its under surface, and presents a bifid, tuberculated extremity.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus88.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=140 SRC="/107/Images/small/image88.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i88"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>88&#150; </FONT>Second cervical vertebra, epistropheus, or axis, from the side. (<A HREF="/107/illus88.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus89.html"><IMG WIDTH=175 HEIGHT=183 SRC="/107/Images/small/image89.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i89"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>89&#150; </FONT>Seventh cervical vertebra. (<A HREF="/107/illus89.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--

</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Seventh Cervical Vertebra <A HREF="/107/illus89.html">(Fig. 89</A>).</B>&#151;The most distinctive characteristic of this vertebra is the existence of a long and prominent spinous process, hence the name <B>vertebra prominens.</B> This process is thick, nearly horizontal in direction, not bifurcated, but terminating in a tubercle to which the lower end of the ligamentum nuch&#230; is attached. The <B>transverse processes</B> are of considerable size, their posterior roots are large and prominent, while the anterior are small and faintly marked; the upper surface of each has usually a shallow sulcus for the eighth spinal nerve, and its extremity seldom presents more than a trace of bifurcation. The foramen <!-- PAGE NUM="102" -->transversarium may be as large as that in the other cervical vertebr&#230;, but is generally smaller on one or both sides; occasionally it is double, sometimes it is absent. On the left side it occasionally gives passage to the vertebral artery; more frequently the vertebral vein traverses it on both sides; but the usual arrangement is for both artery and vein to pass in front of the transverse process, and not through the foramen. Sometimes the anterior root of the transverse process attains a large size and exists as a separate bone, which is known as a <B>cervical rib.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>3a. 2. The Thoracic Vertebr&#230;</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Vertebr&#230; Thoracales).</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>thoracic vertebr&#230;</B> <A HREF="/107/illus90.html">(Fig. 90)</A> are intermediate in size between those of the cervical and lumbar regions; they increase in size from above downward, the upper vertebr&#230; being much smaller than those in the lower part of the region. They are distinguished by the presence of facets on the sides of the bodies for articulation with the heads of the ribs, and facets on the transverse processes of all, except the eleventh and twelfth, for articulation with the tubercles of the ribs.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus90.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=172 SRC="/107/Images/small/image90.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i90"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>90&#150; </FONT>A thoracic vertebra. (<A HREF="/107/illus90.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>bodies</B> in the middle of the thoracic region are heart-shaped, and as broad in the antero-posterior as in the transverse direction. At the ends of the thoracic region they resemble respectively those of the cervical and lumbar vertebr&#230;. They are slightly thicker behind than in front, flat above and below, convex from side to side in front, deeply concave behind, and slightly constricted laterally and in front. They present, on either side, two costal demi-facets, one above, near the root of the pedicle, the other below, in front of the inferior vertebral notch; these are covered with cartilage in the fresh state, and, when the vertebr&#230; are articulated with one another, form, with the intervening intervertebral fibrocartilages, oval surfaces for the reception of the heads of the ribs. The <B>pedicles</B> are directed backward and slightly upward, and the inferior vertebral notches are of large size, and deeper than in any other region of the vertebral column. The <B>lamin&#230;</B> are broad, thick, and imbricated&#151;that is to say, they overlap those of subjacent vertebr&#230; like tiles on a roof. The <B>vertebral foramen</B> is small, and of a circular form. The <B>spinous process</B> is long, triangular on coronal section, directed obliquely downward, and ends in a tuberculated extremity. These processes <!-- PAGE NUM="103" -->overlap from the fifth to the eighth, but are less oblique in direction above and below. The <B>superior articular processes</B> are thin plates of bone projecting upward from the junctions of the pedicles and lamin&#230;; their articular facets are practically flat, and are directed backward and a little lateralward and upward. The <B>inferior articular processes</B> are fused to a considerable extent with the lamin&#230;, and project but slightly beyond their lower borders; their facets are directed forward and a little medialward and downward. The <B>transverse processes</B> arise from the arch behind the superior articular processes and pedicles; they are thick, strong, and of considerable length, directed obliquely backward and lateralward, and each ends in a clubbed extremity, on the front of which is a small, concave surface, for articulation with the tubercle of a rib. <!-- PAGE NUM="104" -->The <I>first, ninth, tenth, eleventh,</I> and <I>twelfth</I> thoracic vertebr&#230; present certain peculiarities, and must be specially considered <A HREF="/107/illus91.html">(Fig. 91</A>).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus91.html"><IMG WIDTH=214 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image91.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i91"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>91&#150; </FONT>Peculiar thoracic vertebr&#230;. (<A HREF="/107/illus91.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>First Thoracic Vertebra</B> has, on either side of the <B>body,</B> an entire articular facet for the head of the first rib, and a demi-facet for the upper half of the head of the second rib. The body is like that of a cervical vertebra, being broad transversely; its upper surface is concave, and lipped on either side. The <B>superior articular surfaces</B> are directed upward and backward; the <B>spinous process</B> is thick, long, and almost horizontal. The <B>transverse processes</B> are long, and the upper vertebral notches are deeper than those of the other thoracic vertebr&#230;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Ninth Thoracic Vertebra</B> may have no demi-facets below. In some subjects however, it has two demi-facets on either side; when this occurs the tenth has only demi-facets at the upper part.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Tenth Thoracic Vertebra</B> has (except in the cases just mentioned) an entire articular facet on either side, which is placed partly on the lateral surface of the pedicle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In the <B>Eleventh Thoracic Vertebra</B> the <B>body</B> approaches in its form and size to that of the lumbar vertebr&#230;. The articular facets for the heads of the ribs are of large size, and placed chiefly on the pedicles, which are thicker and stronger in this and the next vertebra than in any other part of the thoracic region. The <B>spinous process</B> is short, and nearly horizontal in direction. The <B>transverse processes</B> are very short, tuberculated at their extremities, and have no articular facets.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Twelfth Thoracic Vertebra</B> has the same general characteristics as the eleventh, but may be distinguished from it by its inferior articular surfaces being convex and directed lateralward, like those of the lumbar vertebr&#230;; by the general form of the body, lamin&#230;, and spinous process, in which it resembles the lumbar vertebr&#230;; and by each transverse process being subdivided into three elevations, the superior, inferior, and lateral tubercles: the superior and inferior correspond to the mammillary and accessory processes of the lumbar vertebr&#230;. Traces of similar elevations are found on the transverse processes of the tenth and eleventh thoracic vertebr&#230;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus92.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=133 SRC="/107/Images/small/image92.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i92"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>92&#150; </FONT>A lumbar vertebra seen from the side. (<A HREF="/107/illus92.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>3a. 3. The Lumbar Vertebr&#230;</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Vertebr&#230; Lumbales).</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>lumbar vertebr&#230;</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus92.html">Figs. 92</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus93.html">93</A>) are the largest segments of the movable part of the vertebral column, and can be distinguished by the absence of a foramen in the transverse process, and by the absence of facets on the sides of the body.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>body</B> is large, wider from side to side than from before backward, and a little thicker in front than behind. It is flattened or slightly concave above and <!-- PAGE NUM="105" -->below, concave behind, and deeply constricted in front and at the sides. The <B>pedicles</B> are very strong, directed backward from the upper part of the body; consequently, the inferior vertebral notches are of considerable depth. The <B>lamin&#230;</B> are broad, short, and strong; the <B>vertebral foramen</B> is triangular, larger than in the thoracic, but smaller than in the cervical region. The <B>spinous process</B> is thick, broad, and somewhat quadrilateral; it projects backward and ends in <!-- PAGE NUM="106" -->a rough, uneven border, thickest below where it is occasionally notched. The <B>superior</B> and <B>inferior articular processes</B> are well-defined, projecting respectively upward and downward from the junctions of pedicles and lamin&#230;. The facets on the superior processes are concave, and look backward and medialward; those on the inferior are convex, and are directed forward and lateralward. The former are wider apart than the latter, since in the articulated column the inferior articular processes are embraced by the superior processes of the subjacent vertebra. The <B>transverse processes</B> are long, slender, and horizontal in the upper three lumbar vertebr&#230;; they incline a little upward in the lower two. In the upper three vertebr&#230; they arise from the junctions of the pedicles and lamin&#230;, but in the lower two they are set farther forward and spring from the pedicles and posterior parts of the bodies. They are situated in front of the articular processes instead of behind them as in the thoracic vertebr&#230;, and are homologous with the ribs. Of the three tubercles noticed in connection with the transverse processes of the lower thoracic vertebr&#230;, the superior one is connected in the lumbar region with the back part of the superior articular process, and is named the <B>mammillary process;</B> the inferior is situated at the back part of the base of the transverse process, and is called the <B>accessory process</B> <A HREF="/107/illus93.html">(Fig. 93</A>).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus93.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=178 SRC="/107/Images/small/image93.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i93"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>93&#150; </FONT>A lumbar vertebra from above and behind. (<A HREF="/107/illus93.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus94.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=195 SRC="/107/Images/small/image94.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i94"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>94&#150; </FONT>Fifth lumbar vertebra, from above. (<A HREF="/107/illus94.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Fifth Lumbar Vertebra</B> <A HREF="/107/illus94.html">(Fig. 94)</A> is characterized by its body being much deeper in front than behind, which accords with the prominence of the sacrovertebral articulation; by the smaller size of its spinous process; by the wide interval between the inferior articular processes; and by the thickness of its transverse processes, which spring from the body as well as from the pedicles.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>3a. 4. The Sacral and Coccygeal Vertebr&#230;</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The <B>sacral</B> and <B>coccygeal vertebr&#230;</B> consist at an early period of life of nine separate segments which are united in the adult, so as to form two bones, five entering into the formation of the sacrum, four into that of the coccyx. Sometimes the coccyx consists of five bones; occasionally the number is reduced to three.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Sacrum (<I>os sacrum</I>).</B>&#151;The sacrum is a large, triangular bone, situated in the lower part of the vertebral column and at the upper and back part of the pelvic cavity, where it is inserted like a wedge between the two hip bones; its upper part or base articulates with the last lumbar vertebra, its apex with the coccyx. It is curved upon itself and placed very obliquely, its base projecting forward and forming the <B>prominent sacrovertebral angle</B> when articulated with the last lumbar vertebra; its central part is projected backward, so as to give increased capacity to the pelvic cavity.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Pelvic Surface (<I>facies pelvina</I>).</B>&#151;The pelvic surface <A HREF="/107/illus95.html">(Fig. 95)</A> is concave from above downward, and slightly so from side to side. Its middle part is crossed by four <B>transverse ridges,</B> the positions of which correspond with the original planes of separation between the five segments of the bone. The portions of bone intervening between the ridges are the bodies of the sacral vertebr&#230;. The body of the first segment is of large size, and in form resembles that of a lumbar vertebra; the succeeding ones diminish from above downward, are flattened from before backward, and curved so as to accommodate themselves to the form of the sacrum, being concave in front, convex behind. At the ends of the ridges are seen the <B>anterior sacral foramina,</B> four in number on either side, somewhat rounded in form, diminishing in size from above downward, and directed lateralward and forward; they give exit to the anterior divisions of the sacral nerves and entrance to the lateral sacral arteries. Lateral to these foramina are the <B>lateral parts of the sacrum,</B> each consisting of five separate segments at an early period of life; in the adult, these are blended with the bodies and with each other. Each lateral part is traversed <!-- PAGE NUM="107" -->by four broad, shallow grooves, which lodge the anterior divisions of the sacral nerves, and are separated by prominent ridges of bone which give origin to the Piriformis muscle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;If a sagittal section be made through the center of the sacrum <A HREF="/107/illus99.html">(Fig. 99),</A> the bodies are seen to be united at their circumferences by bone, wide intervals being left centrally, which, in the fresh state, are filled by the intervertebral fibrocartilages. In some bones this union is more complete between the lower than the upper segments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus95.html"><IMG WIDTH=262 HEIGHT=275 SRC="/107/Images/small/image95.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i95"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>95&#150; </FONT>Sacrum, pelvic surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus95.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Dorsal Surface (<I>facies dorsalis</I>).</B>&#151;The dorsal surface <A HREF="/107/illus96.html">(Fig. 96)</A> is convex and narrower than the pelvic. In the middle line it displays a crest, the <B>middle sacral crest,</B> surmounted by three or four tubercles, the rudimentary spinous processes of the upper three or four sacral vertebr&#230;. On either side of the middle sacral crest is a shallow groove, the <B>sacral groove,</B> which gives origin to the Multifidus, the floor of the groove being formed by the united lamin&#230; of the corresponding vertebr&#230;. The lamin&#230; of the fifth sacral vertebra, and sometimes those of the fourth, fail to meet behind, and thus a hiatus or deficiency occurs in the posterior wall of the sacral canal. On the lateral aspect of the sacral groove is a linear series of tubercles produced by the fusion of the articular processes which together form the indistinct <B>sacral articular crests.</B> The articular processes of the first sacral vertebra are large and oval in shape; their facets are concave from side to side, look backward and medialward, and articulate with the facets on the inferior processes of the fifth lumbar vertebra. The tubercles which represent the inferior articular processes of the fifth sacral vertebra are prolonged downward as rounded <!-- PAGE NUM="108" -->processes, which are named the <B>sacral cornua,</B> and are connected to the cornua of the coccyx. Lateral to the articular processes are the four <B>posterior sacral foramina;</B> they are smaller in size and less regular in form than the anterior, and transmit the posterior divisions of the sacral nerves. On the lateral side of the posterior sacral foramina is a series of tubercles, which represent the transverse processes of the sacral vertebr&#230;, and form the <B>lateral crests</B> of the sacrum. The transverse tubercles of the first sacral vertebra are large and very distinct; they, together with the transverse tubercles of the second vertebra, give attachment to the horizontal parts of the posterior sacroiliac ligaments; those of the third vertebra give attachment to the oblique fasciculi of the posterior sacroiliac ligaments; and those of the fourth and fifth to the sacrotuberous ligaments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus96.html"><IMG WIDTH=283 HEIGHT=250 SRC="/107/Images/small/image96.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i96"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>96&#150; </FONT>Sacrum, dorsal surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus96.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Lateral Surface.</B>&#151;The lateral surface is broad above, but narrowed into a thin edge below. The upper half presents in front an ear-shaped surface, the <B>auricular surface,</B> covered with cartilage in the fresh state, for articulation with the ilium. Behind it is a rough surface, the <B>sacral tuberosity,</B> on which are three deep and uneven impressions, for the attachment of the posterior sacroiliac ligament. The lower half is thin, and ends in a projection called the <B>inferior lateral angle;</B> medial to this angle is a notch, which is converted into a foramen by the transverse process of the first piece of the coccyx, and transmits the anterior division of the fifth sacral nerve. The thin lower half of the lateral surface gives attachment to the sacrotuberous and sacrospinous ligaments, to some fibers of the Glut&#230;us maximus behind, and to the Coccygeus in front.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus97.html"><IMG WIDTH=190 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image97.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i97"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>97&#150; </FONT>Lateral surfaces of sacrum and coccyx. (<A HREF="/107/illus97.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus98.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=166 SRC="/107/Images/small/image98.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i98"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>98&#150; </FONT>Base of sacrum. (<A HREF="/107/illus98.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Base (<I>basis oss. sacri</I>).</B>&#151;The base of the sacrum, which is broad and expanded, is directed upward and forward. In the middle is a large oval articular surface, <!-- PAGE NUM="109" -->the upper surface of the body of the first sacral vertebra, which is connected with the under surface of the body of the last lumbar vertebra by an intervertebral <!-- PAGE NUM="110" -->fibrocartilage. Behind this is the large triangular orifice of the sacral canal, which is completed by the lamin&#230; and spinous process of the first sacral vertebra. The superior articular processes project from it on either side; they are oval, concave, directed backward and medialward, like the superior articular processes of a lumbar vertebra. They are attached to the body of the first sacral vertebra and to the al&#230; by short thick pedicles; on the upper surface of each pedicle is a vertebral notch, which forms the lower part of the foramen between the last lumbar and first sacral vertebr&#230;. On either side of the body is a large triangular surface, which supports the Psoas major and the lumbosacral trunk, and in the articulated pelvis is continuous with the iliac fossa. This is called the <B>ala;</B> it is slightly concave from side to side, convex from before backward, and gives attachment to a few of the fibers of the Iliacus. The posterior fourth of the ala represents the transverse process, and its anterior three-fourths the costal process of the first sacral segment.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus99.html"><IMG WIDTH=158 HEIGHT=275 SRC="/107/Images/small/image99.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i99"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>99&#150; </FONT>Median sagittal section of the sacrum. (<A HREF="/107/illus99.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus100.html"><IMG WIDTH=115 HEIGHT=299 SRC="/107/Images/small/image100.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i100"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>100&#150; </FONT>Coccyx. (<A HREF="/107/illus100.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Apex (<I>apex oss. sacri</I>).</B>&#151;The apex is directed downward, and presents an oval facet for articulation with the coccyx.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Vertebral Canal (<I>canalis sacralis; sacral canal</I>).</B>&#151;The vertebral canal <A HREF="/107/illus99.html">(Fig. 99)</A> runs throughout the greater part of the bone; above, it is triangular in form; below, its posterior wall is incomplete, from the non-development of the lamin&#230; and spinous processes. It lodges the sacral nerves, and its walls are perforated by the anterior and posterior sacral foramina through which these nerves pass out.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The sacrum consists of cancellous tissue enveloped by a thin layer of compact bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The sacrum articulates with <I>four</I> bones; the last lumbar vertebra above, the coccyx below, and the hip bone on either side.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Differences in the Sacrum of the Male and Female.</B>&#151;In the female the sacrum is shorter and wider than in the male; the lower half forms a greater angle with the upper; the upper half is nearly straight, the lower half presenting the greatest amount of curvature. The bone is also directed more obliquely backward; this increases the size of the pelvic cavity and renders the sacrovertebral angle more prominent. In the male the curvature is more evenly distributed over the whole length of the bone, and is altogether greater than in the female.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Variations.</B>&#151;The sacrum, in some cases, consists of six pieces; occasionally the number is reduced to four. The bodies of the first and second vertebr&#230; may fail to unite. Sometimes the uppermost transverse tubercles are not joined to the rest of the ala on one or both sides, or the sacral canal may be open throughout a considerable part of its length, in consequence of the imperfect development of the lamin&#230; and spinous processes. The sacrum, also, varies considerably with respect to its degree of curvature.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Coccyx (<I>os coccygis</I>).</B>&#151;The coccyx <A HREF="/107/illus100.html">(Fig. 100)</A> is usually formed of four rudimentary vertebr&#230;; the number may however be increased to five or diminished to three. In each of the first three segments may be traced a rudimentary body and articular and transverse processes; the last piece (sometimes the third) is a mere nodule of bone. All the segments are destitute of pedicles, lamin&#230;, and spinous processes. The first is the largest; it resembles the lowest sacral vertebra, and often exists as a separate piece; the last three diminish in size from above downward, and are usually fused with one another.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface</B> is slightly concave, and marked with three transverse grooves which indicate the junctions of the different segments. It gives attachment to the anterior sacrococcygeal ligament and the Levatores ani, and supports part of the rectum. The <B>posterior surface</B> is convex, marked by transverse grooves similar to those on the anterior surface, and presents on either side a linear row of tubercles, the rudimentary articular processes of the coccygeal vertebr&#230;. Of these, the superior pair are large, and are called the <B>coccygeal cornua;</B> they project upward, and articulate with the cornua of the sacrum, and on either side complete the foramen for the transmission of the posterior division of the fifth sacral nerve.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>lateral borders</B> are thin, and exhibit a series of small eminences, which represent the transverse processes of the coccygeal vertebr&#230;. Of these, the first is the largest; it is flattened from before backward, and often ascends to join the lower part of the thin lateral edge of the sacrum, thus completing the foramen for the transmission of the anterior division of the fifth sacral nerve; the others diminish in size from above downward, and are often wanting. The borders of the coccyx are narrow, and give attachment on either side to the sacrotuberous and sacrospinous ligaments, to the Coccygeus in front of the ligaments, and to the Glut&#230;us maximus behind them.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Base.</B>&#151;The base presents an oval surface for articulation with the sacrum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Apex.</B>&#151;The apex is rounded, and has attached to it the tendon of the Sphincter ani externus. It may be bifid, and is sometimes deflected to one or other side.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification of the Vertebral Column.</B>&#151;Each cartilaginous vertebra is ossified from three primary centers <A HREF="/107/illus101.html">(Fig. 101),</A> two for the vertebral arch and one for the body.<A NAME="txt16">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/24.html#note16">16</A> Ossification of the vertebral arches begins in the upper cervical vertebr&#230; about the seventh or eighth week of fetal life, and gradually extends down the column. The ossific granules first appear in the situations where the transverse processes afterward project, and spread backward to the spinous process forward into the pedicles, and lateralward into the transverse and articular processes. Ossification of the bodies begins about the eighth week in the lower thoracic region, and subsequently extends upward and downward along the column. The center for the body does not give rise to the whole of the body of the adult vertebra, the postero-lateral portions of which are ossified by extensions from the vertebral arch centers. The body of the vertebra during the first few years of life shows, therefore, <!-- PAGE NUM="112" -->two synchondroses, <B>neurocentral synchondroses,</B> traversing it along the planes of junction of the three centers <A HREF="/107/illus102.html">(Fig. 102</A>). In the thoracic region, the facets for the heads of the ribs lie behind the neurocentral synchondroses and are ossified from the centers for the vertebral arch. At birth the vertebra consists of three pieces, the body and the halves of the vertebral arch. During the first year the halves of the arch unite behind, union taking place first in the lumbar region and then extending upward through the thoracic and cervical regions. About the third year the bodies of the upper cervical vertebr&#230; are joined to the arches on either side; in the lower lumbar vertebr&#230; the union is not completed until the sixth year. Before puberty, no other changes occur, excepting a gradual increase of these primary centers, the upper and under surfaces of the bodies and the ends of the transverse and spinous processes being cartilaginous. <!-- PAGE NUM="113" -->About the sixteenth year <A HREF="/107/illus102.html">(Fig. 102),</A> five secondary centers appear, one for the tip of each transverse process, one for the extremity of the spinous process, one for the upper and one for the lower surface of the body <A HREF="/107/illus103.html">(Fig. 103</A>). These fuse with the rest of the bone about the age of twenty-five.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="19"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;19</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus101.html"><IMG WIDTH=175 HEIGHT=95 SRC="/107/Images/small/image101.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i101"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>101&#150; </FONT>Ossification of a vertebra (<A HREF="/107/illus101.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus102.html"><IMG WIDTH=164 HEIGHT=131 SRC="/107/Images/small/image102.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i102"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>102&#150; </FONT>No caption. (<A HREF="/107/illus102.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus103.html"><IMG WIDTH=190 HEIGHT=62 SRC="/107/Images/small/image103.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i103"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>103&#150; </FONT>No caption. (<A HREF="/107/illus103.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus104.html"><IMG WIDTH=197 HEIGHT=75 SRC="/107/Images/small/image104.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i104"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>104&#150; </FONT>Atlas. (<A HREF="/107/illus104.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus105.html"><IMG WIDTH=208 HEIGHT=82 SRC="/107/Images/small/image105.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i105"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>105&#150; </FONT>Axis. (<A HREF="/107/illus105.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus106.html"><IMG WIDTH=172 HEIGHT=112 SRC="/107/Images/small/image106.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i106"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>106&#150; </FONT>Lumbar vertebra. (<A HREF="/107/illus106.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus107.html"><IMG WIDTH=138 HEIGHT=118 SRC="/107/Images/small/image107.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i107"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>107&#150; </FONT>No caption. (<A HREF="/107/illus107.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus108.html"><IMG WIDTH=141 HEIGHT=113 SRC="/107/Images/small/image108.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i108"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>108&#150; </FONT>No caption. (<A HREF="/107/illus108.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus109.html"><IMG WIDTH=169 HEIGHT=156 SRC="/107/Images/small/image109.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i109"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>109&#150; </FONT>Ossification of the sacrum. (<A HREF="/107/illus109.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Exceptions to this mode of development occur in the first, second, and seventh cervical vertebr&#230;, and in the lumbar vertebr&#230;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="20"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;20</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Atlas.</B>&#151;The atlas is usually ossified from <I>three</I> centers <A HREF="/107/illus104.html">(Fig. 104</A>). Of these, one appears in each lateral mass about the seventh week of fetal life, and extends backward; at birth, these portions of bone are separated from one another behind by a narrow interval filled with cartilage. Between the third and fourth years they unite either directly or through the medium of a separate center developed in the cartilage. At birth, the anterior arch consists of cartilage; in this a separate center appears about the end of the first year after birth, and joins the lateral masses from the sixth to the eighth year&#151;the lines of union extending across the anterior portions of the superior articular facets. Occasionally there is no separate center, the anterior arch being formed by the forward extension and ultimate junction of the two lateral masses; sometimes this arch is ossified from two centers, one on either side of the middle line.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="21"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;21</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Epistropheus or Axis.</B>&#151;The axis is ossified from <I>five</I> primary and <I>two</I> secondary centers <A HREF="/107/illus105.html">(Fig. 105</A>). The body and vertebral arch are ossified in the same manner as the corresponding parts in the other vertebr&#230;, viz., one center for the body, and two for the vertebral arch. The centers for the arch appear about the seventh or eighth week of fetal life, that for the body about the fourth or fifth month. The dens or odontoid process consists originally of a continuation upward of the cartilaginous mass, in which the lower part of the body is formed. About the sixth month of fetal life, two centers make their appearance in the base of this process: they are placed laterally, and join before birth to form a conical bilobed mass deeply cleft above; the interval between the sides of the cleft and the summit of the process is formed by a wedge-shaped piece of cartilage. The base of the process is separated from the body by a cartilaginous disk, which gradually becomes ossified at its circumference, but remains cartilaginous in its center until advanced age. In this cartilage, rudiments of the lower epiphysial lamella of the atlas and the upper epiphysial lamella of the axis may sometimes be found. The apex of the odontoid process has a separate center which appears in the second and joins about the twelfth year; this is the upper epiphysial lamella of the atlas. In addition to these there is a secondary center for a thin epiphysial plate on the under surface of the body of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="22"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;22</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Seventh Cervical Vertebra.</B>&#151;The anterior or costal part of the transverse process of this vertebra is sometimes ossified from a separate center which appears about the sixth month of fetal life, and joins the body and posterior part of the transverse process between the fifth and sixth years. Occasionally the costal part persists as a separate piece, and, becoming lengthened lateralward and forward, constitutes what is known as a <I>cervical rib.</I> Separate ossific centers have also been found in the costal processes of the fourth, fifth, and sixth cervical vertebr&#230;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="23"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;23</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Lumbar Vertebr&#230;.</B>&#151;The lumbar vertebr&#230; <A HREF="/107/illus106.html">(Fig. 106)</A> have each <I>two</I> additional centers, for the mammillary processes. The transverse process of the first lumbar is sometimes developed as a separate piece, which may remain permanently ununited with the rest of the bone, thus forming a lumbar rib&#151;a peculiarity, however, rarely met with.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="24"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;24</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus110.html"><IMG WIDTH=202 HEIGHT=106 SRC="/107/Images/small/image110.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i110"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>110&#150; </FONT>Base of young sacrum. (<A HREF="/107/illus110.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Sacrum <A HREF="/107/illus107.html">(Figs. 107</A> <A HREF="/107/illus108.html">to</A> <A HREF="/107/illus109.html"></A><A HREF="/107/illus110.html">110)</A>.</B>&#151;The <I>body</I> of each sacral vertebra is ossified from a primary center and <I>two</I> epiphysial plates, one for its upper and another for its under surface, while each vertebral arch is ossified from two centers.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="25"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;25</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The anterior portions of the <I>lateral parts</I> have <I>six</I> additional centers, two for each of the first three vertebr&#230;; these represent the costal elements, and make their appearance above and lateral to the anterior sacral foramina (<A HREF="/107/illus107.html">Figs. 107,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus108.html">108</A>).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="26"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;26</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;On each <I>lateral surface</I> two epiphysial plates are developed (<A HREF="/107/illus109.html">Figs. 109,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus110.html">110</A>): one for the auricular surface, and another for the remaining part of the thin lateral edge of the bone.<A NAME="txt17">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/24.html#note17">17</A></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="27"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;27</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;P<FONT SIZE="-1">ERIODS OF</FONT> O<FONT SIZE="-1">SSIFICATION.</FONT>&#151;About the eighth or ninth week of fetal life, ossification of the central part of the body of the first sacral vertebra commences, and is rapidly followed by deposit of ossific matter in the second and third; ossification does not commence in the bodies of the lower two segments until between the fifth and eighth months of fetal life. Between the sixth <!-- PAGE NUM="114" -->and eighth months ossification of the vertebral arches takes place; and about the same time the costal centers for the lateral parts make their appearance. The junctions of the vertebral arches with the bodies take place in the lower vertebr&#230; as early as the second year, but are not effected in the uppermost until the fifth or sixth year. About the sixteenth year the epiphysial plates for the upper and under surfaces of the bodies are formed; and between the eighteenth and twentieth years, those for the lateral surfaces make their appearance. The bodies of the sacral vertebr&#230; are, during early life, separated from each other by intervertebral fibrocartilages, but about the eighteenth year the two lowest segments become united by bone, and the process of bony union gradually extends upward, with the result that between the twenty-fifth and thirtieth years of life all the segments are united. On examining a sagittal section of the sacrum, the situations of the intervertebral fibrocartilages are indicated by a series of oval cavities <A HREF="/107/illus99.html">(Fig. 99</A>).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="28"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;28</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Coccyx.</B>&#151;The coccyx is ossified from <I>four</I> centers, one for each segment. The ossific nuclei make their appearance in the following order: in the first segment between the first and fourth years; in the second between the fifth and tenth years; in the third between the tenth and fifteenth years; in the fourth between the fourteenth and twentieth years. As age advances, the segments unite with one another, the union between the first and second segments being frequently delayed until after the age of twenty-five or thirty. At a late period of life, especially in females, the coccyx often fuses with the sacrum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="29"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;29</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>3b. The Vertebral Column as a Whole</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The vertebral column is situated in the median line, as the posterior part of the trunk; its average length in the male is about 71 cm. Of this length the cervical part measures 12.5 cm., the thoracic about 28 cm., the lumbar 18 cm., and the sacrum and coccyx 12.5 cm. The female column is about 61 cm. in length.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Curves.</B>&#151;Viewed laterally <A HREF="/107/illus111.html">(Fig. 111),</A> the vertebral column presents several curves, which correspond to the different regions of the column, and are called cervical, thoracic, lumbar, and pelvic. The <B>cervical</B> curve, convex forward, begins at the apex of the odontoid process, and ends at the middle of the second thoracic vertebra; it is the least marked of all the curves. The <B>thoracic</B> curve, concave forward, begins at the middle of the second and ends at the middle of the twelfth thoracic vertebra. Its most prominent point behind corresponds to the spinous process of the seventh thoracic vertebra. The <B>lumbar</B> curve is more marked in the female than in the male; it begins at the middle of the last thoracic vertebra, and ends at the sacrovertebral angle. It is convex anteriorly, the convexity of the lower three vertebr&#230; being much greater than that of the upper two. The <B>pelvic</B> curve begins at the sacrovertebral articulation, and ends at the point of the coccyx; its concavity is directed downward and forward. The thoracic and pelvic curves are termed primary curves, because they alone are present during fetal life. The cervical and lumbar curves are compensatory or secondary, and are developed after birth, the former when the child is able to hold up its head (at three or four months), and to sit upright (at nine months), the latter at twelve or eighteen months, when the child begins to walk.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The vertebral column has also a slight <B>lateral</B> curvature, the convexity of which is directed toward the right side. This may be produced by muscular action, most persons using the right arm in preference to the left, especially in making long-continued efforts, when the body is curved to the right side. In support of this explanation it has been found that in one or two individuals who were left-handed, the convexity was to the left side. By others this curvature is regarded as being produced by the aortic arch and upper part of the descending thoracic aorta&#151;a view which is supported by the fact that in cases where the viscera are transposed and the aorta is on the right side, the convexity of the curve is directed to the left side.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.&#151;Anterior Surface.</B>&#151;When viewed from in front, the width of the bodies of the vertebr&#230; is seen to increase from the second cervical to the first thoracic; there is then a slight diminution in the next three vertebr&#230;; below this there is again a gradual and progressive increase in width as low as the sacrovertebral angle. From this point there is a rapid diminution, to the apex of the coccyx.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="115" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Posterior Surface.</B>&#151;The posterior surface of the vertebral column presents in the median line the spinous processes. In the cervical region (with the exception of the second and seventh vertebr&#230;) these are short and horizontal, with bifid extremities. In the upper part of the thoracic region they are directed obliquely downward; in the middle they are almost vertical, and in the lower part they are nearly horizontal. In the lumbar region they are nearly horizontal. The spinous processes are separated by considerable intervals in the lumbar region, by narrower intervals in the neck, and are closely approximated in the middle of the thoracic region. Occasionally one of these processes deviates a little from the median line&#151;a fact to be remembered in practice, as irregularities of this sort are attendant also on fractures or displacements of the vertebral column. On either side of the spinous processes is the <B>vertebral groove</B> formed by the lamin&#230; in the cervical and lumbar regions, where it is shallow, and by the lamin&#230; and transverse processes in the thoracic region, where it is deep and broad; these grooves lodge the deep muscles of the back. Lateral to the vertebral grooves are the articular processes, and still more laterally the transverse processes. In the thoracic region, the transverse processes stand backward, on a plane considerably behind that of the same processes in the cervical and lumbar regions. In the cervical region, the transverse processes are placed in front of the articular processes, lateral to the pedicles and between the intervertebral foramina. In the thoracic region they are posterior to the pedicles, intervertebral foramina, and articular processes. In the lumbar region they are in front of the articular processes, but behind the intervertebral foramina.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Lateral Surfaces.</B>&#151;The lateral surfaces are separated from the posterior surface by the articular processes in the cervical and lumbar regions, and by the transverse processes in the thoracic region. They present, in front, the sides of the bodies of the vertebr&#230;, marked in the thoracic region by the facets for articulation with the heads of the ribs. More posteriorly are the intervertebral foramina, formed by the juxtaposition of the vertebral notches, oval in <!-- PAGE NUM="116" -->shape, smallest in the cervical and upper part of the thoracic regions, and gradually increasing in size to the last lumbar. They transmit the spinal nerves and are situated between the transverse processes in the cervical region, and in front of them in the thoracic and lumbar regions.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus111.html"><IMG WIDTH=116 HEIGHT=400 SRC="/107/Images/small/image111.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i111"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>111&#150; </FONT>Lateral view of the vertebral column. (<A HREF="/107/illus111.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Vertebral Canal.</B>&#151;The vertebral canal follows the different curves of the column; it is large and triangular in those parts of the column which enjoy the greatest freedom of movement, viz., the cervical and lumbar regions; and is small and rounded in the thoracic region, where motion is more limited.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Abnormalities.</B>&#151;Occasionally the coalescence of the lamin&#230; is not completed, and consequently a cleft is left in the arches of the vertebr&#230;, through which a protrusion of the spinal membranes (dura mater and arachnoid), and generally of the medulla spinalis itself, takes place, constituting the malformation known as <I>spina bifida.</I> This condition is most common in the lumbosacral region, but it may occur in the thoracic or cervical region, or the arches throughout the whole length of the canal may remain incomplete.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus112.html"><IMG WIDTH=218 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image112.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i112"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>112&#150; </FONT>The thorax from in front. (Spalteholz.) (<A HREF="/107/illus112.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>4. The Thorax</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">
