<TR><TD>The skeleton of the <B>thorax</B> or <B>chest</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus112.html">Figs. 112,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus113.html">113,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus114.html">114</A>) is an osseo-cartilaginous cage, containing and protecting the principal organs of respiration and circulation. <!-- PAGE NUM="117" -->It is conical in shape, being narrow above and broad below, flattened from before backward, and longer behind than in front. It is somewhat reniform on transverse section on account of the projection of the vertebral bodies into the cavity.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus113.html"><IMG WIDTH=206 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image113.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i113"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>113&#150; </FONT>The thorax from behind. (Spalteholz.) (<A HREF="/107/illus113.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Boundaries.</B>&#151;The <B>posterior surface</B> is formed by the twelve thoracic vertebr&#230; and the posterior parts of the ribs. It is convex from above downward, and presents on either side of the middle line a deep groove, in consequence of the lateral and backward direction which the ribs take from their vertebral extremities to their angles. The <B>anterior surface,</B> formed by the sternum and costal cartilages, is flattened or slightly convex, and inclined from above downward and forward. The <B>lateral surfaces</B> are convex; they are formed by the ribs, separated from each other by the intercostal spaces, eleven in number, which are occupied by the Intercostal muscles and membranes.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>upper opening</B> of the thorax is reniform in shape, being broader from side to side than from before backward. It is formed by the first thoracic vertebra behind, the upper margin of the sternum in front, and the first rib on either side. <!-- PAGE NUM="118" -->It slopes downward and forward, so that the anterior part of the opening is on a lower level than the posterior. Its antero-posterior diameter is about 5 cm., and its transverse diameter about 10 cm. The <B>lower opening</B> is formed by the twelfth thoracic vertebra behind, by the eleventh and twelfth ribs at the sides, and in front by the cartilages of the tenth, ninth, eighth, and seventh ribs, which ascend on either side and form an angle, the <B>subcostal angle,</B> into the apex of which the xiphoid process projects. The lower opening is wider transversely than from before backward, and slopes obliquely downward and backward, it is closed by the diaphragm which forms the floor of the thorax.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus114.html"><IMG WIDTH=229 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image114.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i114"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>114&#150; </FONT>The thorax from the right. (Spalteholz.) (<A HREF="/107/illus114.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The thorax of the female differs from that of the male as follows: 1. Its capacity is less. 2. The sternum is shorter. 3. The upper margin of the sternum is on a level with the lower part of the body of the third thoracic vertebra, whereas in the male it is on a level with the lower part of the body of the second. 4. The upper ribs are more movable, and so allow a greater enlargement of the upper part of the thorax.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR> <!-- PAGE NUM="119" -->


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>4a. The Sternum</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Breast Bone)</B></DIV></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus115.html"><IMG WIDTH=263 HEIGHT=325 SRC="/107/Images/small/image115.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i115"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>115&#150; </FONT>Anterior surface of sternum and costa cartilages. (<A HREF="/107/illus115.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>sternum</B> <A HREF="/107/illus115.html">(Figs. 115</A> <A HREF="/107/illus116.html"></A><A HREF="/107/illus117.html">to 117)</A> is an elongated, flattened bone, forming the middle portion of the anterior wall of the thorax. Its upper end supports the clavicles, and its margins articulate with the cartilages of the first seven <B>pairs</B> of ribs. It consists of three parts, named from above downward, the <B>manubrium,</B> the <B>body</B> or <B>gladiolus,</B> and the <B>xiphoid process;</B> in early life the body consists of four segments or <I>sternebr&#156;.</I> In its natural position the inclination of the bone is oblique from above, downward and forward. It is slightly convex in front and concave <!-- PAGE NUM="120" -->behind; broad above, becoming narrowed at the point where the manubrium joins the body, after which it again widens a little to below the middle of the body, and then narrows to its lower extremity. Its average length in the adult is about 17 cm., and is rather greater in the male than in the female.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Manubrium (<I>manubrium sterni</I>).</B>&#151;The manubrium is of a somewhat quadrangular form, broad and thick above, narrow below at its junction with the body.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;Its <B>anterior surface,</B> convex from side to side, concave from above downward, is smooth, and affords attachment on either side to the sternal origins of the Pectoralis major and Sternocleidomastoideus. Sometimes the ridges limiting the attachments of these muscles are very distinct. Its <B>posterior surface,</B> concave and smooth, affords attachment on either side to the Sternohyoideus and Sternothyreoideus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus116.html"><IMG WIDTH=165 HEIGHT=289 SRC="/107/Images/small/image116.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i116"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>116&#150; </FONT>Posterior surface of sternum. (<A HREF="/107/illus116.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus117.html"><IMG WIDTH=168 HEIGHT=250 SRC="/107/Images/small/image117.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i117"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>117&#150; </FONT>Lateral border of sternum. (<A HREF="/107/illus117.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>superior border</B> is the thickest and presents at its center the <B>jugular</B> or <B>presternal notch;</B> on either side of the notch is an oval articular surface, directed upward, backward, and lateralward, for articulation with the sternal end of the clavicle. The <B>inferior border,</B> oval and rough, is covered in a fresh state with a thin layer of cartilage, for articulation with the body. The <B>lateral borders</B> are each marked above by a depression for the first costal cartilage, and below by a small facet, which, with a similar facet on the upper angle of the body, forms a notch for the reception of the costal cartilage of the second rib. Between the depression for the first costal cartilage and the demi-facet for the second is a narrow, curved edge, which slopes from above downward and medialward.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Body (<I>corpus sterni; gladiolus</I>).</B>&#151;The body, considerably longer, narrower, and thinner than the manubrium, attains its greatest breadth close to the lower end.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;Its <B>anterior surface</B> is nearly flat, directed upward and forward, and marked by three transverse ridges which cross the bone opposite the third, <!-- PAGE NUM="121" -->fourth, and fifth articular depressions.<A NAME="txt18">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/27.html#note18">18</A> It affords attachment on either side to the sternal origin of the Pectoralis major. At the junction of the third and fourth pieces of the body is occasionally seen an orifice, the <B>sternal foramen,</B> of varying size and form. The <B>posterior surface,</B> slightly concave, is also marked by three transverse lines, less distinct, however, than those in front; from its lower part, on either side, the Transversus thoracis takes origin.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>superior border</B> is oval and articulates with the manubrium, the junction of the two forming the <B>sternal angle</B> (<I>angulus Ludovici</I><A NAME="txt19">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/27.html#note19">19</A>). The <B>inferior border</B> is narrow, and articulates with the xiphoid process. Each <B>lateral border</B> <A HREF="/107/illus117.html">(Fig. 117)</A>, at its superior angle, has a small facet, which with a similar facet on the manubrium, forms a cavity for the cartilage of the second rib; below this are four angular depressions which receive the cartilages of the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth ribs, while the inferior angle has a small facet, which, with a corresponding one on the xiphoid process, forms a notch for the cartilage of the seventh rib. These articular depressions are separated by a series of curved interarticular intervals, which diminish in length from above downward, and correspond to the intercostal spaces. Most of the cartilages belonging to the true ribs, as will be seen from the foregoing description, articulate with the sternum at the lines of junction of its primitive component segments. This is well seen in many of the lower animals, where the parts of the bone remain ununited longer than in man.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Xiphoid Process (<I>processus xiphoideus; ensiform or xiphoid appendix</I>).</B>&#151;The xiphoid process is the smallest of the three pieces: it is thin and elongated, cartilaginous in structure in youth, but more or less ossified at its upper part in the adult.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;Its <B>anterior surface</B> affords attachment on either side to the anterior costoxiphoid ligament and a small part of the Rectus abdominis; its <B>posterior surface,</B> to the posterior costoxiphoid ligament and to some of the fibers of the diaphragm and Transversus thoracis, its <B>lateral borders,</B> to the aponeuroses of the abdominal muscles. Above, it articulates with the lower end of the body, and on the front of each superior angle presents a facet for part of the cartilage of the seventh rib; below, by its pointed extremity, it gives attachment to the linea alba. The xiphoid process varies much in form; it may be broad and thin, pointed, bifid, perforated, curved, or deflected considerably to one or other side.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The sternum is composed of highly vascular cancellous tissue, covered by a thin layer of compact bone which is thickest in the manubrium between the articular facets for the clavicles.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The sternum originally consists of two cartilaginous bars, situated one on either side of the median plane and connected with the cartilages of the upper nine ribs of its own side. These two bars fuse with each other along the middle line to form the cartilaginous sternum which is ossified from <I>six</I> centers: one for the manubrium, four for the body, and one for the xiphoid process <A HREF="/107/illus118.html">(Fig. 118)</A>. The ossific centers appear in the intervals between the articular depressions for the costal cartilages, in the following order: in the manubrium and first piece of the body, during the sixth month; in the second and third pieces of the body, during the seventh month of fetal life; in its fourth piece, during the first year after birth; and in the xiphoid process, between the fifth and eighteenth years. The centers make their appearance at the upper parts of the segments, and proceed gradually downward.<A NAME="txt20">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/27.html#note20">20</A> To these may be added the occasional existence of two small episternal centers, which make their appearance one on either side of the jugular notch; they are probably vestiges of the episternal bone of the monotremata and lizards. Occasionally some of the segments are formed from more than one center, the number and position of which vary <A HREF="/107/illus120.html">(Fig. 120)</A>. Thus, the first piece may have two, three, or even six centers. When two are 
<!-- PAGE NUM="122" --> 
<!-- PAGE NUM="123" -->present, they are generally situated one above the other, the upper being the larger; the second piece has seldom more than one; the third, fourth, and fifth pieces are often formed from two centers placed laterally, the irregular union of which explains the rare occurrence of the sternal foramen <A HREF="/107/illus121.html">(Fig. 121)</A>, or of the vertical fissure which occasionally intersects this part of the bone constituting the malformation known as <I>fissura sterni;</I> these conditions are further explained by the manner in which the cartilaginous sternum is formed. More rarely still the upper end of the sternum may be divided by a fissure. Union of the various centers of the body begins about puberty, and proceeds from below upward <A HREF="/107/illus119.html">(Fig. 119)</A>; by the age of twenty-five they are all united. The xiphoid process may become joined to the body before the age of thirty, but this occurs more frequently after forty; on the other hand, it sometimes remains ununited in old age. In advanced life the manubrium is occasionally joined to the body by bone. When this takes place, however, the bony tissue is generally only superficial, the central portion of the intervening cartilage remaining unossified.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus118.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=131 SRC="/107/Images/small/image118.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i118"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>118&#150; </FONT>Ossification of the sternum. (<A HREF="/107/illus118.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus119.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=153 SRC="/107/Images/small/image119.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i119"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>119&#150; </FONT>No caption. (<A HREF="/107/illus119.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus120.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=126 SRC="/107/Images/small/image120.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i120"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>120&#150; </FONT>Peculiarities. (<A HREF="/107/illus120.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus121.html"><IMG WIDTH=212 HEIGHT=134 SRC="/107/Images/small/image121.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i121"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>121&#150; </FONT>No caption. (<A HREF="/107/illus121.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The sternum articulates on either side with the clavicle and upper seven costal cartilages.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>4b. The Ribs</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Cost&#230;)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>ribs</B> are elastic arches of bone, which form a large part of the thoracic skeleton. They are twelve in number on either side; but this number may be increased by the development of a cervical or lumbar rib, or may be diminished to eleven. The first seven are connected behind with the vertebral column, and in front, through the intervention of the costal cartilages, with the sternum <A HREF="/107/illus115.html">(Fig. 115)</A>; they are called <B>true</B> or <B>vertebro-sternal ribs.</B><A NAME="txt21">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/28.html#note21">21</A> The remaining five are <B>false ribs;</B> of these, the first three have their cartilages attached to the cartilage of the rib above (<B>vertebro-chondral</B>): the last two are free at their anterior extremities and are termed <B>floating</B> or <B>vertebral ribs.</B> The ribs vary in their direction, the upper ones being less oblique than the lower; the obliquity reaches its maximum at the ninth rib, and gradually decreases from that rib to the twelfth. The ribs are situated one below the other in such a manner that spaces called <B>intercostal spaces</B> are left between them. The length of each space corresponds to that of the adjacent ribs and their cartilages; the breadth is greater in front than behind, and between the upper than the lower ribs. The ribs increase in length from the first to the seventh, below which they diminish to the twelfth. In breadth they decrease from above downward; in the upper ten the greatest breadth is at the sternal extremity.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Common Characteristics of the Ribs (<A HREF="/107/illus122.html">Figs. 122,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus123.html">123</A>).</B>&#151;A rib from the middle of the series should be taken in order to study the common characteristics of these bones.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Each rib has two extremities, a <B>posterior</B> or <B>vertebral,</B> and an <B>anterior</B> or <B>sternal,</B> and an intervening portion&#151;the <B>body</B> or <B>shaft.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Posterior Extremity.</B>&#151;The <B>posterior</B> or <B>vertebral extremity</B> presents for examination a head, neck, and tubercle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>head</B> is marked by a kidney-shaped articular surface, divided by a horizontal crest into two facets for articulation with the depression formed on the bodies of two adjacent thoracic vertebr&#230;; the upper facet is the smaller; to the crest is attached the interarticular ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>neck</B> is the flattened portion which extends lateralward from the head; it is about 2.5 cm. long, and is placed in front of the transverse process of the lower of the two vertebr&#230; with which the head articulates. Its <B>anterior surface</B> is flat and smooth, its <B>posterior</B> rough for the attachment of the ligament of the neck, and perforated by numerous foramina. Of its two borders the <B>superior</B> presents a rough crest (<I>crista colli cost&#156;</I>) for the attachment of the anterior costotransverse ligament; its <B>inferior border</B> is rounded. On the posterior surface at the junction of the neck and body, and nearer the lower than the upper border, is an eminence&#151;the <!-- PAGE NUM="124" --><B>tubercle;</B> it consists of an articular and a non-articular portion. The <I>articular portion,</I> the lower and more medial of the two, presents a small, oval surface for articulation with the end of the transverse process of the lower of the two vertebr&#230; to which the head is connected. The <I>non-articular portion</I> is a rough elevation, and affords attachment to the ligament of the tubercle. The tubercle is much more prominent in the upper than in the lower ribs.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus122.html"><IMG WIDTH=232 HEIGHT=400 SRC="/107/Images/small/image122.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i122"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>122&#150; </FONT>A central rib of the left side. Inferior aspect. (<A HREF="/107/illus122.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Body.</B>&#151;The <B>body</B> or <B>shaft</B> is thin and flat, with two surfaces, an external and an internal; and two borders, a superior and an inferior. The <B>external surface</B> is convex, smooth, and marked, a little in front of the tubercle, by a prominent line, directed downward and lateralward; this gives attachment to a tendon of the Iliocostalis, and is called the <B>angle.</B> At this point the rib is bent in two directions, and at the same time twisted on its long axis. If the rib be laid upon its lower border, the portion of the body in front of the angle rests upon this border, while the portion behind the angle is bent medialward and at the same time tilted upward; as the result of the twisting, the external surface, behind the angle, looks downward, and in front of the angle, slightly upward. The distance between the angle and the tubercle is progressively greater from the second to the tenth ribs. The portion between the angle and the tubercle is rounded, rough, and irregular, and serves for the attachment of the Longissimus dorsi. The <B>internal surface</B> is concave, smooth, directed a little upward behind the angle, a little downward in front of it, and is marked by a ridge which commences at the lower extremity of the head; this ridge is strongly marked as far as the angle, and gradually becomes lost at the junction of the anterior and middle thirds of the bone. Between it and the inferior border is a groove, the <B>costal groove,</B> for the intercostal vessels and nerve. At the back part of the bone, this groove belongs to the <!-- PAGE NUM="125" -->inferior border, but just in front of the angle, where it is deepest and broadest, it is on the internal surface. The superior edge of the groove is rounded and serves for the attachment of an Intercostalis internus; the inferior edge corresponds to the lower margin of the rib, and gives attachment to an Intercostalis externus. Within the groove are seen the orifices of numerous small foramina for nutrient vessels which traverse the shaft obliquely from before backward. The <B>superior border,</B> thick and rounded, is marked by an external and an internal lip, more distinct behind than in front, which serve for the attachment of Intercostales externus and internus. The <B>inferior border</B> is thin, and has attached to it an Intercostalis externus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Anterior Extremity.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior</B> or <B>sternal extremity</B> is flattened, and presents a porous, oval, concave depression, into which the costal cartilage is received.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Peculiar Ribs.</B>&#151;The first, second, tenth, eleventh, and twelfth ribs present certain variations from the common characteristics described above, and require special consideration.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus123.html"><IMG WIDTH=275 HEIGHT=148 SRC="/107/Images/small/image123.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i123"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>123&#150; </FONT>A central rib of the left side, viewed from behind. (<A HREF="/107/illus123.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>First Rib.</B>&#151;The first rib <A HREF="/107/illus124.html">(Fig. 124)</A> is the most curved and usually the shortest of all the ribs; it is broad and flat, its surfaces looking upward and downward, and its borders inward and outward. The <B>head</B> is small, rounded, and possesses only a single articular facet, for articulation with the body of the first thoracic vertebra. The <B>neck</B> is narrow and rounded. The <B>tubercle,</B> thick and prominent, is placed on the outer border. There is <I>no angle,</I> but at the tubercle the rib is slightly bent, with the convexity upward, so that the head of the bone is directed downward. The <B>upper surface</B> of the body is marked by two shallow grooves, separated from each other by a slight ridge prolonged internally into a tubercle, the <B>scalene tubercle,</B> for the attachment of the Scalenus anterior; the anterior groove transmits the subclavian vein, the posterior the subclavian artery and the lowest trunk of the brachial plexus.<A NAME="txt22">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/28.html#note22">22</A> Behind the posterior groove is a rough area for the attachment of the Scalenus medius. The <B>under surface</B> is smooth, and destitute of a costal groove. The <B>outer border</B> is convex, thick, and rounded, and at its posterior part gives attachment to the first digitation of the Serratus anterior; the <B>inner border</B> is concave, thin, and sharp, and marked about its center by the scalene tubercle. The <B>anterior extremity</B> is larger and thicker than that of any of the other ribs.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Second Rib.</B>&#151;The second rib <A HREF="/107/illus124.html">(Fig. 125)</A> is much longer than the first, but has a very similar curvature. The non-articular portion of the <B>tubercle</B> is occasionally <!-- PAGE NUM="126" -->only feebly marked. The <B>angle</B> is slight, and situated close to the tubercle. The <B>body</B> is not twisted, so that both ends touch any plane surface upon which it may be laid; but there is a bend, with its convexity upward, similar to, though smaller than that found in the first rib. The body is not flattened horizontally like that of the first rib. Its <B>external surface</B> is convex, and looks upward and a little outward; near the middle of it is a rough eminence for the origin of the lower part of the first and the whole of the second digitation of the Serratus anterior; behind and above this is attached the Scalenus posterior. The <B>internal surface,</B> smooth, and concave, is directed downward and a little inward: on its posterior part there is a short costal groove.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus124.html"><IMG WIDTH=205 HEIGHT=157 SRC="/107/Images/small/image124.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i124"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>124&#150; </FONT>Peculiar ribs. (<A HREF="/107/illus124.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus125.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=178 SRC="/107/Images/small/image125.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i125"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>125&#150; </FONT>Peculiar ribs. (<A HREF="/107/illus125.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus126.html"><IMG WIDTH=293 HEIGHT=79 SRC="/107/Images/small/image126.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i126"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>126&#150; </FONT>Peculiar ribs. (<A HREF="/107/illus126.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus127.html"><IMG WIDTH=297 HEIGHT=74 SRC="/107/Images/small/image127.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i127"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>127&#150; </FONT>Peculiar ribs. (<A HREF="/107/illus127.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus128.html"><IMG WIDTH=278 HEIGHT=71 SRC="/107/Images/small/image128.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i128"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>128&#150; </FONT>Peculiar ribs. (<A HREF="/107/illus128.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Tenth Rib.</B>&#151;The tenth rib <A HREF="/107/illus124.html">(Fig. 126)</A> has only a single articular facet on its head.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Eleventh and Twelfth Ribs.</B>&#151;The eleventh and twelfth ribs (<A HREF="/107/illus124.html">Figs. 127</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus124.html">128</A>) have each a single articular facet on the head, which is of rather large size; they <!-- PAGE NUM="127" -->have <I>no necks or tubercles,</I> and are pointed at their anterior ends. The eleventh has a slight angle and a shallow costal groove. The twelfth has neither; it is much shorter than the eleventh, and its head is inclined slightly downward. Sometimes the twelfth rib is even shorter than the first.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The ribs consist of highly vascular cancellous tissue, enclosed in a thin layer of compact bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;Each rib, with the exception of the last two, is ossified from <I>four</I> centers; a primary center for the body, and three epiphysial centers, one for the head and one each for the articular and non-articular parts of the tubercle. The eleventh and twelfth ribs have each only <I>two</I> centers, those for the tubercles being wanting. Ossification begins near the angle toward the end of the second month of fetal life, and is seen first in the sixth and seventh ribs. The epiphyses for the head and tubercle make their appearance between the sixteenth and twentieth years, and are united to the body about the twenty-fifth year. Fawcett<A NAME="txt23">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/28.html#note23">23</A> states that &#147;in all probability there is usually no epiphysis on the non-articular part of the tuberosity below the sixth or seventh rib.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>4c. The Costal Cartilages</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Cartilagines Costales)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>costal cartilages</B> <A HREF="/107/illus115.html">(Fig. 115)</A> are bars of hyaline cartilage which serve to prolong the ribs forward and contribute very materially to the elasticity of the walls of the thorax. The first seven pairs are connected with the sternum; the next three are each articulated with the lower border of the cartilage of the preceding rib; the last two have pointed extremities, which end in the wall of the abdomen. Like the ribs, the costal cartilages vary in their length, breadth, and direction. They increase in length from the first to the seventh, then gradually decrease to the twelfth. Their breadth, as well as that of the intervals between them, diminishes from the first to the last. They are broad at their attachments to the ribs, and taper toward their sternal extremities, excepting the first two, which are of the same breadth throughout, and the sixth, seventh, and eighth, which are enlarged where their margins are in contact. They also vary in direction: the first descends a little, the second is horizontal, the third ascends slightly, while the others are angular, following the course of the ribs for a short distance, and then ascending to the sternum or preceding cartilage. Each costal cartilage presents two surfaces, two borders, and two extremities.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface</B> is convex, and looks forward and upward: that of the first gives attachment to the costoclavicular ligament and the Subclavius muscle; those of the first six or seven at their sternal ends, to the Pectoralis major. The others are covered by, and give partial attachment to, some of the flat muscles of the abdomen. The <B>posterior surface</B> is concave, and directed backward and downward; that of the first gives attachment to the Sternothyroideus, those of the third to the sixth inclusive to the Transversus thoracis, and the six or seven inferior ones to the Transversus abdominis and the diaphragm.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;Of the two borders the <B>superior</B> is concave, the <B>inferior</B> convex; they afford attachment to the Intercostales interni: the upper border of the sixth gives attachment also to the Pectoralis major. The inferior borders of the sixth, seventh, eighth, and ninth cartilages present heel-like projections at the points of greatest convexity. These projections carry smooth oblong facets which articulate respectively with facets on slight projections from the upper borders of the seventh, eighth, ninth, and tenth cartilages.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Extremities.</B>&#151;The <B>lateral end</B> of each cartilage is continuous with the osseous tissue of the rib to which it belongs. The <B>medial end</B> of the first is continuous with the sternum; the medial ends of the six succeeding ones are rounded and are received into shallow concavities on the lateral margins of the sternum. The medial ends of the eighth, ninth, and tenth costal cartilages are pointed, and are connected each with the cartilage immediately above. Those of the eleventh and twelfth are pointed and free. In old age the costal cartilages are prone to undergo superficial ossification.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="128" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Cervical ribs</I> derived from the seventh cervical vertebra (page 83) are of not infrequent occurrence, and are important clinically because they may give rise to obscure nervous or vascular symptoms. The cervical rib may be a mere epiphysis articulating only with the transverse process of the vertebra, but more commonly it consists of a defined head, neck, and tubercle, with or without a body. It extends lateralward, or forward and lateralward, into the posterior triangle of the neck, where it may terminate in a free end or may join the first thoracic rib, the first costal cartilage, or the sternum.<A NAME="txt24">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/29.html#note24">24</A> It varies much in shape, size, direction, and mobility. If it reach far enough forward, part of the brachial plexus and the subclavian artery and vein cross over it, and are apt to suffer compression in so doing. Pressure on the artery may obstruct the circulation so much that arterial thrombosis results, causing gangrene of the finger tips. Pressure on the nerves is commoner, and affects the eighth cervical and first thoracic nerves, causing paralysis of the muscles they supply, and neuralgic pains and paresthesia in the area of skin to which they are distributed: no oculopupillary changes are to be found.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>thorax</I> is frequently found to be altered in shape in certain diseases.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In <I>rickets,</I> the ends of the ribs, where they join the costal cartilages, become enlarged, giving rise to the so-called &#147;rickety rosary,&#148; which in mild cases is only found on the internal surface of the thorax. Lateral to these enlargements the softened ribs sink in, so as to present a groove passing downward and lateralward on either side of the sternum. This bone is forced forward by the bending of the ribs, and the antero-posterior diameter of the chest is increased. The ribs affected are the second to the eighth, the lower ones being prevented from falling in by the presence of the liver, stomach, and spleen; and when the abdomen is distended, as it often is in rickets, the lower ribs may be pushed outward, causing a transverse groove (Harrison&#146;s sulcus) just above the costal arch. This deformity or forward projection of the sternum, often asymmetrical, is known as <I>pigeon breast,</I> and may be taken as evidence of active or old rickets except in cases of primary spinal curvature. In many instances it is associated in children with obstruction in the upper air passages, due to enlarged tonsils or adenoid growths. In some rickety children or adults, and also in others who give no history or further evidence of having had rickets, an opposite condition obtains. The lower part of the sternum and often the xiphoid process as well are deeply depressed backward, producing an oval hollow in the lower sternal and upper epigastric regions. This is known as <I>funnel breast</I> (German, <I>Trichterbrust</I>); it never appears to produce the least disturbance of any of the vital functions. The <I>phthisical chest</I> is often long and narrow, and with great obliquity of the ribs and projection of the scapul&#230;. In <I>pulmonary emphysema</I> the chest is enlarged in all its diameters, and presents on section an almost circular outline. It has received the name of the <I>barrel-shaped chest.</I> In severe cases of <I>lateral curvature of the vertebral column</I> the thorax becomes much distorted. In consequence of the rotation of the bodies of the vertebr&#230; which takes place in this disease, the ribs opposite the convexity of the dorsal curve become extremely convex behind, being thrown out and bulging, and at the same time flattened in front, so that the two ends of the same rib are almost parallel. Coincidently with this the ribs on the opposite side, on the concavity of the curve, are sunk and depressed behind, and bulging and convex in front.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5. The Skull</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The <B>skull</B> is supported on the summit of the vertebral column, and is of an oval shape, wider behind than in front. It is composed of a series of flattened or irregular bones which, with one exception (the mandible), are immovably jointed together. It is divisible into two parts: (1) the <B>cranium,</B> which lodges and protects the brain, consists of eight bones, and (2) the <B>skeleton of the face,</B> of fourteen, as follows:</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD><TABLE BORDER="0" ALIGN="CENTER">
<TR><TD ROWSPAN=15 VALIGN=MIDDLE><I>Skull,</I> 22 bones</TD><TD ROWSPAN=7 VALIGN=MIDDLE><I>Cranium,</I> 8 bones&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD>
<TD>Occipital.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Two Parietals.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Frontal.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Two Temporals.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Sphenoidal.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Ethmoidal.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD><BR></TD></TR>
<TR><TD ROWSPAN=8 VALING=MIDDLE><I>Face,</I> 14 bones</TD><TD>Two Nasals.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Two Maxill&#230;.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Two Lacrimals.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Two Zygomatics.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Two Palatines.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Two Inferior Nasal Conch&#230;.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Vomer.</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>Mandible.</TD></TR>
</TABLE></TD></TR><!-- PAGE NUM="129" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In the Basle nomenclature, certain bones developed in association with the nasal capsule, viz., the inferior nasal conch&#230;, the lacrimals, the nasals, and the vomer, are grouped as cranial and not as facial bones.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The hyoid bone, situated at the root of the tongue and attached to the base of the skull by ligaments, is described in this section.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5a. The Cranial Bones. 1. The Occipital Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Ossa Cranii) &amp; (Os Occipitale).</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>occipital bone</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus129.html">Figs. 129,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus130.html">130</A>), situated at the back and lower part of the cranium, is trapezoid in shape and curved on itself. It is pierced by a large oval aperture, the <B>foramen magnum,</B> through which the cranial cavity communicates with the vertebral canal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus129.html"><IMG WIDTH=275 HEIGHT=264 SRC="/107/Images/small/image129.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i129"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>129&#150; </FONT>Occipital bone. Outer surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus129.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The curved, expanded plate behind the foramen magnum is named the <B>squama;</B> the thick, somewhat quadrilateral piece in front of the foramen is called the <B>basilar part,</B> whilst on either side of the foramen is the <B>lateral portion.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Squama (<I>squama occipitalis</I>).</B>&#151;The squama, situated above and behind the foramen magnum, is curved from above downward and from side to side.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>external surface</B> is convex and presents midway between the summit of the bone and the foramen magnum a prominence, the <B>external occipital protuberance.</B> Extending lateralward from this on either side are two curved lines, one a little above the other. The upper, often faintly marked, is named the <B>highest nuchal line,</B> and to it the galea aponeurotica is attached. The lower <!-- PAGE NUM="130" -->is termed the <B>superior nuchal line.</B> That part of the squama which lies above the highest nuchal lines is named the <B>planum occipitale,</B> and is covered by the Occipitalis muscle; that below, termed the <B>planum nuchale,</B> is rough and irregular for the attachment of several muscles. From the external occipital protuberance a ridge or crest, the <B>median nuchal line,</B> often faintly marked, descends to the foramen magnum, and affords attachment to the ligamentum nuch&#230;; running from the middle of this line across either half of the nuchal plane is the <B>inferior nuchal line.</B> Several muscles are attached to the outer surface of the squama, thus: the superior nuchal line gives origin to the Occipitalis and Trapezius, and insertion to the Sternocleidomastoideus and Splenius capitis: into the surface between the superior and inferior nuchal lines the Semispinalis capitis and the Obliquus capitis superior are inserted, while the inferior nuchal line and the area below it receive the insertions of the Recti capitis posteriores major and minor. The posterior atlanto&#246;ccipital membrane is attached around the postero-lateral part of the foramen magnum, just outside the margin of the foramen.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus130.html"><IMG WIDTH=267 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image130.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i130"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>130&#150; </FONT>Occipita bone. Inner surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus130.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>internal surface</B> is deeply concave and divided into four foss&#230; by a <B>cruciate eminence.</B> The upper two foss&#230; are triangular and lodge the occipital lobes of the cerebrum; the lower two are quadrilateral and accommodate the hemispheres of the cerebellum. At the point of intersection of the four divisions of the cruciate eminence is the <B>internal occipital protuberance.</B> From this protuberance the upper division of the cruciate eminence runs to the superior angle of the bone, and on <!-- PAGE NUM="131" -->one side of it (generally the right) is a deep groove, the <B>sagittal sulcus,</B> which lodges the hinder part of the superior sagittal sinus; to the margins of this sulcus the falx cerebri is attached. The lower division of the cruciate eminence is prominent, and is named the <B>internal occipital crest;</B> it bifurcates near the foramen magnum and gives attachment to the falx cerebelli; in the attached margin of this falx is the occipital sinus, which is sometimes duplicated. In the upper part of the internal occipital crest, a small depression is sometimes distinguishable; it is termed the <B>vermian fossa</B> since it is occupied by part of the vermis of the cerebellum. Transverse grooves, one on either side, extend from the internal occipital protuberance to the lateral angles of the bone; those grooves accommodate the transverse sinuses, and their prominent margins give attachment to the tentorium cerebelli. The groove on the right side is usually larger than that on the left, and is continuous with that for the superior sagittal sinus. Exceptions to this condition are, however, not infrequent; the left may be larger than the right or the two may be almost equal in size. The angle of union of the superior sagittal and transverse sinuses is named the <B>confluence of the sinuses</B> (<I>torcular Herophili</I><A NAME="txt25">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/31.html#note25">25</A>), and its position is indicated by a depression situated on one or other side of the protuberance.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Lateral Parts (<I>pars lateralis</I>).</B>&#151;The lateral parts are situated at the sides of the foramen magnum; on their under surfaces are the <B>condyles</B> for articulation with the superior facets of the atlas. The condyles are oval or reniform in shape, and their anterior extremities, directed forward and medialward, are closer together than their posterior, and encroach on the basilar portion of the bone; the posterior extremities extend back to the level of the middle of the foramen magnum. The articular surfaces of the condyles are convex from before backward and from side to side, and look downward and lateralward. To their margins are attached the capsules of the atlanto&#246;ccipital articulations, and on the medial side of each is a rough impression or tubercle for the alar ligament. At the base of either condyle the bone is tunnelled by a short canal, the <B>hypoglossal canal</B> (<I>anterior condyloid foramen</I>). This begins on the cranial surface of the bone immediately above the foramen magnum, and is directed lateralward and forward above the condyle. It may be partially or completely divided into two by a spicule of bone; it gives exit to the hypoglossal or twelfth cerebral nerve, and entrance to a meningeal branch of the ascending pharyngeal artery. Behind either condyle is a depression, the <B>condyloid fossa,</B> which receives the posterior margin of the superior facet of the atlas when the head is bent backward; the floor of this fossa is sometimes perforated by the <B>condyloid canal,</B> through which an emissary vein passes from the transverse sinus. Extending lateralward from the posterior half of the condyle is a quadrilateral plate of bone, the <B>jugular process,</B> excavated in front by the <B>jugular notch,</B> which, in the articulated skull, forms the posterior part of the jugular foramen. The jugular notch may be divided into two by a bony spicule, the <B>intrajugular process,</B> which projects lateralward above the hypoglossal canal. The under surface of the jugular process is rough, and gives attachment to the Rectus capitis lateralis muscle and the lateral atlanto&#246;ccipital ligament; from this surface an eminence, the <B>paramastoid process,</B> sometimes projects downward, and may be of sufficient length to reach, and articulate with, the transverse process of the atlas. Laterally the jugular process presents a rough quadrilateral or triangular area which is joined to the jugular surface of the temporal bone by a plate of cartilage; after the age of twenty-five this plate tends to ossify.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>upper surface</B> of the lateral part presents an oval eminence, the <B>jugular tubercle,</B> which overlies the hypoglossal canal and is sometimes crossed by an oblique groove for the glossopharyngeal, vagus, and accessory nerves. On the <!-- PAGE NUM="132" -->upper surface of the jugular process is a deep groove which curves medialward and forward and is continuous with the jugular notch. This groove lodges the terminal part of the transverse sinus, and opening into it, close to its medial margin, is the orifice of the condyloid canal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Basilar Part (<I>pars basilaris</I>).</B>&#151;The basilar part extends forward and upward from the foramen magnum, and presents <I>in front</I> an area more or less quadrilateral in outline. In the young skull this area is rough and uneven, and is joined to the body of the sphenoid by a plate of cartilage. By the twenty-fifth year this cartilaginous plate is ossified, and the occipital and sphenoid form a continuous bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;On its <B>lower surface,</B> about 1 cm. in front of the foramen magnum, is the <B>pharyngeal tubercle</B> which gives attachment to the fibrous raph&#233; of the pharynx. On either side of the middle line the Longus capitis and Rectus capitis anterior are inserted, and immediately in front of the foramen magnum the anterior atlanto&#246;ccipital membrane is attached.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>upper surface</B> presents a broad, shallow groove which inclines upward and forward from the foramen magnum; it supports the medulla oblongata, and near the margin of the foramen magnum gives attachment to the membrana tectoria. On the lateral margins of this surface are faint grooves for the inferior petrosal sinuses.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Foramen Magnum.</B>&#151;The foramen magnum is a large oval aperture with its long diameter antero-posterior; it is wider behind than in front where it is encroached upon by the condyles. It transmits the medulla oblongata and its membranes, the accessory nerves, the vertebral arteries, the anterior and posterior spinal arteries, and the membrana tectoria and alar ligaments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Angles.</B>&#151;The <B>superior angle</B> of the occipital bone articulates with the occipital angles of the parietal bones and, in the fetal skull, corresponds in position with the <B>posterior fontanelle.</B> The <B>inferior angle</B> is fused with the body of the sphenoid. The <B>lateral angles</B> are situated at the extremities of the grooves for the transverse sinuses: each is received into the interval between the mastoid angle of the parietal and the mastoid part of the temporal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>superior borders</B> extend from the superior to the lateral angles: they are deeply serrated for articulation with the occipital borders of the parietals, and form by this union the <B>lambdoidal suture.</B> The <B>inferior borders</B> extend from the lateral angles to the inferior angle; the upper half of each articulates with the mastoid portion of the corresponding temporal, the lower half with the petrous part of the same bone. These two portions of the inferior border are separated from one another by the jugular process, the notch on the anterior surface of which forms the posterior part of the jugular foramen.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus131.html"><IMG WIDTH=151 HEIGHT=187 SRC="/107/Images/small/image131.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i131"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>131&#150; </FONT>Occipital bone at birth. (<A HREF="/107/illus131.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The occipital, like the other cranial the <I>outer</I> and <I>inner tables,</I> between which is the cancellous tissue or diplo&#235;; the bone is especially thick at the ridges, protuberances, condyles, and anterior part of the basilar part; in the inferior foss&#230; it is thin, semitransparent, and destitute of diplo&#235;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification <A HREF="/107/illus131.html">(Fig. 131</A>).</B>&#151;The planum occipitale of the squama is developed in membrane, and may remain separate throughout life when it constitutes the <I>interparietal</I> bone; the rest of <!-- PAGE NUM="133" -->the bone is developed in cartilage. The number of nuclei for the planum occipitale is usually given as four, two appearing near the middle line about the second month, and two some little distance from the middle line about the third month of fetal life. The planum nuchale of the squama is ossified from two centers, which appear about the seventh week of fetal life and soon unite to form a single piece. Union of the upper and lower portions of the squama takes place in the third month of fetal life. An occasional center (Kerckring) appears in the posterior margin of the foramen magnum during the fifth month; this forms a separate ossicle (sometimes double) which unites with the rest of the squama before birth. Each of the lateral parts begins to ossify from a single center during the eighth week of fetal life. The basilar portion is ossified from two centers, one in front of the other; these appear about the sixth week of fetal life and rapidly coalesce. Mall<A NAME="txt26">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/31.html#note26">26</A> states that the planum occipitale is ossified from two centers and the basilar portion from one. About the fourth year the squama and the two lateral portions unite, and about the sixth year the bone consists of a single piece. Between the eighteenth and twenty-fifth years the occipital and sphenoid become united, forming a single bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The occipital articulates with <I>six</I> bones: the two parietals, the two temporals, the sphenoid, and the atlas.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5a. 2. The Parietal Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Parietale)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>parietal bones</B> form, by their union, the sides and roof of the cranium. Each bone is irregularly quadrilateral in form, and has two surfaces, four borders, and four angles.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus132.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=257 SRC="/107/Images/small/image132.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i132"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>132&#150; </FONT>Left parietal bone. Outer surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus132.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>external surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus132.html">(Fig. 132)</A> is convex, smooth, and marked near the center by an eminence, the <B>parietal eminence</B> (<I>tuber parietale</I>), which indicates the point where ossification commenced. Crossing the middle of the bone in an <!-- PAGE NUM="134" -->arched direction are two curved lines, the <B>superior</B> and <B>inferior temporal lines;</B> the former gives attachment to the temporal fascia, and the latter indicates the upper limit of the muscular origin of the Temporalis. Above these lines the bone is covered by the galea aponeurotica; below them it forms part of the temporal fossa, and affords attachment to the Temporalis muscle. At the back part and close to the upper or sagittal border is the <B>parietal foramen,</B> which transmits a vein to the superior sagittal sinus, and sometimes a small branch of the occipital artery; it is not constantly present, and its size varies considerably.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>internal surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus133.html">(Fig. 133)</A> is concave; it presents depressions corresponding to the cerebral convolutions, and numerous furrows for the ramifications of the middle meningeal vessel;<A NAME="txt27">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/32.html#note27">27</A> the latter run upward and backward from the sphenoidal angle, and from the central and posterior part of the squamous border. Along the upper margin is a shallow groove, which, together with that on the opposite parietal, forms a channel, the <B>sagittal sulcus,</B> for the superior sagittal sinus; the edges of the sulcus afford attachment to the falx cerebri. Near the groove are several depressions, best marked in the skulls of old persons, for the <B>arachnoid granulations</B> (<I>Pacchionian bodies</I>). In the groove is the internal opening of the parietal foramen when that aperture exists.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus133.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=238 SRC="/107/Images/small/image133.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i133"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>133&#150; </FONT>Left parietal bone. Inner surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus133.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>sagittal border,</B> the longest and thickest, is dentated and articulates with its fellow of the opposite side, forming the sagittal suture. The <B>squamous border</B> is divided into three parts: of these, the anterior is thin and pointed, bevelled at the expense of the outer surface, and overlapped by the tip of the great wing of the sphenoid; the middle portion is arched, bevelled at the expense of the outer surface, and overlapped by the squama of the temporal; the posterior part is thick and serrated for articulation with the mastoid portion of the temporal. The <!-- PAGE NUM="135" --><B>frontal border</B> is deeply serrated, and bevelled at the expense of the outer surface above and of the inner below; it articulates with the frontal bone, forming onehalf of the <B>coronal suture.</B> The <B>occipital border,</B> deeply denticulated, articulates with the occipital, forming one-half of the <B>lambdoidal suture.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Angles.</B>&#151;The <B>frontal angle</B> is practically a right angle, and corresponds with the point of meeting of the sagittal and coronal sutures; this point is named the <B>bregma;</B> in the fetal skull and for about a year and a half after birth this region is membranous, and is called the <B>anterior fontanelle.</B> The <B>sphenoidal angle,</B> thin and acute, is received into the interval between the frontal bone and the great wing of the sphenoid. Its inner surface is marked by a deep groove, sometimes a canal, for the anterior divisions of the middle meningeal artery. The <B>occipital angle</B> is rounded and corresponds with the point of meeting of the sagittal and lambdoidal sutures&#151;a point which is termed the <B>lambda;</B> in the fetus this part of the skull is membranous, and is called the <B>posterior fontanelle.</B> The <B>mastoid angle</B> is truncated; it articulates with the occipital bone and with the mastoid portion of the temporal, and presents on its inner surface a broad, shallow groove which lodges part of the transverse sinus. The point of meeting of this angle with the occipital and the mastoid part of the temporal is named the <B>asterion.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The parietal bone is ossified in membrane from a single center, which appears at the parietal eminence about the eighth week of fetal life. Ossification gradually extends in a radial manner from the center toward the margins of the bone; the angles are consequently the parts last formed, and it is here that the fontanelles exist. Occasionally the parietal bone is divided into two parts, upper and lower, by an antero-posterior suture.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The parietal articulates with <I>five</I> bones: the opposite parietal, the occipital, frontal, temporal, and sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5a. 3. The Frontal Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Frontale)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>frontal bone</B> resembles a cockle-shell in form, and consists of two portions&#151;a <B>vertical</B> portion, the <B>squama,</B> corresponding with the region of the forehead; and an <B>orbital</B> or <B>horizontal</B> portion, which enters into the formation of the roofs of the orbital and nasal cavities.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Squama (<I>squama frontalis</I>).&#151;Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>external surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus134.html">(Fig. 134)</A> of this portion is convex and usually exhibits, in the lower part of the middle line, the remains of the <B>frontal</B> or <B>metopic suture;</B> in infancy this suture divides the bone into two, a condition which may persist throughout life. On either side of this suture, about 3 cm. above the supraorbital margin, is a rounded elevation, the <B>frontal eminence</B> (<I>tuber frontale</I>). These eminences vary in size in different individuals, are occasionally unsymmetrical, and are especially prominent in young skulls; the surface of the bone above them is smooth, and covered by the galea aponeurotica. Below the frontal eminences, and separated from them by a shallow groove, are two arched elevations, the <B>superciliary arches;</B> these are prominent medially, and are joined to one another by a smooth elevation named the <B>glabella.</B> They are larger in the male than in the female, and their degree of prominence depends to some extent on the size of the frontal air sinuses;<A NAME="txt28">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/33.html#note28">28</A> prominent ridges are, however, occasionally associated with small air sinuses. Beneath each superciliary arch is a curved and prominent margin, the <B>supraorbital margin,</B> which forms the upper boundary of the base of the orbit, and separates the squama from the orbital portion of the bone. The lateral part of this margin is sharp and prominent, affording to the eye, in that situation, considerable protection from injury; the medial part is rounded. At the junction of its medial and intermediate thirds is <!-- PAGE NUM="136" -->a notch, sometimes converted into a foramen, the <B>supraorbital notch</B> or <B>foramen,</B> which transmits the supraorbital vessels and nerve. A small aperture in the upper part of the notch transmits a vein from the diplo&#235; to join the supraorbital vein. The supraorbital margin ends laterally in the <B>zygomatic process,</B> which is strong and prominent, and articulates with the zygomatic bone. Running upward and backward from this process is a well-marked line, the <B>temporal line,</B> which divides into the <B>upper</B> and <B>lower temporal lines,</B> continuous, in the articulated skull, with the corresponding lines on the parietal bone. The area below and behind the temporal line forms the anterior part of the temporal fossa, and gives origin to the Temporalis muscle. Between the supraorbital margins the squama projects downward to a level below that of the zygomatic processes; this portion is known as the <B>nasal part</B> and presents a rough, uneven interval, the <B>nasal notch,</B> which articulates on either side of the middle line with the nasal bone, and laterally with the frontal process of the maxilla and with the lacrimal. The term <B>nasion</B> is applied to the middle of the frontonasal suture. From the center of the notch the <B>nasal process</B> projects downward and forward beneath the nasal bones and frontal processes of the maxill&#230;, and supports the bridge of the nose. The nasal process ends below in a sharp <B>spine,</B> and on either side of this is a small grooved surface which enters into the formation of the roof of the corresponding nasal cavity. The spine forms part of the septum of the nose, articulating in front with the crest of the nasal bones and behind with the perpendicular plate of the ethmoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus134.html"><IMG WIDTH=259 HEIGHT=250 SRC="/107/Images/small/image134.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i134"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>134&#150; </FONT>Frontal bone. Outer surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus134.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>internal surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus135.html">(Fig. 135)</A> of the squama is concave and presents in the upper part of the middle line a vertical groove, the <B>sagittal sulcus,</B> the edges of which unite below to form a ridge, the <B>frontal crest;</B> the sulcus lodges the superior sagittal sinus, while its margins and the crest afford attachment to the falx cerebri. <!-- PAGE NUM="137" -->The crest ends below in a small notch which is converted into a foramen, the <B>foramen cecum,</B> by articulation with the ethmoid. This foramen varies in size in different subjects, and is frequently impervious; when open, it transmits a vein from the nose to the superior sagittal sinus. On either side of the middle line the bone presents depressions for the convolutions of the brain, and numerous small furrows for the anterior branches of the middle meningeal vessels. Several small, irregular foss&#230; may also be seen on either side of the sagittal sulcus, for the reception of the arachnoid granulations.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Orbital or Horizontal Part (<I>pars orbitalis</I>).</B>&#151;This portion consists of two thin triangular plates, the <B>orbital plates,</B> which form the vaults of the orbits, and are separated from one another by a median gap, the <B>ethmoidal notch.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus135.html"><IMG WIDTH=275 HEIGHT=259 SRC="/107/Images/small/image135.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i135"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>135&#150; </FONT>Frontal bone. Inner surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus135.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>inferior surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus135.html">(Fig. 135)</A> of each orbital plate is smooth and concave, and presents, laterally, under cover of the zygomatic process, a shallow depression, the <B>lacrimal fossa,</B> for the lacrimal gland; near the nasal part is a depression, the <B>fovea trochlearis,</B> or occasionally a small <B>trochlear spine,</B> for the attachment of the cartilaginous pulley of the Obliquus oculi superior. The <B>superior surface</B> is convex, and marked by depressions for the convolutions of the frontal lobes of the brain, and faint grooves for the meningeal branches of the ethmoidal vessels.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>ethmoidal notch</B> separates the two orbital plates; it is quadrilateral, and filled, in the articulated skull, by the cribriform plate of the ethmoid. The margins of the notch present several half-cells which, when united with corresponding half-cells on the upper surface of the ethmoid, complete the ethmoidal air cells. Two grooves cross these edges transversely; they are converted into the <B>anterior</B> <!-- PAGE NUM="138" -->and <B>posterior ethmoidal canals</B> by the ethmoid, and open on the medial wall of the orbit. The anterior canal transmits the nasociliary nerve and anterior ethmoidal vessels, the posterior, the posterior ethmoidal nerve and vessels. In front of the ethmoidal notch, on either side of the frontal spine, are the openings of the <B>frontal air sinuses.</B> These are two irregular cavities, which extend backward, upward, and lateralward for a variable distance between the two tables of the skull; they are separated from one another by a thin bony septum, which often deviates to one or other side, with the result that the sinuses are rarely symmetrical.
