Absent at birth, they are usually fairly well-developed between the seventh and eighth years, but only reach their full size after puberty. They vary in size in different persons, and are larger in men than in women.<A NAME="txt29">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/33.html#note29">29</A> They are lined by mucous membrane, and each communicates with the corresponding nasal cavity by means of a passage called the <B>frontonasal duct.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>border of the squama</B> is thick, strongly serrated, bevelled at the expense of the inner table above, where it rests upon the parietal bones, and at the expense of the outer table on either side, where it receives the lateral pressure of those bones; this border is continued below into a triangular, rough surface, which articulates with the great wing of the sphenoid. The <B>posterior borders of the orbital plates</B> are thin and serrated, and articulate with the small wings of the sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The squama and the zygomatic processes are very thick, consisting of diploic tissue contained between two compact lamin&#230;; the diploic tissue is absent in the regions occupied by the frontal air sinuses. The orbital portion is thin, translucent, and composed entirely of compact bone; hence the facility with which instruments can penetrate the cranium through this part of the orbit; when the frontal sinuses are exceptionally large they may extend backward for a considerable distance into the orbital portion, which in such cases also consists of only two tables.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification <A HREF="/107/illus136.html">(Fig. 136</A>).</B>&#151;The frontal bone is ossified in membrane from <I>two primary</I> centers, one for each half, which appear toward the end of the second month of fetal life, one above each supraorbital margin. From each of these centers ossification extends upward to form the corresponding half of the squama, and backward to form the orbital plate. The spine is ossified from a pair of <I>secondary</I> centers, on either side of the middle line; similar centers appear in the nasal part and zygomatic processes. At birth the bone consists of two pieces, separated by the frontal suture, which is usually obliterated, except at its lower part, by the eighth year, but occasionally persists throughout life. It is generally maintained that the development of the frontal sinuses begins at the end of the first or beginning of the second year, but Onodi&#146;s researches indicate that development begins at birth. The sinuses are of considerable size by the seventh or eighth year, but do not attain their full proportions until after puberty.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The frontal articulates with <I>twelve</I> bones: the sphenoid, the ethmoid, the two parietals, the two nasals, the two maxill&#230;, the two lacrimals, and the two zygomatics.</</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus136.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=171 SRC="/107/Images/small/image136.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i136"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>136&#150; </FONT>Frontal bone at birth. (<A HREF="/107/illus136.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5a. 4. The Temporal Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Temporale)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>temporal bones</B> are situated at the sides and base of the skull. Each consists of five parts, viz., the <B>squama,</B> the <B>petrous, mastoid,</B> and <B>tympanic parts,</B> and the <B>styloid process.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="139" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Squama (<I>squama temporalis</I>).</B>&#151;The squama forms the anterior and upper part of the bone, and is scale-like, thin, and translucent.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus137.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=224 SRC="/107/Images/small/image137.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i137"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>137&#150; </FONT>Left temporal bone. Outer surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus137.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;Its <B>outer surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus137.html">(Fig. 137)</A> is smooth and convex; it affords attachment to the Temporalis muscle, and forms part of the temporal fossa; on its hinder part is a vertical groove for the middle temporal artery. A curved line, the <B>temporal line,</B> or <B>supramastoid crest,</B> runs backward and upward across its posterior part; it serves for the attachment of the temporal fascia, and limits the origin of the Temporalis muscle. The boundary between the squama and the mastoid portion of the bone, as indicated by traces of the original suture, lies about 1 cm. below this line. Projecting from the lower part of the squama is a long, arched process, the <B>zygomatic process.</B> This process is at first directed lateralward, its two surfaces looking upward and downward; it then appears as if twisted inward upon itself, and runs forward, its surfaces now looking medialward and lateralward. The superior border is long, thin, and sharp, and serves for the attachment of the temporal fascia; the inferior, short, thick, and arched, has attached to it some fibers of the Masseter. The lateral surface is convex and subcutaneous; the medial is concave, and affords attachment to the Masseter. The anterior end is deeply serrated and articulates with the zygomatic bone. The posterior end is connected to the squama by two roots, the <B>anterior</B> and <B>posterior roots.</B> The posterior root, a prolongation of the upper border, is strongly marked; it runs backward above the external acoustic meatus, and is continuous with the temporal line. The anterior root, continuous with the lower border, is short but broad and strong; it is directed medialward and ends in a rounded eminence, the <B>articular tubercle</B> (<I>eminentia articularis</I>). This tubercle forms the front boundary of the mandibular fossa, <!-- PAGE NUM="140" -->and in the fresh state is covered with cartilage. In front of the articular tubercle is a small triangular area which assists in forming the infratemporal fossa; this area is separated from the outer surface of the squama by a ridge which is continuous behind with the anterior root of the zygomatic process, and in front, in the articulated skull, with the infratemporal crest on the great wing of the sphenoid. Between the posterior wall of the external acoustic meatus and the posterior root of the zygomatic process is the area called the <B>suprameatal triangle</B> (Macewen), or <B>mastoid fossa,</B> through which an instrument may be pushed into the tympanic antrum. At the junction of the anterior root with the zygomatic process is a projection for the attachment of the temporomandibular ligament; and behind the anterior root is an oval depression, forming part of the mandibular fossa, for the reception of the condyle of the mandible. The <B>mandibular fossa</B> (<I>glenoid fossa</I>) is bounded, in front, by the articular tubercle; behind, by the tympanic part of the bone, which separates it from the external acoustic meatus; it is divided into two parts by a narrow slit, the <B>petrotympanic fissure</B> (<I>Glaserian fissure</I>). The anterior part, formed by the squama, is smooth, covered in the fresh state with cartilage, and articulates with the condyle of the mandible. Behind this part of the fossa is a small conical eminence; this is the representative of a prominent tubercle which, in some mammals, descends behind the condyle of the mandible, and prevents its backward displacement. The posterior part of the mandibular fossa, formed by the tympanic part of the bone, is non-articular, and sometimes lodges a portion of the parotid gland. The petrotympanic fissure leads into the middle ear or tympanic cavity; it lodges the anterior process of the malleus, and transmits the tympanic branch of the internal maxillary artery. The chorda <!-- PAGE NUM="141" -->tympani nerve passes through a canal (<I>canal of Huguier</I>), separated from the anterior edge of the petrotympanic fissure by a thin scale of bone and situated on the lateral side of the auditory tube, in the retiring angle between the squama and the petrous portion of the temporal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus138.html"><IMG WIDTH=286 HEIGHT=275 SRC="/107/Images/small/image138.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i138"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>138&#150; </FONT>Left temporal bone. Inner surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus138.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>internal surface</B> of the squama <A HREF="/107/illus138.html">(Fig. 138)</A> is concave; it presents depressions corresponding to the convolutions of the temporal lobe of the brain, and grooves for the branches of the middle meningeal vessels.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>superior border</B> is thin, and bevelled at the expense of the internal table, so as to overlap the squamous border of the parietal bone, forming with it the squamosal suture. Posteriorly, the superior border forms an angle, the <B>parietal notch,</B> with the mastoid portion of the bone. The <B>antero-inferior border</B> is thick, serrated, and bevelled at the expense of the inner table above and of the outer below, for articulation with the great wing of the sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Mastoid Portion (<I>pars mastoidea</I>).</B>&#151;The mastoid portion forms the posterior part of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus139.html"><IMG WIDTH=275 HEIGHT=251 SRC="/107/Images/small/image139.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i139"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>139&#150; </FONT>Coronal section of right temporal bone. (<A HREF="/107/illus139.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;Its <B>outer surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus137.html">(Fig. 137)</A> is rough, and gives attachment to the Occipitalis and Auricularis posterior. It is perforated by numerous foramina; one of these, of large size, situated near the posterior border, is termed the <B>mastoid foramen;</B> it transmits a vein to the transverse sinus and a small branch of the occipital artery to the dura mater. The position and size of this foramen are very variable; it is not always present; sometimes it is situated in the occipital bone, or in the suture between the temporal and the occipital. The mastoid portion is continued below into a conical projection, the <B>mastoid process,</B> the size and form of which very somewhat; it is larger in the male than in the female. This process serves for the attachment of the Sternocleidomastoideus, Splenius capitis, and Longissimus capitis. On the medial side of the process is a deep groove, the <B>mastoid notch</B> (<I>digastric fossa</I>), for the attachment of the Digastricus; medial to this is a shallow furrow, the <B>occipital groove,</B> which lodges the occipital artery.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="142" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>inner surface</B> of the mastoid portion presents a deep, curved groove, the <B>sigmoid sulcus,</B> which lodges part of the transverse sinus; in it may be seen the opening of the mastoid foramen. The groove for the transverse sinus is separated from the innermost of the mastoid air cells by a very thin lamina of bone, and even this may be partly deficient.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>superior border</B> of the mastoid portion is broad and serrated, for articulation with the mastoid angle of the parietal. The <B>posterior border,</B> also serrated, articulates with the inferior border of the occipital between the lateral angle and jugular process. Anteriorly the mastoid portion is fused with the descending process of the squama above; below it enters into the formation of the external acoustic meatus and the tympanic cavity.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;A section of the mastoid process <A HREF="/107/illus139.html">(Fig. 139)</A> shows it to be hollowed out into a number of spaces, the <B>mastoid cells,</B> which exhibit the greatest possible variety as to their size and number. At the upper and front part of the process they are large and irregular and contain air, but toward the lower part they diminish in size, while those at the apex of the process are frequently quite small and contain marrow; occasionally they are entirely absent, and the mastoid is then solid throughout. In addition to these a large irregular cavity is situated at the upper and front part of the bone. It is called the <B>tympanic antrum,</B> and must be distinguished from the mastoid cells, though it communicates with them. Like the mastoid cells it is filled with air and lined by a prolongation of the mucous membrane of the tympanic cavity, with which it communicates. The tympanic antrum is bounded above by a thin plate of bone, the <B>tegmen tympani,</B> which separates it from the middle fossa of the base of the skull; below by the mastoid process; laterally by the squama just below the temporal line, and medially by the lateral semicircular canal of the internal ear which projects into its cavity. It opens in front into that portion of the tympanic cavity which is known as the <B>attic</B> or <B>epitympanic recess.</B> The tympanic antrum is a cavity of some considerable size at the time of birth; the mastoid air cells may be regarded as diverticula from the antrum, and begin to appear at or before birth; by the fifth year they are well-marked, but their development is not completed until toward puberty.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Petrous Portion (<I>pars petrosa</I> [<I>pyramis</I>]).</B>&#151;The petrous portion or <B>pyramid</B> is pyramidal and is wedged in at the base of the skull between the sphenoid and occipital. Directed medialward, forward, and a little upward, it presents for examination a base, an apex, three surfaces, and three angles, and contains, in its interior, the essential parts of the organ of hearing.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Base.</B>&#151;The base is fused with the internal surfaces of the squama and mastoid portion.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Apex.</B>&#151;The apex, rough and uneven, is received into the angular interval between the posterior border of the great wing of the sphenoid and the basilar part of the occipital; it presents the anterior or internal orifice of the carotid canal, and forms the postero-lateral boundary of the foramen lacerum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface</B> forms the posterior part of the middle fossa of the base of the skull, and is continuous with the inner surface of the squamous portion, to which it is united by the <B>petrosquamous suture,</B> remains of which are distinct even at a late period of life. It is marked by depressions for the convolutions of the brain, and presents six points for examination: (1) near the center, an <B>eminence</B> (<I>eminentia arcuata</I>) which indicates the situation of the superior semicircular canal; (2) in front of and a little lateral to this eminence, a depression indicating the position of the tympanic cavity: here the layer of bone which separates the tympanic from the cranial cavity is extremely thin, and is known as the <B>tegmen tympani;</B> (3) a shallow groove, sometimes double, leading lateralward and backward to an oblique opening, the <B>hiatus of the facial canal,</B> for the passage of the greater superficial petrosal nerve and the petrosal branch of the middle meningeal <!-- PAGE NUM="143" -->artery; (4) lateral to the hiatus, a smaller opening, occasionally seen, for the passage of the lesser superficial petrosal nerve; (5) near the apex of the bone, the termination of the carotid canal, the wall of which in this situation is deficient in front; (6) above this canal the shallow <B>trigeminal impression</B> for the reception of the semilunar ganglion.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>posterior surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus138.html">(Fig. 138)</A> forms the front part of the posterior fossa of the base of the skull, and is continuous with the inner surface of the mastoid portion. Near the center is a large orifice, the <B>internal acoustic meatus,</B> the size of which varies considerably; its margins are smooth and rounded, and it leads into a short canal, about 1 cm. in length, which runs lateralward. It transmits the facial and acoustic nerves and the internal auditory branch of the basilar artery. The lateral end of the canal is closed by a vertical plate, which is divided by a horizontal crest, the <B>crista falciformis,</B> into two unequal portions <A HREF="/107/illus140.html">(Fig. 140</A>). Each portion is further subdivided by a vertical ridge into an anterior and a posterior part. In the portion beneath the crista falciformis are three sets of foramina; one group, just below the posterior part of the crest, situated in the <B>area cribrosa media,</B> consists of several small openings for the nerves to the saccule; below and behind this area is the <B>foramen singulare,</B> or opening for the nerve to the posterior semicircular duct; in front of and below the first is the <B>tractus spiralis foraminosus,</B> consisting of a number of small spirally arranged openings, which encircle the <B>canalis centralis cochle&#230;;</B> these openings together with this central canal transmit the nerves to the cochlea. The portion above the crista falciformis presents behind, the <B>area cribrosa superior,</B> pierced by a series of small openings, for the passage of the nerves to the utricle and the superior and lateral semicircular ducts, and, in front, the <B>area facians,</B> with one large opening, the commencement of the canal for the facial nerve (<B>aqu&#230;ductus Fallopii</B>). Behind the internal acoustic meatus is a small slit almost hidden by a thin plate of bone, leading to a canal, the <B>aqu&#230;ductus vestibuli,</B> which transmits the ductus endolymphaticus together with a small artery and vein. Above and between these two openings is an irregular depression which lodges a process of the dura mater and transmits a small vein; in the infant this depression is represented by a large fossa, the <B>subarcuate fossa,</B> which extends backward as a blind tunnel under the superior semicircular canal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus140.html"><IMG WIDTH=159 HEIGHT=152 SRC="/107/Images/small/image140.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i140"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>140&#150; </FONT>Diagrammatic view of the fundus of the right internal acoustic meatus. (Testut.) 1. Crista falciformis. 2. Area facialis, with (2&#146;) internal opening of the facial canal. 3. Ridge separating the area facialis from the area cribrosa superior. 4. Area cribrosa superior, with (4&#146;) openings for nerve filaments. 5. Anterior inferior cribriform area, with (5&#146;) the tractus spiralis foraminosus, and (5&#146;&#146;) the canalis centralis of the cochlea. 6. Ridge separating the tractus spiralis foraminosus from the area cribrosa media. 7. Area cribrosa media, with (7&#146;) orifices for nerves to saccule. 8. Foramen singulare. (<A HREF="/107/illus140.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>inferior surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus141.html">(Fig. 141)</A> is rough and irregular, and forms part of the exterior of the base of the skull. It presents eleven points for examination: (1) near the apex is a rough surface, quadrilateral in form, which serves partly for the attachment of the Levator veli palatini and the cartilaginous portion of the auditory tube, and partly for connection with the basilar part of the occipital bone through the intervention of some dense fibrous tissue; (2) behind this is the large circular aperture of the <B>carotid canal,</B> which ascends at first vertically, and then, making a bend, runs horizontally forward and medialward; it transmits into the cranium the internal carotid artery, and the carotid plexus of nerves; (3) medial to the opening for the carotid canal and close to its posterior border, in front of the jugular fossa, is a triangular depression; at the apex of this is a small opening, the <!-- PAGE NUM="144" --><B>aqu&#230;ductus cochle&#230;,</B> which lodges a tubular prolongation of the dura mater establishing a communication between the perilymphatic space and the subarachnoid space, and transmits a vein from the cochlea to join the internal jugular; (4) behind these openings is a deep depression, the <B>jugular fossa,</B> of variable depth and size in different skulls; it lodges the bulb of the internal jugular vein; (5) in the bony ridge dividing the carotid canal from the jugular fossa is the small <B>inferior tympanic canaliculus</B> for the passage of the tympanic branch of the glossopharyngeal nerve; (6) in the lateral part of the jugular fossa is the <B>mastoid canaliculus</B> for the entrance of the auricular branch of the vagus nerve; (7) behind the jugular fossa is a quadrilateral area, the <B>jugular surface,</B> covered with cartilage in the fresh state, and articulating with the jugular process of the occipital bone; (8) extending backward from the carotid canal is the <B>vaginal process,</B> a sheath-like plate of bone, which divides behind into two lamin&#230;; the lateral lamina is continuous with the tympanic part of the bone, the medial with the lateral margin of the jugular surface; (9) between these lamin&#230; is the <B>styloid process,</B> a sharp spine, about 2.5 cm. in length; (10) between the styloid and mastoid processes is the <B>stylomastoid foramen;</B> it is the termination of the facial canal, and transmits the facial nerve and stylomastoid artery; (11) situated between the tympanic portion and the mastoid process is the tympanomastoid fissure, for the exit of the auricular branch of the vagus nerve.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus141.html"><IMG WIDTH=275 HEIGHT=247 SRC="/107/Images/small/image141.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i141"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>141&#150; </FONT>Left temporal bone. Inferior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus141.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Angles.</B>&#151;The <B>superior angle,</B> the longest, is grooved for the superior petrosal sinus, and gives attachment to the tentorium cerebelli; at its medial extremity is a notch, in which the trigeminal nerve lies. The <B>posterior angle</B> is intermediate in length between the superior and the anterior. Its medial half is marked by a sulcus, which forms, with a corresponding sulcus on the occipital bone, the channel for the inferior petrosal sinus. Its lateral half presents an excavation&#151;the <B>jugular fossa</B>&#151;which, with the jugular notch on the occipital, forms the <!-- PAGE NUM="145" --><B>jugular foramen;</B> an eminence occasionally projects from the center of the fossa, and divides the foramen into two. The <B>anterior angle</B> is divided into two parts&#151;a lateral joined to the squama by a suture (<I>petrosquamous</I>), the remains of which are more or less distinct; a medial, free, which articulates with the spinous process of the sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;At the angle of junction of the petrous part and the squama are two canals, one above the other, and separated by a thin plate of bone, the <B>septum canalis musculotubarii</B> (<I>processus cochleariformis</I>); both canals lead into the tympanic cavity. The upper one (<I>semicanalis m. tensoris tympani</I>) transmits the Tensor tympani, the lower one (<I>semicanalis tub&#230; auditiv&#230;</I>) forms the bony part of the auditory tube.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The tympanic cavity, auditory ossicles, and internal ear, are described with the organ of hearing.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="19"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;19</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Tympanic Part (<I>pars tympanica</I>).</B>&#151;The tympanic part is a curved plate of bone lying below the squama and in front of the mastoid process.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="20"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;20</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;Its <B>postero-superior surface</B> is concave, and forms the anterior wall, the floor, and part of the posterior wall of the bony external acoustic meatus. Medially, it presents a narrow furrow, the <B>tympanic sulcus,</B> for the attachment of the tympanic membrane. Its <B>antero-inferior surface</B> is quadrilateral and slightly concave; it constitutes the posterior boundary of the mandibular fossa, and is in contact with the retromandibular part of the parotid gland.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="21"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;21</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;Its <B>lateral border</B> is free and rough, and gives attachment to the cartilaginous part of the external acoustic meatus. Internally, the tympanic part is fused with the petrous portion, and appears in the retreating angle between it and the squama, where it lies below and lateral to the orifice of the auditory tube. Posteriorly, it blends with the squama and mastoid part, and forms the anterior boundary of the tympanomastoid fissure. Its <B>upper border</B> fuses laterally with the back of the postglenoid process, while medially it bounds the petrotympanic fissure. The medial part of the <B>lower border</B> is thin and sharp; its lateral part splits to enclose the root of the styloid process, and is therefore named the <B>vaginal process.</B> The central portion of the tympanic part is thin, and in a considerable percentage of skulls is perforated by a hole, the <B>foramen of Huschke.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="22"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;22</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>external acoustic meatus</B> is nearly 2 cm. long and is directed inward and slightly forward: at the same time it forms a slight curve, so that the floor of the canal is convex upward. In sagittal section it presents an oval or elliptical shape with the long axis directed downward and slightly backward. Its anterior wall and floor and the lower part of its posterior wall are formed by the tympanic part; the roof and upper part of the posterior wall by the squama. Its inner end is closed, in the recent state, by the tympanic membrane; the upper limit of its outer orifice is formed by the posterior root of the zygomatic process, immediately below which there is sometimes seen a small spine, the <B>suprameatal spine,</B> situated at the upper and posterior part of the orifice.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="23"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;23</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Styloid Proc&#233;ss (<I>processus styloideus</I>).</B>&#151;The styloid process is slender, pointed, and of varying length; it projects downward and forward, from the under surface of the temporal bone. Its proximal part (<I>tympanohyal</I>) is ensheathed by the vaginal process of the tympanic portion, while its distal part (<I>stylohyal</I>) gives attachment to the stylohyoid and stylomandibular ligaments, and to the Styloglossus, Stylohyoideus, and Stylopharyngeus muscles. The stylohyoid ligament extends from the apex of the process to the lesser cornu of the hyoid bone, and in some instances is partially, in others completely, ossified.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="24"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;24</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The structure of the squama is like that of the other cranial bones: the mastoid portion is spongy, and the petrous portion dense and hard.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="25"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;25</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus142.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=163 SRC="/107/Images/small/image142.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i142"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>142&#150; </FONT>The three principal parts of the tempora bone at birth. 1. Outer surface of petromastoid part. 2. Outer surface of tympanic ring. 3. Inner surface of squama. (<A HREF="/107/illus142.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus143.html"><IMG WIDTH=215 HEIGHT=178 SRC="/107/Images/small/image143.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i143"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>143&#150; </FONT>Temporal bone at birth. Outer aspect. (<A HREF="/107/illus143.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus144.html"><IMG WIDTH=164 HEIGHT=182 SRC="/107/Images/small/image144.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i144"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>144&#150; </FONT>Temporal bone at birth. Inner aspect. (<A HREF="/107/illus144.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The temporal bone is ossified from <I>eight</I> centers, exclusive of those for the internal ear and the tympanic ossicles, viz., one for the squama including the zygomatic process, one for <!-- PAGE NUM="146" -->the tympanic part, four for the petrous and mastoid parts, and two for the styloid process. Just before the close of fetal life <A HREF="/107/illus142.html">(Fig. 142)</A> the temporal bone consists of three principal parts: 1. The <I>squama</I> is ossified in membrane from a single nucleus, which appears near the root of the zygomatic process about the second month. 2. The <I>petromastoid</I> part is developed from four centers, which make their appearance in the cartilaginous ear capsule about the fifth or sixth month. One (<I>pro&#246;tic</I>) appears in the neighborhood of the eminentia arcuata, spreads in front and above the internal acoustic meatus and extends to the apex of the bone; it forms part of the cochlea, vestibule, superior semicircular canal, and medial wall of the tympanic cavity. A second (<I>opisthotic</I>) appears at the promontory on the medial wall of the tympanic cavity and surrounds the fenestra cochle&#230;; it forms the floor of the tympanic cavity and vestibule, surrounds the carotid canal, invests the lateral and lower part of the cochlea, and spreads medially below the internal acoustic meatus. A third (<I>pterotic</I>) roofs in the tympanic cavity and antrum; while the fourth (<I>epiotic</I>) appears near the posterior semicircular canal and extends to form the mastoid process (Vrolik). 3. The <I>tympanic ring</I> is an incomplete circle, in the concavity of which is a groove, the tympanic sulcus, for the attachment of the circumference of the tympanic membrane. This ring expands to form the tympanic part, and is ossified in membrane from a single center which appears about the third month. The <I>styloid process</I> is developed from the proximal part of the cartilage of the second branchial or hyoid arch by two centers: one for the proximal part, the <I>tympanohyal,</I> appears before birth; the other, comprising the rest of the process, is named the <!-- PAGE NUM="147" --><I>stylohyal,</I> and does not appear until after birth. The tympanic ring unites with the squama shortly before birth; the petromastoid part and squama join during the first year, and the tympanohyal portion of the styloid process about the same time (<A HREF="/107/illus143.html">Figs. 143,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus144.html">144</A>). The stylohyal does not unite with the rest of the bone until after puberty, and in some skulls never at all.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="26"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;26</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The chief subsequent changes in the temporal bone apart from increase in size are: (1) The tympanic ring extends outward and backward to form the tympanic part. This extension does not, however, take place at an equal rate all around the circumference of the ring, but occurs most rapidly on its anterior and posterior portions, and these outgrowths meet and blend, and thus, for a time, there exists in the floor of the meatus a foramen, the <I>foramen of Huschke;</I> this foramen is usually closed about the fifth year, but may persist throughout life. (2) The mandibular fossa is at first extremely shallow, and looks lateralward as well as downward; it becomes deeper and is ultimately directed downward. Its change in direction is accounted for as follows. The part of the squama which forms the fossa lies at first below the level of the zygomatic process. As, however, the base of the skull increases in width, this lower part of the squama is directed horizontally inward to contribute to the middle fossa of the skull, and its surfaces therefore come to look upward and downward; the attached portion of the zygomatic process also becomes everted, and projects like a shelf at right angles to the squama. (3) The mastoid portion is at first quite flat, and the stylomastoid foramen and rudimentary styloid process lie immediately behind the tympanic ring. With the development of the air cells the outer part of the mastoid portion grows downward and forward to form the mastoid process, and the styloid process and stylomastoid foramen now come to lie on the under surface. The descent of the foramen is necessarily accompanied by a corresponding lengthening of the facial canal. (4) The downward and forward growth of the mastoid process also pushes forward the tympanic part, so that the portion of it which formed the original floor of the meatus and contained the foramen of Huschke is ultimately found in the anterior wall. (5) The fossa subarcuata becomes filled up and almost obliterated.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="27"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;27</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The temporal articulates with <I>five</I> bones: occipital, parietal, sphenoid, mandible and zygomatic.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="28"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;28</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5a. 5. The Sphenoid Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Sphenoidale)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>sphenoid bone</B> is situated at the base of the skull in front of the temporals and basilar part of the occipital. It somewhat resembles a bat with its wings extended, and is divided into a median portion or body, two great and two small wings extending outward from the sides of the body, and two pterygoid processes which project from it below.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Body (<I>corpus sphenoidale</I>).</B>&#151;The body, more or less cubical in shape, is hollowed out in its interior to form two large cavities, the <B>sphenoidal air sinuses,</B> which are separated from each other by a septum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>superior surface</B> of the body <A HREF="/107/illus145.html">(Fig. 145)</A> presents in front a prominent spine, the <B>ethmoidal spine,</B> for articulation with the cribriform plate of the ethmoid; behind this is a smooth surface slightly raised in the middle line, and grooved on either side for the olfactory lobes of the brain. This surface is bounded behind by a ridge, which forms the anterior border of a narrow, transverse groove, the <B>chiasmatic groove</B> (<I>optic groove</I>), above and behind which lies the optic chiasma; the groove ends on either side in the <B>optic foramen,</B> which transmits the optic nerve and ophthalmic artery into the orbital cavity. Behind the chiasmatic groove is an elevation, the <B>tuberculum sell&#230;;</B> and still more posteriorly, a deep depression, the <B>sella turcica,</B> the deepest part of which lodges the hypophysis cerebri and is known as the <B>fossa hypophyseos.</B> The anterior boundary of the sella turcica is completed by two small eminences, one on either side, called the <B>middle clinoid processes,</B> while the posterior boundary is formed by a square-shaped plate of bone, the <B>dorsum sell&#230;,</B> ending at its superior angles in two tubercles, the <B>posterior clinoid processes,</B> the size and form of which vary considerably in different individuals. The posterior clinoid processes deepen the sella turcica, and give attachment to the tentorium cerebelli. On either side of the dorsum sell&#230; is a notch for the passage of the abducent nerve, and below the notch a sharp process, the <B>petrosal process,</B> which articulates with the apex of the petrous portion of the temporal bone, and forms the medial boundary of the foramen lacerum. <!-- PAGE NUM="148" -->Behind the dorsum sell&#230; is a shallow depression, the <B>clivus,</B> which slopes obliquely backward, and is continuous with the groove on the basilar portion of the occipital bone; it supports the upper part of the pons.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus145.html"><IMG WIDTH=362 HEIGHT=223 SRC="/107/Images/small/image145.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i145"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>145&#150; </FONT>Sphenoid bone. Upper surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus145.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus146.html"><IMG WIDTH=326 HEIGHT=222 SRC="/107/Images/small/image146.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i146"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>146&#150; </FONT>Sphenoid bone. Anterior and inferior surfaces. (<A HREF="/107/illus146.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral surfaces</B> of the body are united with the great wings and the medial pterygoid plates. Above the attachment of each great wing is a broad groove, curved something like the italic letter <I>f;</I> it lodges the internal carotid artery and the cavernous sinus, and is named the <B>carotid groove.</B> Along the posterior part of the lateral margin of this groove, in the angle between the body and great wing, is a ridge of bone, called the <B>lingula.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>posterior surface,</B> quadrilateral in form <A HREF="/107/illus147.html">(Fig. 147),</A> is joined, during infancy and adolescence, to the basilar part of the occipital bone by a plate of cartilage. <!-- PAGE NUM="149" -->Between the eighteenth and twenty-fifth years this becomes ossified, ossification commencing above and extending downward.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>anterior surface</B> of the body <A HREF="/107/illus146.html">(Fig. 146)</A> presents, in the middle line, a vertical crest, the <B>sphenoidal crest,</B> which articulates with the perpendicular plate of the ethmoid, and forms part of the septum of the nose. On either side of the crest is an irregular opening leading into the corresponding <B>sphenoidal air sinus.</B> These sinuses are two large, irregular cavities hollowed out of the interior of the body of the bone, and separated from one another by a bony septum, which is commonly bent to one or the other side. They vary considerably in form and size,<A NAME="txt30">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/35.html#note30">30</A> are seldom symmetrical, and are often partially subdivided by irregular bony lamin&#230;. Occasionally, they extend into the basilar part of the occipital nearly as far as the foramen magnum. They begin to be developed before birth, and are of a considerable size by the age of six. They are partially closed, in front and below, by two thin, curved plates of bone, the <B>sphenoidal conch&#230;</B> (see page 152), leaving in the articulated skull a round opening at the upper part of each sinus by which it communicates with the upper and back part of the nasal cavity and occasionally with the posterior ethmoidal air cells. The lateral margin of the anterior surface is serrated, and articulates with the lamina papyracea of the ethmoid, completing the posterior ethmoidal cells; the lower margin articulates with the orbital process of the palatine bone, and the upper with the orbital plate of the frontal bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus147.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=202 SRC="/107/Images/small/image147.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i147"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>147&#150; </FONT>Sphenoid bone. Upper and posterior surfaces. (<A HREF="/107/illus147.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>inferior surface</B> presents, in the middle line, a triangular spine, the <B>sphenoidal rostrum,</B> which is continuous with the sphenoidal crest on the anterior surface, and is received in a deep fissure between the al&#230; of the vomer. On either side of the rostrum is a projecting lamina, the <B>vaginal process,</B> directed medialward from the base of the medial pterygoid plate, with which it will be described.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Great Wings (<I>al&#230; magn&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The great wings, or <B>ali-sphenoids,</B> are two strong processes of bone, which arise from the sides of the body, and are curved upward, lateralward, and backward; the posterior part of each projects as a triangular process which fits into the angle between the squama and the petrous <!-- PAGE NUM="150" -->portion of the temporal and presents at its apex a downwardly directed process, the <B>spina angularis</B> (<I>sphenoidal spine</I>).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>superior</B> or <B>cerebral surface</B> of each great wing <A HREF="/107/illus145.html">(Fig. 145)</A> forms part of the middle fossa of the skull; it is deeply concave, and presents depressions for the convolutions of the temporal lobe of the brain. At its anterior and medial part is a circular aperture, the <B>foramen rotundum,</B> for the transmission of the maxillary nerve. Behind and lateral to this is the <B>foramen ovale,</B> for the transmission of the mandibular nerve, the accessory meningeal artery, and sometimes the lesser superficial petrosal nerve.<A NAME="txt31">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/35.html#note31">31</A> Medial to the foramen ovale, a small aperture, the <B>foramen Vesalii,</B> may occasionally be seen opposite the root of the pterygoid process; it opens below near the scaphoid fossa, and transmits a small vein from the cavernous sinus. Lastly, in the posterior angle, near to and in front of the spine, is a short canal, sometimes double, the <B>foramen spinosum,</B> which transmits the middle meningeal vessels and a recurrent branch from the mandibular nerve.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus146.html">(Fig. 146)</A> is convex, and divided by a transverse ridge, the <B>infratemporal crest,</B> into two portions. The superior or temporal portion, convex from above downward, concave from before backward, forms a part of the temporal fossa, and gives attachment to the Temporalis; the inferior or infratemporal, smaller in size and concave, enters into the formation of the infratemporal fossa, and, together with the infratemporal crest, affords attachment to the Pterygoideus externus. It is pierced by the foramen ovale and foramen spinosum, and at its posterior part is the spina angularis, which is frequently grooved on its medial surface for the chorda tympani nerve. To the spina angularis are attached the sphenomandibular ligament and the Tensor veli palatini. Medial to the anterior extremity of the infratemporal crest is a triangular process which serves to increase the attachment of the Pterygoideus externus; extending downward and medialward from this process on to the front part of the lateral pterygoid plate is a ridge which forms the anterior limit of the infratemporal surface, and, in the articulated skull, the posterior boundary of the pterygomaxillary fissure.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>orbital surface</B> of the great wing <A HREF="/107/illus146.html">(Fig. 146),</A> smooth, and quadrilateral in shape, is directed forward and medialward and forms the posterior part of the lateral wall of the orbit. Its upper serrated edge articulates with the orbital plate of the frontal. Its inferior rounded border forms the postero-lateral boundary of the inferior orbital fissure. Its medial sharp margin forms the lower boundary of the superior orbital fissure and has projecting from about its center a little tubercle which gives attachment to the inferior head of the Rectus lateralis oculi; at the upper part of this margin is a notch for the transmission of a recurrent branch of the lacrimal artery. Its lateral margin is serrated and articulates with the zygomatic bone. Below the medial end of the superior orbital fissure is a grooved surface, which forms the posterior wall of the pterygopalatine fossa, and is pierced by the foramen rotundum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Margin <A HREF="/107/illus145.html">(Fig. 145</A>).</B>&#151;Commencing from behind, that portion of the circumference of the great wing which extends from the body to the spine is irregular. Its medial half forms the anterior boundary of the foramen lacerum, and presents the posterior aperture of the pterygoid canal for the passage of the corresponding nerve and artery. Its lateral half articulates, by means of a synchondrosis, with the petrous portion of the temporal, and between the two bones on the under surface of the skull, is a furrow, the <B>sulcus tub&#230;,</B> for the lodgement of the cartilaginous part of the auditory tube. In front of the spine the circumference presents a concave, serrated edge, bevelled at the expense of the inner table below, and of the outer table above, for articulation with the temporal squama. At the tip of the great wing is a triangular portion, bevelled at the expense of the <!-- PAGE NUM="151" -->internal surface, for articulation with the sphenoidal angle of the parietal bone; this region is named the <B>pterion.</B> Medial to this is a triangular, serrated surface, for articulation with the frontal bone; this surface is continuous medially with the sharp edge, which forms the lower boundary of the superior orbital fissure, and laterally with the serrated margin for articulation with the zygomatic bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Small Wings (<I>al&#230; parv&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The small wings or <B>orbito-sphenoids</B> are two thin triangular plates, which arise from the upper and anterior parts of the body, and, projecting lateralward, end in sharp points <A HREF="/107/illus145.html">(Fig. 145</A>).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>superior surface</B> of each is flat, and supports part of the frontal lobe of the brain. The <B>inferior surface</B> forms the back part of the roof of the orbit, and the upper boundary of the <B>superior orbital fissure.</B> This fissure is of a triangular form, and leads from the cavity of the cranium into that of the orbit: it is bounded <I>medially</I> by the body; <I>above,</I> by the small wing; <I>below,</I> by the medial margin of the orbital surface of the great wing; and is completed <I>laterally</I> by the frontal bone. It transmits the oculomotor, trochlear, and abducent nerves, the three branches of the ophthalmic division of the trigeminal nerve, some filaments from the cavernous plexus of the sympathetic, the orbital branch of the middle meningeal artery, a recurrent branch from the lacrimal artery to the dura mater, and the ophthalmic vein.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior border</B> is serrated for articulation with the frontal bone. The <B>posterior border,</B> smooth and rounded, is received into the lateral fissure of the brain; the medial end of this border forms the <B>anterior clinoid process,</B> which gives attachment to the tentorium cerebelli; it is sometimes joined to the middle clinoid process by a spicule of bone, and when this occurs the termination of the groove for the internal carotid artery is converted into a foramen (<I>carotico-clinoid</I>). The small wing is connected to the body by two roots, the upper thin and flat, the lower thick and triangular; between the two roots is the <B>optic foramen,</B> for the transmission of the optic nerve and ophthalmic artery.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Pterygoid Processes (<I>processus pterygoidei</I>).</B>&#151;The pterygoid processes, one on either side, descend perpendicularly from the regions where the body and great wings unite. Each process consists of a medial and a lateral plate, the upper parts of which are fused anteriorly; a vertical sulcus, the <B>pterygopalatine groove,</B> descends on the front of the line of fusion. The plates are separated below by an angular cleft, the <B>pterygoid fissure,</B> the margins of which are rough for articulation with the pyramidal process of the palatine bone. The two plates diverge behind and enclose between them a V-shaped fossa, the <B>pterygoid fossa,</B> which contains the Pterygoideus internus and Tensor veli palatini. Above this fossa is a small, oval, shallow depression, the <B>scaphoid fossa,</B> which gives origin to the Tensor veli palatini. The anterior surface of the pterygoid process is broad and triangular near its root, where it forms the posterior wall of the pterygopalatine fossa and presents the anterior orifice of the pterygoid canal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Lateral Pterygoid Plate.</B>&#151;The <B>lateral pterygoid plate</B> is broad, thin, and everted; its <B>lateral surface</B> forms part of the medial wall of the infratemporal fossa, and gives attachment to the Pterygoideus externus; its <B>medial surface</B> forms part of the pterygoid fossa, and gives attachment to the Pterygoideus internus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Medial Pterygoid Plate.</B>&#151;The medial pterygoid plate is narrower and longer than the lateral; it curves lateralward at its lower extremity into a hook-like process, the <B>pterygoid hamulus,</B> around which the tendon of the Tensor veli palatini glides. The <B>lateral surface</B> of this plate forms part of the pterygoid fossa, the <B>medial surface</B> constitutes the lateral boundary of the choana or posterior aperture of the corresponding nasal cavity. Superiorly the medial plate is prolonged on to the under surface of the body as a thin lamina, named the <B>vaginal process,</B> which articulates in front with the sphenoidal process of the palatine and behind this with the ala of the vomer. The angular prominence between the posterior margin <!-- PAGE NUM="152" -->of the vaginal process and the medial border of the scaphoid fossa is named the <B>pterygoid tubercle,</B> and immediately above this is the posterior opening of the pterygoid canal. On the under surface of the vaginal process is a furrow, which is converted into a canal by the sphenoidal process of the palatine bone, for the transmission of the pharyngeal branch of the internal maxillary artery and the pharyngeal nerve from the sphenopalatine ganglion. The pharyngeal aponeurosis is attached to the entire length of the posterior edge of the medial plate, and the Constrictor pharyngis superior takes origin from its lower third. Projecting backward from near the middle of the posterior edge of this plate is an angular process, the <B>processus tubarius,</B> which supports the pharyngeal end of the auditory tube. The anterior margin of the plate articulates with the posterior border of the vertical part of the palatine bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Sphenoidal Conch&#230; (<I>conch&#230; sphenoidales; sphenoidal turbinated processes</I>).</B>&#151;The sphenoidal conch&#230; are two thin, curved plates, situated at the anterior and lower part of the body of the sphenoid. An aperture of variable size exists in the anterior wall of each, and through this the sphenoidal sinus opens into the nasal cavity. Each is irregular in form, and tapers to a point behind, being broader and thinner in front. Its upper surface is concave, and looks toward the cavity of the sinus; its under surface is convex, and forms part of the roof of the corresponding nasal cavity. Each bone articulates in front with the ethmoid, laterally with the palatine; its pointed posterior extremity is placed above the vomer, and is received between the root of the pterygoid process laterally and the rostrum of the sphenoid medially. A small portion of the sphenoidal concha sometimes enters into the formation of the medial wall of the orbit, between the lamina papyracea of the ethmoid in front, the orbital plate of the palatine below, and the frontal bone above.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="19"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;19</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;Until the seventh or eighth month of fetal life the body of the sphenoid consists of two parts, viz., one in front of the tuberculum sell&#230;, the <I>presphenoid,</I> with which the small wings are continuous; the other, comprising the sella turcica and dorsum sell&#230;, the <I>postsphenoid,</I> with which are associated the great wings, and pterygoid processes. The greater part of the bone is ossified in cartilage. There are fourteen centers in all, <I>six</I> for the presphenoid and <I>eight</I> for the postsphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="20"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;20</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus148.html"><IMG WIDTH=210 HEIGHT=101 SRC="/107/Images/small/image148.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i148"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>148&#150; </FONT>Sphenoid bone at birth. Posterior aspect. (<A HREF="/107/illus148.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Presphenoid.</I>&#151;About the ninth week of fetal life an ossific center appears for each of the small wings (orbitosphenoids) just lateral to the optic foramen; shortly afterward two nuclei appear in the presphenoid part of the body. The sphenoidal conch&#230; are each developed from a center which makes its appearance about the fifth month;<A NAME="txt32">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/35.html#note32">32</A> at birth they consist of small triangular lamin&#230;, and it is not until the third year that they become hollowed out and coneshaped; about the fourth year they fuse with the labyrinths of the ethmoid, and between the ninth and twelfth years they unite with the sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="21"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;21</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Postsphenoid.</I>&#151;The first ossific nuclei are those for the great wings (ali-sphenoids)<A NAME="txt33">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/35.html#note33">33</A>. One makes its appearance in each wing between the foramen rotundum and foramen ovale about the eighth week. The orbital plate and that part of the sphenoid which is found in the temporal fossa, as well as the lateral pterygoid plate, are ossified in membrane (Fawcett)<A NAME="txt34">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/35.html#note34">34</A>. Soon after, the centers for the postsphenoid part of the body appear, one on either side of the sella turcica, and become blended together about the middle of fetal life. Each medial pterygoid plate (with the exception of its hamulus) is ossified in membrane, and its center probably appears about the ninth or tenth week; the hamulus becomes chondrified during the third month, and almost at once undergoes <!-- PAGE NUM="153" -->ossification (Fawcett).<A NAME="txt35">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/35.html#note35">35</A> The medial joins the lateral pterygoid plate about the sixth month. About the fourth month a center appears for each lingula and speedily joins the rest of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="22"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;22</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The presphenoid is united to the postsphenoid about the eighth month, and at birth the bone is in three pieces <A HREF="/107/illus148.html">(Fig. 148):</A> a central, consisting of the body and small wings, and two lateral, each comprising a great wing and pterygoid process. In the first year after birth the great wings and body unite, and the small wings extend inward above the anterior part of the body, and, meeting with each other in the middle line, form an elevated smooth surface, termed the <I>jugum sphenoidale.</I> By the twenty-fifth year the sphenoid and occipital are completely fused. Between the pre- and postsphenoid there are occasionally seen the remains of a canal, the <I>canalis cranio-pharyngeus,</I> through which, in early fetal life, the hypophyseal diverticulum of the buccal ectoderm is transmitted.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="23"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;23</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The sphenoidal sinuses are present as minute cavities at the time of birth (Onodi), but do not attain their full size until after puberty.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="24"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;24</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Intrinsic Ligaments of the Sphenoid.</B>&#151;The more important of these are: the <I>pterygospinous,</I> stretching between the spina angularis and the lateral pterygoid plate (see <I>cervical fascia</I>); the <I>interclinoid,</I> a fibrous process joining the anterior to the posterior clinoid process; and the <I>caroticoclinoid,</I> connecting the anterior to the middle clinoid process. These ligaments occasionally ossify.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="25"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;25</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The sphenoid articulates with <I>twelve</I> bones: four single, the vomer, ethmoid, frontal, and occipital; and four paired, the parietal, temporal, zygomatic, and palatine.<A NAME="txt36">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/35.html#note36">36</A></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="26"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;26</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5a. 6. Ethmoid bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Ethmoidale)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>ethmoid bone</B> is exceedingly light and spongy, and cubical in shape; it is situated at the anterior part of the base of the cranium, between the two orbits, at the roof of the nose, and contributes to each of these cavities. It consists of four parts: a <B>horizontal</B> or <B>cribriform plate,</B> forming part of the base of the cranium; a <B>perpendicular plate,</B> constituting part of the nasal septum; and two <B>lateral masses</B> or labyrinths.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Cribiform Plate (<I>lamina cribrosa; horizontal lamina</I>).</B>&#151;The cribriform plate <A HREF="/107/illus149.html">(Fig. 149)</A> is received into the ethmoidal notch of the frontal bone and roofs in the nasal cavities. Projecting upward from the middle line of this plate is a thick, smooth, triangular process, the <B>crista galli,</B> so called from its resemblance to a cock&#146;s comb. The long thin posterior border of the crista galli serves for the attachment of the falx cerebri. Its anterior border, short and thick, articulates with the frontal bone, and presents two small projecting <B>al&#230;,</B> which are received into corresponding depressions in the frontal bone and complete the foramen cecum. Its sides are smooth, and sometimes bulging from the presence of a small air sinus in the interior. On either side of the crista galli, the cribriform plate is narrow and deeply grooved; it supports the olfactory bulb and is perforated by foramina for the passage of the olfactory nerves. The foramina in the middle of the groove are small and transmit the nerves to the roof of the nasal cavity; those at the medial and lateral parts of the groove are larger&#151;the former transmit the nerves to the upper part of the nasal septum, the latter those to the superior nasal concha. At the front part of the cribriform <!-- PAGE NUM="154" -->plate, on either side of the crista galli, is a small fissure which is occupied by a process of dura mater. Lateral to this fissure is a notch or foramen which transmits the nasociliary nerve; from this notch a groove extends backward to the anterior ethmoidal foramen.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus149.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=172 SRC="/107/Images/small/image149.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i149"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>149&#150; </FONT>Ethmoid bone from above. (<A HREF="/107/illus149.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus150.html"><IMG WIDTH=241 HEIGHT=175 SRC="/107/Images/small/image150.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i150"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>150&#150; </FONT>Perpendicular plate of ethmoid. Shown by removing the right labyrinth. (<A HREF="/107/illus150.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Perpendicular Plate (<I>lamina perpendicularis; vertical plate</I>).</B>&#151;The perpendicular plate (<A HREF="/107/illus150.html">Figs. 150,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus151.html">151</A>) is a thin, flattened lamina, polygonal in form, which descends from the under surface of the cribriform plate, and assists in forming the septum of the nose; it is generally deflected a little to one or other side. The <B>anterior border</B> articulates with the spine of the frontal bone and the crest of the nasal bones. The <B>posterior border</B> articulates by its upper half with the sphenoidal crest, by its lower with the vomer. The <B>inferior border</B> is thicker than the posterior, and serves for the attachment of the septal cartilage of the nose. The surfaces of the plate are smooth, except above, where numerous grooves and canals are seen; these lead from the medial foramina on the cribriform plate and lodge filaments of the olfactory nerves.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Labyrinth</B> or <B>Lateral Mass</B> (<I>labyrinthus ethmoidalis</I>) consists of a number of thin-walled cellular cavities, the <B>ethmoidal cells,</B> arranged in three groups, <I>anterior, middle,</I> and <I>posterior,</I> and interposed between two vertical plates of bone; the lateral plate forms part of the orbit, the medial, part of the corresponding nasal cavity. In the disarticulated bone many of these cells are opened into, but when the bones are articulated, they are closed in at every part, except where they open into the nasal cavity.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>upper surface</B> of the labyrinth <A HREF="/107/illus149.html">(Fig. 149)</A> presents a number of half-broken cells, the walls of which are completed, in the articulated skull, by the edges of the ethmoidal notch of the frontal bone. Crossing this surface are two grooves, converted into canals by articulation with the frontal; they are the <B>anterior</B> and <B>posterior ethmoidal canals,</B> and open on the inner wall of the orbit. The <B>posterior surface</B> presents large irregular cellular cavities, which are closed in <!-- PAGE NUM="155" -->by articulation with the sphenoidal concha and orbital process of the palatine. The <B>lateral surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus152.html">(Fig. 152)</A> is formed of a thin, smooth, oblong plate, the <B>lamina papyracea</B> (<I>os planum</I>), which covers in the middle and posterior ethmoidal cells and forms a large part of the medial wall of the orbit; it articulates above with the orbital plate of the frontal bone, below with the maxilla and orbital process of the palatine, in front with the lacrimal, and behind with the sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus151.html"><IMG WIDTH=178 HEIGHT=146 SRC="/107/Images/small/image151.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i151"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>151&#150; </FONT>Ethmoid bone from behind. (<A HREF="/107/illus151.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus152.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=152 SRC="/107/Images/small/image152.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i152"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>152&#150; </FONT>Ethmoid bone from the right side. (<A HREF="/107/illus152.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus153.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=250 SRC="/107/Images/small/image153.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i153"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>153&#150; </FONT>Lateral wall of nasal cavity, showing ethmoid bone in position. (<A HREF="/107/illus153.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In front of the lamina papyracea are some broken air cells which are overlapped and completed by the lacrimal bone and the frontal process of the maxilla. A curved lamina, the <B>uncinate process,</B> projects downward and backward from this part of the labyrinth; it forms a small part of the medial wall of the maxillary sinus, and articulates with the ethmoidal process of the inferior nasal concha.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="156" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial surface</B> of the labyrinth <A HREF="/107/illus153.html">(Fig. 153)</A> forms part of the lateral wall of the corresponding nasal cavity. It consists of a thin lamella, which descends from the under surface of the cribriform plate, and ends below in a free, convoluted margin, the <B>middle nasal concha.</B> It is rough, and marked above by numerous grooves, directed nearly vertically downward from the cribriform plate; they lodge branches of the olfactory nerves, which are distributed to the mucous membrane covering the superior nasal concha. The back part of the surface is subdivided by a narrow oblique fissure, the <B>superior meatus</B> of the nose, bounded above by a thin, curved plate, the <B>superior nasal concha;</B> the posterior ethmoidal cells open into this meatus. Below, and in front of the superior meatus, is the convex surface of the middle nasal concha; it extends along the whole length of the medial surface of the labyrinth, and its lower margin is free and thick. The lateral surface of the middle concha is concave, and assists in forming the <B>middle meatus</B> of the nose. The middle ethmoidal cells open into the central part of this meatus, and a sinuous passage, termed the <B>infundibulum,</B> extends upward and forward through the labyrinth and communicates with the anterior ethmoidal cells, and in about 50 per cent. of skulls is continued upward as the frontonasal duct into the frontal sinus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The ethmoid is ossified in the cartilage of the nasal capsule by <I>three</I> centers: one for the perpendicular plate, and one for each labyrinth.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The labyrinths are first developed, ossific granules making their appearance in the region of the lamina papyracea between the fourth and fifth months of fetal life, and extending into the conch&#230;. At birth, the bone consists of the two labyrinths, which are small and ill-developed. During the first year after birth, the perpendicular plate and crista galli begin to ossify from a single center, and are joined to the labyrinths about the beginning of the second year. The cribriform plate is ossified partly from the perpendicular plate and partly from the labyrinths. The development of the ethmoidal cells begins during fetal life.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The ethmoid articulates with <I>fifteen</I> bones: four of the cranium&#151;the frontal, the sphenoid, and the two sphenoidal conch&#230;; and eleven of the face&#151;the two nasals, two maxill&#230;, two lacrimals, two palatines, two inferior nasal conch&#230;, and the vomer.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--

</script>

src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Sutural or Wormian<A NAME="txt37">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/36.html#note37">37</A> Bones.</B>&#151;In addition to the usual centers of ossification of the cranium, others may occur in the course of the sutures, giving rise to irregular, isolated bones, termed <I>sutural</I> or <I>Wormian bones.</I> They occur most frequently in the course of the lambdoidal suture, but are occasionally seen at the fontanelles, especially the posterior. One, the <I>pterion ossicle,</I> sometimes exists between the sphenoidal angle of the parietal and the great wing of the sphenoid. They have a tendency to be more or less symmetrical on the two sides of the skull, and vary much in size. Their number is generally limited to two or three; but more than a hundred have been found in the skull of an adult hydrocephalic subject.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. The Facial Bones. 1. The Nasal Bones</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">
