<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Ossa Faciei) &amp; (Ossa Nasalia)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>nasal bones</B> are two small oblong bones, varying in size and form in different individuals; they are placed side by side at the middle and upper part of the face, and form, by their junction, &#147;the bridge&#148; of the nose <A HREF="/107/illus190.html">(Fig. 190</A>). Each has two surfaces and four borders.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>outer surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus155.html">(Fig. 155)</A> is concavoconvex from above downward, convex from side to side; it is covered by the Procerus and Compressor naris, and perforated about its center by a foramen, for the transmission of a small vein. The <B>inner surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus156.html">(Fig. 156)</A> is concave from side to side, and is traversed from above downward, by a groove for the passage of a branch of the nasociliary nerve.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>superior border</B> is narrow, thick, and serrated for articulation with the nasal notch of the frontal bone. The <B>inferior border</B> is thin, and gives attachment <!-- PAGE NUM="157" -->to the lateral cartilage of the nose; near its middle is a notch which marks the end of the groove just referred to. The <B>lateral border</B> is serrated, bevelled at the expense of the inner surface above, and of the outer below, to articulate with the frontal process of the maxilla. The <B>medial border,</B> thicker above than below, articulates with its fellow of the opposite side, and is prolonged behind into a vertical crest, which forms part of the nasal septum: this crest articulates, from above downward, with the spine of the frontal, the perpendicular plate of the ethmoid, and the septal cartilage of the nose.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus154.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=230 SRC="/107/Images/small/image154.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i154"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>154&#150; </FONT>Articulation of nasal and lacrimal bones with maxilla. (<A HREF="/107/illus154.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus155.html"><IMG WIDTH=113 HEIGHT=124 SRC="/107/Images/small/image155.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i155"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>155&#150; </FONT>Right nasal bone. Outer surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus155.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus156.html"><IMG WIDTH=106 HEIGHT=125 SRC="/107/Images/small/image156.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i156"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>156&#150; </FONT>Right nasal bone. Inner surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus156.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;Each bone is ossified from <I>one</I> center, which appears at the beginning of the third month of fetal life in the membrane overlying the front part of the cartilaginous nasal capsule.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The nasal articulates with four bones: two of the cranium, the frontal and ethmoid, and two of the face, the opposite nasal and the maxilla.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. 2. The Maxill&#230; (Upper Jaw)</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The <B>maxill&#230;</B> are the largest bones of the face, excepting the mandible, and form, by their union, the whole of the upper jaw. Each assists in forming the <!-- PAGE NUM="158" -->boundaries of three cavities, viz., the roof of the mouth, the floor and lateral wall of the nose and the floor of the orbit; it also enters into the formation of two foss&#230;, the infratemporal and pterygopalatine, and two fissures, the inferior orbital and pterygomaxillary.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Each bone consists of a body and four processes&#151;zygomatic, frontal, alveolar, and palatine.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body (<I>corpus maxill&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The body is somewhat pyramidal in shape, and contains a large cavity, the <B>maxillary sinus</B> (<I>antrum of Highmore</I>). It has four surfaces&#151;an anterior, a posterior or infratemporal, a superior or orbital, and a medial or nasal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus157.html">(Fig. 157)</A> is directed forward and lateralward. It presents at its lower part a series of eminences corresponding to the positions of the roots of the teeth. Just above those of the incisor teeth is a depression, the <B>incisive fossa,</B> which gives origin to the Depressor al&#230; nasi; to the alveolar border below the fossa is attached a slip of the Orbicularis oris; above and a little lateral to it, the Nasalis arises. Lateral to the incisive fossa is another depression, the <B>canine fossa;</B> it is larger and deeper than the incisive fossa, and is separated from it by a vertical ridge, the <B>canine eminence,</B> corresponding to the socket of the canine tooth; the canine fossa gives origin to the Caninus. Above the fossa is the <B>infraorbital foramen,</B> the end of the infraorbital canal; it transmits the infraorbital vessels and nerve. Above the foramen is the margin of the orbit, which affords attachment to part of the Quadratus labii superioris. Medially, the anterior surface is limited by a deep concavity, the <B>nasal notch,</B> the margin of which gives attachment to the Dilatator naris posterior and ends below in a pointed process, which with its fellow of the opposite side forms the <B>anterior nasal spine.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus157.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=217 SRC="/107/Images/small/image157.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i157"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>157&#150; </FONT>Left maxilla. Outer surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus157.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>infratemporal surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus157.html">(Fig. 157)</A> is convex, directed backward and lateralward, and forms part of the infratemporal fossa. It is separated from the anterior surface by the zygomatic process and by a strong ridge, extending upward from the socket of the first molar tooth. It is pierced about its center by the apertures of the <B>alveolar canals,</B> which transmit the posterior superior alveolar vessels and nerves. At the lower part of this surface is a rounded eminence, the <B>maxillary <!-- PAGE NUM="159" -->tuberosity,</B> especially prominent after the growth of the wisdom tooth; it is rough on its lateral side for articulation with the pyramidal process of the palatine bone and in some cases articulates with the lateral pterygoid plate of the sphenoid. It gives origin to a few fibers of the Pterygoideus internus. Immediately above this is a smooth surface, which forms the anterior boundary of the pterygopalatine fossa, and presents a groove, for the maxillary nerve; this groove is directed lateralward and slightly upward, and is continuous with the infraorbital groove on the orbital surface.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>orbital surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus157.html">(Fig. 157)</A> is smooth and triangular, and forms the greater part of the floor of the orbit. It is bounded <I>medially</I> by an irregular margin which in front presents a notch, the <B>lacrimal notch;</B> behind this notch the margin articulates with the lacrimal, the lamina papyracea of the ethmoid and the orbital process of the palatine. It is bounded <I>behind</I> by a smooth rounded edge which forms the anterior margin of the inferior orbital fissure, and sometimes articulates at its lateral extremity with the orbital surface of the great wing of the sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus158.html"><IMG WIDTH=275 HEIGHT=225 SRC="/107/Images/small/image158.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i158"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>158&#150; </FONT>Left maxilla. Nasal surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus158.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;It is limited <I>in front</I> by part of the circumference of the orbit, which is continuous medially with the frontal process, and laterally with the zyogmatic process. Near the middle of the posterior part of the orbital surface is the <B>infraorbital groove,</B> for the passage of the infraorbital vessels and nerve. The groove begins at the middle of the posterior border, where it is continuous with that near the upper edge of the infratemporal surface, and, passing forward, ends in a canal, which subdivides into two branches. One of the canals, the <B>infraorbital canal,</B> opens just below the margin of the orbit; the other, which is smaller, runs downward in the substance of the anterior wall of the maxillary sinus, and transmits the anterior superior alveolar vessels and nerve to the front teeth of the maxilla. From the back part of the infraorbital canal, a second small canal is sometimes given off; it runs downward in the lateral wall of the sinus, and conveys the middle alveolar nerve to the premolar teeth. At the medial and forepart of the orbital surface just lateral to the lacrimal groove, is a depression, which gives origin to the Obliquus oculi inferior.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="160" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>nasal surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus158.html">(Fig. 158)</A> presents a large, irregular opening leading into the maxillary sinus. At the upper border of this aperture are some broken air cells, which, in the articulated skull, are closed in by the ethmoid and lacrimal bones. Below the aperture is a smooth concavity which forms part of the inferior meatus of the nasal cavity, and behind it is a rough surface for articulation with the perpendicular part of the palatine bone; this surface is traversed by a groove, commencing near the middle of the posterior border and running obliquely downward and forward; the groove is converted into a canal, the <B>pterygopalatine canal,</B> by the palatine bone. In front of the opening of the sinus is a deep groove, the <B>lacrimal groove,</B> which is converted into the nasolacrimal canal, by the lacrimal bone and inferior nasal concha; this canal opens into the inferior meatus of the nose and transmits the nasolacrimal duct. More anteriorly is an oblique ridge, the <B>conchal crest,</B> for articulation with the inferior nasal concha. The shallow concavity above this ridge forms part of the atrium of the middle meatus of the nose, and that below it, part of the inferior meatus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus159.html"><IMG WIDTH=350 HEIGHT=251 SRC="/107/Images/small/image159.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i159"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>159&#150; </FONT>Left maxillary sinus opened from the exterior. (<A HREF="/107/illus159.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Maxillary Sinus or Antrum of Highmore (<I>sinus maxillaris</I>).</B>&#151;The maxillary sinus is a large pyramidal cavity, within the body of the maxilla: its <B>apex,</B> directed lateralward, is formed by the zygomatic process; its <B>base,</B> directed medialward, by the lateral wall of the nose. Its walls are everywhere exceedingly thin, and correspond to the nasal orbital, anterior, and infratemporal surfaces of the body of the bone. Its <B>nasal wall,</B> or <B>base,</B> presents, in the disarticulated bone, a large, irregular aperture, communicating with the nasal cavity. In the articulated skull this aperture is much reduced in size by the following bones: the uncinate process of the ethmoid above, the ethmoidal process of the inferior nasal concha below, the vertical part of the palatine behind, and a small part of the lacrimal above and in front (<A HREF="/107/illus158.html">Figs. 158,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus159.html">159</A>); the sinus communicates with the middle meatus <!-- PAGE NUM="161" -->of the nose, generally by two small apertures left between the above-mentioned bones. In the fresh state, usually only one small opening exists, near the upper part of the cavity; the other is closed by mucous membrane. On the <B>posterior wall</B> are the <B>alveolar canals,</B> transmitting the posterior superior alveolar vessels and nerves to the molar teeth. The <B>floor</B> is formed by the alveolar process of the maxilla, and, if the sinus be of an average size, is on a level with the floor of the nose; if the sinus be large it reaches below this level.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Projecting into the floor of the antrum are several conical processes, corresponding to the roots of the first and second molar teeth;<A NAME="txt38">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/38.html#note38">38</A> in some cases the floor is perforated by the fangs of the teeth. The infraorbital canal usually projects into the cavity as a well-marked ridge extending from the roof to the anterior wall; additional ridges are sometimes seen in the posterior wall of the cavity, and are caused by the alveolar canals. The size of the cavity varies in different skulls, and even on the two sides of the same skull.<A NAME="txt39">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/38.html#note39">39</A></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>The Zygomatic Process (<I>processus zygomaticus; malar process</I>).</B>&#151;The zygomatic process is a rough triangular eminence, situated at the angle of separation of the anterior, zygomatic, and orbital surfaces. <I>In front</I> it forms part of the anterior surface; <I>behind,</I> it is concave, and forms part of the infratemporal fossa; <I>above,</I> it is rough and serrated for articulation with the zygomatic bone; while <I>below,</I> it presents the prominent arched border which marks the division between the anterior and infratemporal surfaces.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Frontal Process (<I>processus frontalis; nasal process</I>).</B>&#151;The frontal process is a strong plate, which projects upward, medialward, and backward, by the side of the nose, forming part of its lateral boundary. Its <I>lateral surface</I> is smooth, continuous with the anterior surface of the body, and gives attachment to the Quadratus labii superioris, the Orbicularis oculi, and the medial palpebral ligament. Its <I>medial surface</I> forms part of the lateral wall of the nasal cavity; at its upper part is a rough, uneven area, which articulates with the ethmoid, closing in the anterior ethmoidal cells; below this is an oblique ridge, the <B>ethmoidal crest,</B> the posterior end of which articulates with the middle nasal concha, while the anterior part is termed the <B>agger nasi;</B> the crest forms the upper limit of the atrium of the middle meatus. The <I>upper border</I> articulates with the frontal bone and the <I>anterior</I> with the nasal; the <I>posterior border</I> is thick, and hollowed into a groove, which is continuous below with the lacrimal groove on the nasal surface of the body: by the articulation of the medial margin of the groove with the anterior border of the lacrimal a corresponding groove on the lacrimal is brought into continuity, and together they form the <B>lacrimal fossa</B> for the lodgement of the lacrimal sac. The lateral margin of the groove is named the <B>anterior lacrimal crest,</B> and is continuous below with the orbital margin; at its junction with the orbital surface is a small tubercle, the <B>lacrimal tubercle,</B> which serves as a guide to the position of the lacrimal sac.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Alveolar Process (<I>processus alveolaris</I>).</B>&#151;The alveolar process is the thickest and most spongy part of the bone. It is broader behind than in front, and excavated into deep cavities for the reception of the teeth. These cavities are eight in number, and vary in size and depth according to the teeth they contain. That for the canine tooth is the deepest; those for the molars are the widest, and are subdivided into minor cavities by septa; those for the incisors are single, but deep and narrow. The Buccinator arises from the outer surface of this process, as far forward as the first molar tooth. When the maxill&#230; are articulated with each other, their alveolar processes together form the <B>alveolar arch;</B> the center of the anterior margin of this arch is named the <B>alveolar point.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="162" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Palatine Process (<I>processus palatinus; palatal process</I>).</B>&#151;The palatine process, thick and strong, is horizontal and projects medialward from the nasal surface of the bone. It forms a considerable part of the floor of the nose and the roof of the mouth and is much thicker in front than behind. Its <I>inferior surface</I> <A HREF="/107/illus160.html">(Fig. 160)</A> is concave, rough and uneven, and forms, with the palatine process of the opposite bone, the anterior three-fourths of the hard plate. It is perforated by numerous foramina for the passage of the nutrient vessels; is channelled at the back part of its lateral border by a groove, sometimes a canal, for the transmission of the descending palatine vessels and the anterior palatine nerve from the spheno-palatine ganglion; and presents little depressions for the lodgement of the palatine glands. When the two maxill&#230; are articulated, a funnel-shaped opening, the <B>incisive foramen,</B> is seen in the middle line, immediately behind the incisor teeth. In this opening the orifices of two lateral canals are visible; they are named the <B>incisive canals</B> or <B>foramina of Stenson;</B> through each of them passes the terminal branch of the descending palatine artery and the nasopalatine nerve. Occasionally two additional canals are present in the middle line; they are termed the <B>foramina of Scarpa,</B> and when present transmit the nasopalatine nerves, the left passing through the anterior, and the right through the posterior canal. On the under surface of the palatine process, a delicate linear suture, well seen in young skulls, may sometimes be noticed extending lateralward and forward on either side from the incisive foramen to the interval between the lateral incisor and the canine tooth. The small part in front of this suture constitutes the <B>premaxilla</B> (<I>os incisivum</I>), which in most vertebrates forms an independent bone; it includes the whole thickness of the alveolus, the corresponding part of the floor of the nose and the anterior nasal spine, and contains the sockets of the incisor teeth. The <I>upper surface</I> of the palatine process is concave from side to side, smooth, and forms the greater part of the floor of the nasal cavity. It presents, close to its medial margin, the <!-- PAGE NUM="163" -->upper orifice of the incisive canal. The <I>lateral border</I> of the process is incorporated with the rest of the bone. The <I>medial border</I> is thicker in front than behind, and is raised above into a ridge, the <B>nasal crest,</B> which, with the corresponding ridge of the opposite bone, forms a groove for the reception of the vomer. The front part of this ridge rises to a considerable height, and is named the <B>incisor crest;</B> it is prolonged forward into a sharp process, which forms, together with a similar process of the opposite bone, the <B>anterior nasal spine.</B> The <I>posterior border</I> is serrated for articulation with the horizontal part of the palatine bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus160.html"><IMG WIDTH=246 HEIGHT=250 SRC="/107/Images/small/image160.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i160"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>160&#150; </FONT>The bony palate and alveolar arch. (<A HREF="/107/illus160.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The maxilla is ossified in membrane. Mall<A NAME="txt40">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/38.html#note40">40</A> and Fawcett<A NAME="txt41">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/38.html#note41">41</A> maintain that it is ossified from <I>two</I> centers only, one for the maxilla proper and one for the premaxilla. These centers appear during the sixth week of fetal life and unite in the beginning of the third month, but the suture between the two portions persists on the palate until nearly middle life. Mall states that the frontal process is developed from both centers. The maxillary sinus appears as a shallow groove on the nasal surface of the bone about the fourth month of fetal life, but does not reach its full size until after the second dentition. The maxilla was formerly described as ossifying from six centers, viz., one, the <I>orbitonasal,</I> forms that portion of the body of the bone which lies medial to the infraorbital canal, including the medial part of the floor of the orbit and the lateral wall of the nasal cavity; a second, the <I>zygomatic,</I> gives origin to the portion which lies lateral to the infraorbital canal, including the zygomatic process; from a third, the <I>palatine,</I> is developed the palatine process posterior to the incisive canal together with the adjoining part of the nasal wall; a fourth, the <I>premaxillary,</I> forms the incisive bone which carries the incisor teeth and corresponds to the premaxilla of the lower vertebrates;<A NAME="txt42">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/38.html#note42">42</A> a fifth, the <I>nasal,</I> gives rise to the frontal process and the portion above the canine tooth; and a sixth, the <I>infravomerine,</I> lies between the palatine and premaxillary centers and beneath the vomer; this center, together with the corresponding center of the opposite bone, separates the incisive canals from each other.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus161.html"><IMG WIDTH=110 HEIGHT=91 SRC="/107/Images/small/image161.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i161"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>161&#150; </FONT>Anterior surface of maxilla at birth. (<A HREF="/107/illus161.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus162.html"><IMG WIDTH=92 HEIGHT=94 SRC="/107/Images/small/image162.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i162"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>162&#150; </FONT>Inferior surface of maxilla at birth. (<A HREF="/107/illus162.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The maxilla articulates with <I>nine</I> bones: two of the cranium, the frontal and ethmoid, and seven of the face, viz., the nasal, zygomatic, lacrimal, inferior nasal concha, palatine, vomer, and its fellow of the opposite side. Sometimes it articulates with the orbital surface, and sometimes with the lateral pterygoid plate of the sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Changes Produced in the Maxilla by Age</B>At birth the transverse and antero-posterior diameters of the bone are each greater than the vertical. The frontal process is well-marked and the body of the bone consists of little more than the alveolar process, the teeth sockets reaching almost to the floor of the orbit. The maxillary sinus presents the appearance of a furrow on the lateral wall of the nose. In the adult the vertical diameter is the greatest, owing to the development of the alveolar process and the increase in size of the sinus. In old age the bone reverts in some measure to the infantile condition; its height is diminished, and after the loss of the teeth the alveolar process is absorbed, and the lower part of the bone contracted and reduced in thickness.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. 3. The Lacrimal Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Lacrimale)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>lacrimal bone,</B> the smallest and most fragile bone of the face, is situated at the front part of the medial wall of the orbit <A HREF="/107/illus164.html">(Fig. 164</A>). It has two surfaces and four borders.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="164" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>lateral</B> or <B>orbital surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus163.html">(Fig. 163)</A> is divided by a vertical ridge, the <B>posterior lacrimal crest,</B> into two parts. In front of this crest is a longitudinal groove, the <B>lacrimal sulcus</B> (<I>sulcus lacrimalis</I>), the inner margin of which unites with the frontal process of the maxilla, and the lacrimal fossa is thus completed. The upper part of this fossa lodges the lacrimal sac, the lower part, the naso-lacrimal duct. The portion behind the crest is smooth, and forms part of the medial wall of the orbit. The crest, with a part of the orbital surface immediately behind it, gives origin to the lacrimal part of the Orbicularis oculi and ends below in a small, hook-like projection, the <B>lacrimal hamulus,</B> which articulates with the lacrimal tubercle of the maxilla, and completes the upper orifice of the lacrimal canal; it sometimes exists as a separate piece, and is then called the <B>lesser lacrimal bone.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial</B> or <B>nasal surface</B> presents a longitudinal furrow, corresponding to the crest on the lateral surface. The area in front of this furrow forms part of the middle meatus of the nose; that behind it articulates with the ethmoid, and completes some of the anterior ethmoidal cells.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus163.html"><IMG WIDTH=86 HEIGHT=121 SRC="/107/Images/small/image163.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i163"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>163&#150; </FONT>Left lacrimal bone. Orbital surface. Enlarged. (<A HREF="/107/illus163.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;Of the <I>four borders</I> the <B>anterior</B> articulates with the frontal process of the maxilla; the <B>posterior</B> with the lamina papyracea of the ethmoid; the <B>superior</B> with the frontal bone. The <B>inferior</B> is divided by the lower edge of the posterior lacrimal crest into two parts: the posterior part articulates with the orbital plate of the maxilla; the anterior is prolonged downward as the <B>descending process,</B> which articulates with the lacrimal process of the inferior nasal concha, and assists in forming the canal for the nasolacrimal duct.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The lacrimal is ossified from a single center, which appears about the twelfth week in the membrane covering the cartilaginous nasal capsule.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The lacrimal articulates with <I>four</I> bones: two of the cranium, the frontal and ethmoid, and two of the face, the maxilla and the inferior nasal concha.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. 4. The Zygomatic Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Zygomaticum; Malar Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>zygomatic bone</B> is small and quadrangular, and is situated at the upper and lateral part of the face: it forms the prominence of the cheek, part of the lateral wall and floor of the orbit, and parts of the temporal and infratemporal foss&#230; <A HREF="/107/illus164.html">(Fig. 164</A>). It presents a malar and a temporal surface; four processes, the frontosphenoidal, orbital, maxillary, and temporal; and four borders.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>malar surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus165.html">(Fig. 165)</A> is convex and perforated near its center by a small aperture, the <B>zygomaticofacial foramen,</B> for the passage of the zygomaticofacial nerve and vessels; below this foramen is a slight elevation, which gives origin to the Zygomaticus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>temporal surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus166.html">(Fig. 166),</A> directed backward and medialward, is concave, presenting medially a rough, triangular area, for articulation with the maxilla, and laterally a smooth, concave surface, the upper part of which forms the anterior boundary of the temporal fossa, the lower a part of the infratemporal fossa. Near the center of this surface is the <B>zygomaticotemporal foramen</B> for the transmission of the zygomaticotemporal nerve.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Processes.</B>&#151;The <B>frontosphenoidal process</B> is thick and serrated, and articulates with the zygomatic process of the frontal bone. On its orbital surface, just within the orbital margin and about 11 mm. below the zygomaticofrontal suture is a tubercle of varying size and form, but present in 95 per cent. of skulls (Whitnall<A NAME="txt43">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/40.html#note43">43</A>). <!-- PAGE NUM="165" -->The <B>orbital process</B> is a thick, strong plate, projecting backward and medialward from the orbital margin. Its <I>antero-medial surface</I> forms, by its junction with the orbital surface of the maxilla and with the great wing of the sphenoid, part of the floor and lateral wall of the orbit. On it are seen the orifices of two canals, the <B>zygomatico&#246;rbital foramina;</B> one of these canals opens into the temporal fossa, the other on the malar surface of the bone; the former transmits the zygomaticotemporal, the latter the zygomaticofacial nerve. Its <I>postero-lateral surface,</I> smooth and convex, forms parts of the temporal and infratemporal foss&#230;. Its <I>anterior margin,</I> smooth and rounded, is part of the circumference of the orbit. Its <I>superior</I> <I>margin,</I> rough, and directed horizontally, articulates with the frontal bone behind the zygomatic process. Its <I>posterior margin</I> is serrated for articulation, with the great wing of the sphenoid and the orbital surface of the maxilla. At the angle of junction of the sphenoidal and maxillary portions, a short, concave, non-articular <!-- PAGE NUM="166" -->part is generally seen; this forms the anterior boundary of the inferior orbital fissure: occasionally, this non-articular part is absent, the fissure then being completed by the junction of the maxilla and sphenoid, or by the interposition of a small sutural bone in the angular interval between them. The <B>maxillary process</B> presents a rough, triangular surface which articulates with the maxilla. The <B>temporal process,</B> long, narrow, and serrated, articulates with the zygomatic process of the temporal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus164.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=189 SRC="/107/Images/small/image164.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i164"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>164&#150; </FONT>Left zygomatic bone <I>in situ.</I> (<A HREF="/107/illus164.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus165.html"><IMG WIDTH=184 HEIGHT=176 SRC="/107/Images/small/image165.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i165"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>165&#150; </FONT>Left zygomatic bone. Malar surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus165.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus166.html"><IMG WIDTH=199 HEIGHT=189 SRC="/107/Images/small/image166.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i166"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>166&#150; </FONT>Left zygomatic bone. Temporal surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus166.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>antero-superior</B> or <B>orbital border</B> is smooth, concave, and forms a considerable part of the circumference of the orbit. The <B>antero-inferior</B> or <B>maxillary border</B> is rough, and bevelled at the expense of its inner table, to articulate with the maxilla; near the orbital margin it gives origin to the Quadratus labii superioris. The <B>postero-superior</B> or <B>temporal border,</B> curved like an italic letter <I>f,</I> is continuous above with the commencement of the temporal line, and below with the upper border of the zygomatic arch; the temporal fascia is attached to it. The <B>postero-inferior</B> or <B>zygomatic border</B> affords attachment by its rough edge to the Masseter.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#151;The zygomatic bone is generally described as ossifying from three centers&#151;one for the malar and two for the orbital portion; these appear about the eighth week and fuse about the fifth month of fetal life. Mall describes it as being ossified from one center which appears just beneath and to the lateral side of the orbit. After birth, the bone is sometimes divided by a horizontal suture into an upper larger, and a lower smaller division. In some quadrumana the zygomatic bone consists of two parts, an orbital and a malar.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The zygomatic articulates with <I>four</I> bones: the frontal, sphenoidal, temporal, and maxilla.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus167.html"><IMG WIDTH=261 HEIGHT=226 SRC="/107/Images/small/image167.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i167"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>167&#150; </FONT>Articulation of left palatine bone with maxilla. (<A HREF="/107/illus167.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. 5. The Palatine Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Palatinum; Palate Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>palatine bone</B> is situated at the back part of the nasal cavity between the maxilla and the pterygoid process of the sphenoid <A HREF="/107/illus167.html">(Fig. 167</A>). It contributes to the walls of three cavities: the floor and lateral wall of the nasal cavity, the roof of the mouth, and the floor of the orbit; it enters into the formation of two foss&#230;, the <B>pterygopalatine</B> and <B>pterygoid foss&#230;;</B> and one fissure, the <B>inferior orbital fissure.</B> The palatine bone somewhat resembles the letter L, and consists of a horizontal and a vertical part and three outstanding processes&#151;viz., the pyramidal <!-- PAGE NUM="167" -->process, which is directed backward and lateralward from the junction of the two parts, and the orbital and sphenoidal processes, which surmount the vertical part, and are separated by a deep notch, the sphenopalatine notch.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Horizontal Part (<I>pars horizontalis; horizontal plate</I>) (<A HREF="/107/illus168.html">Figs. 168,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus169.html">169</A>).</B>&#151;The horizontal part is quadrilateral, and has two surfaces and four borders.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>superior surface,</B> concave from side to side, forms the back part of the floor of the nasal cavity. The <B>inferior surface,</B> slightly concave and rough, forms, with the corresponding surface of the opposite bone, the posterior fourth of the hard palate. Near its posterior margin may be seen a more or less marked transverse ridge for the attachment of part of the aponeurosis of the Tensor veli palatini.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus168.html"><IMG WIDTH=220 HEIGHT=209 SRC="/107/Images/small/image168.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i168"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>168&#150; </FONT>Left palatine bone. Nasal aspect. Enlarged. (<A HREF="/107/illus168.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus169.html"><IMG WIDTH=170 HEIGHT=210 SRC="/107/Images/small/image169.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i169"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>169&#150; </FONT>Left palatine bone. Posterior aspect. Enlarged. (<A HREF="/107/illus169.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior border</B> is serrated, and articulates with the palatine process of the maxilla. The <B>posterior border</B> is concave, free, and serves for the attachment of the soft palate. Its medial end is sharp and pointed, and, when united with that of the opposite bone, forms a projecting process, the <B>posterior nasal spine</B> for the attachment of the Musculus uvul&#230;. The <B>lateral border</B> is united with the lower margin of the perpendicular part, and is grooved by the lower end of the pterygopalatine canal. The <B>medial border,</B> the thickest, is serrated for articulation with its fellow of the opposite side; its superior edge is raised into a ridge, which, united with the ridge of the opposite bone, forms the <B>nasal crest</B> for articulation with the posterior part of the lower edge of the vomer.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Vertical Part (<I>pars perpendicularis; perpendicular plate</I>) (<A HREF="/107/illus168.html">Figs. 168,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus169.html">169</A>).</B>&#151;The vertical part is thin, of an oblong form, and presents two surfaces and four borders.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>nasal surface</B> exhibits at its lower part a broad, shallow depression, which forms part of the inferior meatus of the nose. Immediately above this is a well-marked horizontal ridge, the <B>conchal crest,</B> for articulation with the inferior nasal concha; still higher is a second broad, shallow depression, which forms part of the middle meatus, and is limited above by a horizontal crest less prominent than the inferior, the <B>ethmoidal crest,</B> for articulation with the middle nasal concha. Above the ethmoidal crest is a narrow, horizontal groove, which forms part of the superior meatus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="168" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>maxillary surface</B> is rough and irregular throughout the greater part of its extent, for articulation with the nasal surface of the maxilla; its upper and back part is smooth where it enters into the formation of the pterygopalatine fossa; it is also smooth in front, where it forms the posterior part of the medial wall of the maxillary sinus. On the posterior part of this surface is a deep vertical groove, converted into the <B>pterygopalatine canal,</B> by articulation with the maxilla; this canal transmits the descending palatine vessels, and the anterior palatine nerve.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior border</B> is thin and irregular; opposite the conchal crest is a pointed, projecting lamina, the <B>maxillary process,</B> which is directed forward, and closes in the lower and back part of the opening of the maxillary sinus. The <B>posterior border</B> <A HREF="/107/illus169.html">(Fig. 169)</A> presents a deep groove, the edges of which are serrated for articulation with the medial pterygoid plate of the sphenoid. This border is continuous above with the sphenoidal process; below it expands into the pyramidal process. The <B>superior border</B> supports the orbital process in front and the sphenoidal process behind. These processes are separated by the <B>sphenopalatine notch,</B> which is converted into the <B>sphenopalatine foramen</B> by the under surface of the body of the sphenoid. In the articulated skull this foramen leads from the pterygopalatine fossa into the posterior part of the superior meatus of the nose, and transmits the sphenopalatine vessels and the superior nasal and nasopalatine nerves. The <B>inferior border</B> is fused with the lateral edge of the horizontal part, and immediately in front of the pyramidal process is grooved by the lower end of the pterygopalatine canal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Pyramidal Process or Tuberosity (<I>processus pyramidalis</I>).</B>&#151;The pyramidal process projects backward and lateralward from the junction of the horizontal and vertical parts, and is received into the angular interval between the lower extremities of the pterygoid plates. On its <B>posterior surface</B> is a smooth, grooved, triangular area, limited on either side by a rough articular furrow. The furrows articulate with the pterygoid plates, while the grooved intermediate area completes the lower part of the pterygoid fossa and gives origin to a few fibers of the Pterygoideus internus. The anterior part of the <B>lateral surface</B> is rough, for articulation with the tuberosity of the maxilla; its posterior part consists of a smooth triangular area which appears, in the articulated skull, between the tuberosity of the maxilla and the lower part of the lateral pterygoid plate, and completes the lower part of the infratemporal fossa. On the <B>base</B> of the pyramidal process, close to its union with the horizontal part, are the lesser palatine foramina for the transmission of the posterior and middle palatine nerves.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Orbital Process (<I>processus orbitalis</I>).</B>&#151;The orbital process is placed on a higher level than the sphenoidal, and is directed upward and lateralward from the front of the vertical part, to which it is connected by a constricted neck. It presents five surfaces, which enclose an air cell. Of these surfaces, three are articular and two non-articular. The articular surfaces are: (1) the <B>anterior</B> or <B>maxillary,</B> directed forward, lateralward, and downward, of an oblong form, and rough for articulation with the maxilla; (2) the <B>posterior</B> or <B>sphenoidal,</B> directed backward, upward, and medialward; it presents the opening of the air cell, which usually communicates with the sphenoidal sinus; the margins of the opening are serrated for articulation with the sphenoidal concha; (3) the <B>medial</B> or <B>ethmoidal,</B> directed forward, articulates with the labyrinth of the ethmoid. In some cases the air cell opens on this surface of the bone and then communicates with the posterior ethmoidal cells. More rarely it opens on both surfaces, and then communicates with the posterior ethmoidal cells and the sphenoidal sinus. The non-articular surfaces are: (1) the <B>superior</B> or <B>orbital,</B> directed upward and lateralward; it is triangular in shape, and forms the back part of the floor of the orbit; and (2) the <B>lateral,</B> of an oblong form, directed toward the pterygopalatine fossa; it is separated <!-- PAGE NUM="169" -->from the orbital surface by a rounded border, which enters into the formation of the inferior orbital fissure.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>The Sphenoidal Process (<I>processus sphenoidalis</I>).</B>&#151;The sphenoidal process is a thin, compressed plate, much smaller than the orbital, and directed upward and medialward. It presents three surfaces and two borders. The <B>superior surface</B> articulates with the root of the pterygoid process and the under surface of the sphenoidal concha, its medial border reaching as far as the ala of the vomer; it presents a groove which contributes to the formation of the pharyngeal canal. The <B>medial surface</B> is concave, and forms part of the lateral wall of the nasal cavity. The <B>lateral surface</B> is divided into an articular and a non-articular portion: the former is rough, for articulation with the medial pterygoid plate; the latter is smooth, and forms part of the pterygopalatine fossa. The <B>anterior border</B> forms the posterior boundary of the sphenopalatine notch. The <B>posterior border,</B> serrated at the expense of the outer table, articulates with the medial pterygoid plate.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The orbital and sphenoidal processes are separated from one another by the <B>sphenopalatine notch.</B> Sometimes the two processes are united above, and form between them a complete foramen <A HREF="/107/illus168.html">(Fig. 168),</A> or the notch may be crossed by one or more spicules of bone, giving rise to two or more foramina.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The palatine bone is ossified in membrane from a single center, which makes its appearance about the sixth or eighth week of fetal life at the angle of junction of the two parts of the bone. From this point ossification spreads medialward to the horizontal part, downward into the pyramidal process, and upward into the vertical part. Some authorities describe the bone as ossifying from four centers: one for the pyramidal process and portion of the vertical part behind the pterygopalatine groove; a second for the rest of the vertical and the horizontal parts; a third for the orbital, and a fourth for the sphenoidal process. At the time of birth the height of the vertical part is about equal to the transverse width of the horizontal part, whereas in the adult the former measures about twice as much as the latter.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The palatine articulates with <I>six</I> bones: the sphenoid, ethmoid, maxilla, inferior nasal concha, vomer, and opposite palatine.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. 6. The Inferior Nasal Concha</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Concha Nasalis Inferior; Inferior Turbinated Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>inferior nasal concha</B> extends horizontally along the lateral wall of the nasal cavity <A HREF="/107/illus170.html">(Fig. 170)</A> and consists of a lamina of spongy bone, curled upon itself like a scroll. It has two surfaces, two borders, and two extremities.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus171.html">(Fig. 171)</A> is convex, perforated by numerous apertures, and traversed by longitudinal grooves for the lodgement of vessels. The <B>lateral surface</B> is concave <A HREF="/107/illus172.html">(Fig. 172),</A> and forms part of the inferior meatus. Its <B>upper border</B> is thin, irregular, and connected to various bones along the lateral wall of the nasal cavity. It may be divided into three portions: of these, the anterior articulates with the conchal crest of the maxilla; the posterior with the conchal crest of the palatine; the middle portion presents three well-marked processes, which vary much in their size and form. Of these, the anterior or <B>lacrimal process</B> is small and pointed and is situated at the junction of the anterior fourth with the posterior three-fourths of the bone: it articulates, by its apex, with the descending process of the lacrimal bone, and, by its margins, with the groove on the back of the frontal process of the maxilla, and thus assists in forming the canal for the nasolacrimal duct. Behind this process a broad, thin plate, the <B>ethmoidal process,</B> ascends to join the uncinate process of the ethmoid; from its lower border a thin lamina, the <B>maxillary process,</B> curves downward and lateralward; it articulates with the maxilla and forms a part of the medial wall of the maxillary sinus. The <B>inferior border</B> is free, thick, and cellular in structure, more especially in the middle of the bone. Both <B>extremities</B> are more or less pointed, the posterior being the more tapering.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="170" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The inferior nasal concha is ossified from a single center, which appears about the fifth month of fetal life in the lateral wall of the cartilaginous nasal capsule.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The inferior nasal concha articulates with <I>four</I> bones: the ethmoid, maxilla, lacrimal, and palatine.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus170.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=242 SRC="/107/Images/small/image170.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i170"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>170&#150; </FONT>Lateral wall of right nasal cavity showing inferior concha <I>in situ.</I> (<A HREF="/107/illus170.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus171.html"><IMG WIDTH=189 HEIGHT=94 SRC="/107/Images/small/image171.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i171"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>171&#150; </FONT>Right inferior nasal concha. Medial surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus171.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus172.html"><IMG WIDTH=159 HEIGHT=87 SRC="/107/Images/small/image172.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i172"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>172&#150; </FONT>Right inferior nasal concha. Lateral surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus172.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. 7. The Vomer</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The <B>vomer</B> is situated in the median plane, but its anterior portion is frequently bent to one or other side. It is thin, somewhat quadrilateral in shape, and forms the hinder and lower part of the nasal septum <A HREF="/107/illus173.html">(Fig. 173</A>); it has two surfaces and four borders. The <B>surfaces</B> <A HREF="/107/illus174.html">(Fig. 174)</A> are marked by small furrows for blood-vessels, and on each is the <B>nasopalatine groove,</B> which runs obliquely downward and forward, and lodges the nasopalatine nerve and vessels. The <B>superior border,</B> the thickest, presents a deep furrow, bounded on either side by a horizontal projecting ala of bone; the furrow receives the rostrum of the sphenoid, while the margins of the al&#230; articulate with the vaginal processes of the medial pterygoid plates of the sphenoid behind, and with the sphenoidal processes of the palatine bones in front. The <B>inferior border</B> articulates with the crest formed by the maxill&#230; <!-- PAGE NUM="171" -->and palatine bones. The <B>anterior border</B> is the longest and slopes downward and forward. Its upper half is fused with the perpendicular plate of the ethmoid; its lower half is grooved for the inferior margin of the septal cartilage of the nose. The <B>posterior border</B> is free, concave, and separates the choan&#230;. It is thick and bifid above, thin below.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus173.html"><IMG WIDTH=275 HEIGHT=243 SRC="/107/Images/small/image173.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i173"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>173&#150; </FONT>Median wall of left nasal cavity showing vomer <I>in situ.</I> (<A HREF="/107/illus173.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;At an early period the septum of the nose consists of a plate of cartilage, the <I>ethmovomerine cartilage.</I> The postero-superior part of this cartilage is ossified to form the perpendicular plate of the ethmoid; its antero-inferior portion persists as the septal cartilage, while the vomer is ossified in the membrane covering its postero-inferior part. Two ossific centers, one on either side of the middle line, appear about the eighth week of fetal life in this part of the membrane, and hence the vomer consists primarily of two lamell&#230;. About the third month these unite below, and thus a deep groove is formed in which the cartilage is lodged. As growth proceeds, the union of the lamell&#230; extends upward and forward, and at the same time the intervening plate of cartilage undergoes absorption. By the age of puberty the lamell&#230; are almost completely united to form a median plate, but evidence of the bilaminar origin of the bone is seen in the everted al&#230; of its upper border and the groove on its anterior margin.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus174.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=142 SRC="/107/Images/small/image174.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i174"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>174&#150; </FONT>The vomer. (<A HREF="/107/illus174.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus175.html"><IMG WIDTH=102 HEIGHT=46 SRC="/107/Images/small/image175.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i175"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>175&#150; </FONT>Vomer of infant. (<A HREF="/107/illus175.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="172" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The vomer articulates with <I>six</I> bones: two of the cranium, the sphenoid and ethmoid; and four of the face, the two maxill&#230; and the two palatine bones; it also articulates with the septal cartilage of the nose.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. 8. The Mandible (Lower Jaw)</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Inferior Maxillary Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>mandible,</B> the largest and strongest bone of the face, serves for the reception of the lower teeth. It consists of a curved, horizontal portion, the <B>body,</B> and two perpendicular portions, the <B>rami,</B> which unite with the ends of the body nearly at right angles.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body (<I>corpus mandibul&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The body is curved somewhat like a horseshoe and has two surfaces and two borders.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>external surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus176.html">(Fig. 176)</A> is marked in the median line by a faint ridge, indicating the <B>symphysis</B> or line of junction of the two pieces of which the bone is composed at an early period of life. This ridge divides below and encloses a triangular eminence, the <B>mental protuberance,</B> the base of which is depressed in the center but raised on either side to form the <B>mental tubercle.</B> On either side of the symphysis, just below the incisor teeth, is a depression, the <B>incisive fossa,</B> which gives origin to the Mentalis and a small portion of the Orbicularis oris. Below the second premolar tooth, on either side, midway between the upper and lower borders of the body, is the <B>mental foramen,</B> for the passage of the mental vessels and nerve. Running backward and upward from each mental tubercle is a faint ridge, the <B>oblique line,</B> which is continuous with the anterior border of the ramus; it affords attachment to the Quadratus labii inferioris and Triangularis; the Platysma is attached below it.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus176.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=201 SRC="/107/Images/small/image176.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i176"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>176&#150; </FONT>Mandible. Outer surface. Side view. (<A HREF="/107/illus176.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>internal surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus177.html">(Fig. 177)</A> is concave from side to side. Near the lower part of the symphysis is a pair of laterally placed spines, termed the <B>mental spines,</B> which give origin to the Genioglossi. Immediately below these is a second pair of spines, or more frequently a median ridge or impression, for the origin of the Geniohyoidei. In some cases the mental spines are fused to form a single eminence, in others they are absent and their position is indicated merely by an irregularity of the surface. Above the mental spines a median foramen and furrow are sometimes seen; they mark the line of union of the halves of the bone. Below the mental <!-- PAGE NUM="173" -->spines, on either side of the middle line, is an oval depression for the attachment of the anterior belly of the Digastricus. Extending upward and backward on either side from the lower part of the symphysis is the <B>mylohyoid line,</B> which gives origin to the Mylohyoideus; the posterior part of this line, near the alveolar margin, gives attachment to a small part of the Constrictor pharyngis superior, and to the pterygomandibular raph&#233;. Above the anterior part of this line is a smooth triangular area against which the sublingual gland rests, and below the hinder part, an oval fossa for the submaxillary gland.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>superior</B> or <B>alveolar border,</B> wider behind than in front, is hollowed into cavities, for the reception of the teeth; these cavities are sixteen in number, and vary in depth and size according to the teeth which they contain. To the outer lip of the superior border, on either side, the Buccinator is attached as far forward as the first molar tooth. The <B>inferior border</B> is rounded, longer than the superior, and thicker in front than behind; at the point where it joins the lower border of the ramus a shallow groove; for the external maxillary artery, may be present.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus177.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=204 SRC="/107/Images/small/image177.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i177"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>177&#150; </FONT>Mandible. Inner surface. Side view. (<A HREF="/107/illus177.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Ramus (<I>ramus mandibul&#230;; perpendicular portion</I>).</B>&#151;The ramus is quadrilateral in shape, and has two surfaces, four borders, and two processes.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>lateral surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus176.html">(Fig. 176)</A> is flat and marked by oblique ridges at its lower part; it gives attachment throughout nearly the whole of its extent to the Masseter. The <B>medial surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus177.html">(Fig. 177)</A> presents about its center the oblique <B>mandibular foramen,</B> for the entrance of the inferior alveolar vessels and nerve. The margin of this opening is irregular; it presents in front a prominent ridge, surmounted by a sharp spine, the <B>lingula mandibul&#230;,</B> which gives attachment to the sphenomandibular ligament; at its lower and back part is a notch from which the <B>mylohyoid groove</B> runs obliquely downward and forward, and lodges the mylohyoid vessels and nerve. Behind this groove is a rough surface, for the insertion of the Pterygoideus internus. The <B>mandibular canal</B> runs obliquely downward and forward in the ramus, and then horizontally forward in the body, where it is placed under the alveoli and communicates with them by small openings. On arriving at the incisor teeth, it turns back to communicate with the mental foramen, giving off two small canals which run to the cavities containing the incisor teeth. <!-- PAGE NUM="174" -->In the posterior two-thirds of the bone the canal is situated nearer the internal surface of the mandible; and in the anterior third, nearer its external surface. It contains the inferior alveolar vessels and nerve, from which branches are distributed to the teeth. The <B>lower border</B> of the ramus is thick, straight, and continuous with the inferior border of the body of the bone. At its junction with the posterior border is the <B>angle of the mandible,</B> which may be either inverted or everted and is marked by rough, oblique ridges on each side, for the attachment of the Masseter laterally, and the Pterygoideus internus medially; the stylomandibular ligament is attached to the angle between these muscles. The <B>anterior border</B> is thin above, thicker below, and continuous with the oblique line. The <B>posterior border</B> is thick, smooth, rounded, and covered by the parotid gland. The <B>upper border</B> is thin, and is surmounted by two processes, the <B>coronoid</B> in front and the <B>condyloid</B> behind, separated by a deep concavity, the <B>mandibular notch.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Coronoid Process</B> (<I>processus coronoideus</I>) is a thin, triangular eminence, which is flattened from side to side and varies in shape and size. Its <I>anterior border</I> is convex and is continuous below with the anterior border of the ramus; its <I>posterior border</I> is concave and forms the anterior boundary of the mandibular notch. Its <I>lateral surface</I> is smooth, and affords insertion to the Temporalis and Masseter. Its <I>medial surface</I> gives insertion to the Temporalis, and presents a ridge which begins near the apex of the process and runs downward and forward to the inner side of the last molar tooth. Between this ridge and the anterior border is a grooved triangular area, the upper part of which gives attachment to the Temporalis, the lower part to some fibers of the Buccinator.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Condyloid Process</B> (<I>processus condyloideus</I>) is thicker than the coronoid, and consists of two portions: the <B>condyle,</B> and the constricted portion which supports it, the <B>neck.</B> The <B>condyle</B> presents an articular surface for articulation with the articular disk of the temporomandibular joint; it is convex from before backward and from side to side, and extends farther on the posterior than on the anterior surface. Its long axis is directed medialward and slightly backward, and if prolonged to the middle line will meet that of the opposite condyle near the anterior margin of the foramen magnum. At the lateral extremity of the condyle is a small tubercle for the attachment of the temporomandibular ligament. The <B>neck</B> is flattened from before backward, and strengthened by ridges which descend from the forepart and sides of the condyle. Its posterior surface is convex; its anterior presents a depression for the attachment of the Pterygoideus externus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>mandibular notch,</B> separating the two processes, is a deep semilunar depression, and is crossed by the masseteric vessels and nerve.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The mandible is ossified in the fibrous membrane covering the outer surfaces of Meckel&#146;s cartilages. These cartilages form the cartilaginous bar of the mandibular arch (see p. 66), and are two in number, a right and a left. Their proximal or cranial ends are connected with the ear capsules, and their distal extremities are joined to one another at the symphysis by mesodermal tissue. They run forward immediately below the condyles and then, bending downward, lie in a groove near the lower border of the bone; in front of the canine tooth they incline upward to the symphysis. From the proximal end of each cartilage the malleus and incus, two of the bones of the middle ear, are developed; the next succeeding portion, as far as the lingula, is replaced by fibrous tissue, which persists to form the sphenomandibular ligament. Between the lingula and the canine tooth the cartilage disappears, while the portion of it below and behind the incisor teeth becomes ossified and incorporated with this part of the mandible.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Ossification takes place in the membrane covering the outer surface of the ventral end of Meckel&#146;s cartilage <A HREF="/107/illus178.html">(Figs. 178</A> <A HREF="/107/illus179.html">to</A> <A HREF="/107/illus180.html"></A><A HREF="/107/illus181.html">181),</A> and each half of the bone is formed from a single center which appears, near the mental foramen, about the sixth week of fetal life. By the tenth week the portion of Meckel&#146;s cartilage which lies below and behind the incisor teeth is surrounded and invaded by the membrane bone. Somewhat later, accessory nuclei of cartilage make their appearance, viz., a wedge-shaped nucleus in the condyloid process and extending downward through the ramus; a small strip along the anterior border of the coronoid process; and smaller nuclei in the front part of both alveolar walls and along the front of the lower border of the bone. These accessory nuclei possess no separate ossific centers, but are invaded by the surrounding membrane <!-- PAGE NUM="175" -->bone and undergo absorption. The inner alveolar border, usually described as arising from a separate ossific center (<I>splenial center</I>), is formed in the human mandible by an ingrowth from the main mass of the bone. At birth the bone consists of two parts, united by a fibrous symphysis, in which ossification takes place during the first year.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The foregoing description of the ossification of the mandible is based on the researches of Low<A NAME="txt44">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/44.html#note44">44</A> and Fawcett,<A NAME="txt45">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/44.html#note45">45</A> and differs somewhat from that usually given.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The mandible articulates with the <I>two</I> temporal bones.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus178.html"><IMG WIDTH=196 HEIGHT=98 SRC="/107/Images/small/image178.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i178"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>178&#150; </FONT>Mandible of human embryo 24 mm. long. Outer aspect. (From model by Low.) (<A HREF="/107/illus178.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus179.html"><IMG WIDTH=218 HEIGHT=100 SRC="/107/Images/small/image179.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i179"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>179&#150; </FONT>Mandible of human embryo 24 mm. long. Inner aspect. (From model by Low.) (<A HREF="/107/illus179.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus180.html"><IMG WIDTH=280 HEIGHT=135 SRC="/107/Images/small/image180.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i180"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>180&#150; </FONT>Mandible of human embryo 95 mm. long. Outer aspect. Nuclei of cartilage stippled. (From model by Low.) (<A HREF="/107/illus180.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
