<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus181.html"><IMG WIDTH=308 HEIGHT=131 SRC="/107/Images/small/image181.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i181"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>181&#150; </FONT>Mandible of human embryo 95 mm. long. Inner aspect. Nuclei of cartilage stippled. (From model by Low.) (<A HREF="/107/illus181.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Changes Produced in the Mandible by Age</B><B>At birth</B> <A HREF="/107/illus182.html">(Fig. 182)</A> the body of the bone is a mere shell, containing the sockets of the two incisor, the canine, and the two deciduous molar teeth, imperfectly partitioned off from one another. The mandibular canal is of large size, and runs near the lower border of the bone; the mental foramen opens beneath the socket of the first deciduous molar tooth. The angle is obtuse (175&#176;), and the condyloid portion is nearly in line with the body. The coronoid process is of comparatively large size, and projects above the level of the condyle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="176" -->
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus182.html"><IMG WIDTH=136 HEIGHT=66 SRC="/107/Images/small/image182.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i182"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>182&#150; </FONT>At birth. (<A HREF="/107/illus182.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus183.html"><IMG WIDTH=184 HEIGHT=114 SRC="/107/Images/small/image183.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i183"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>183&#150; </FONT>In childhood. (<A HREF="/107/illus183.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus184.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=184 SRC="/107/Images/small/image184.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i184"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>184&#150; </FONT>In the adult. (<A HREF="/107/illus184.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus185.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=172 SRC="/107/Images/small/image185.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i185"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>185&#150; </FONT>In old age. Side view of the mandible at different periods of life. (<A HREF="/107/illus185.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="177" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<B>After birth</B> <A HREF="/107/illus183.html">(Fig. 183)</A> the two segments of the bone become joined at the symphysis, from below upward, in the first year; but a trace of separation may be visible in the beginning of the second year, near the alveolar margin. The body becomes elongated in its whole length, but more especially behind the mental foramen, to provide space for the three additional teeth developed in this part. The depth of the body increases owing to increased growth of the alveolar part, to afford room for the roots of the teeth, and by thickening of the subdental portion which enables the jaw to withstand the powerful action of the masticatory muscles; but the alveolar portion is the deeper of the two, and, consequently, the chief part of the body lies above the oblique line. The mandibular canal, after the second dentition, is situated just above the level of the mylohyoid line; and the mental foramen occupies the position usual to it in the adult. The angle becomes less obtuse, owing to the separation of the jaws by the teeth; about the fourth year it is 140&#176;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<B>In the adult</B> <A HREF="/107/illus184.html">(Fig. 184)</A> the alveolar and subdental portions of the body are usually of equal depth. The mental foramen opens midway between the upper and lower borders of the bone, and the mandibular canal runs nearly parallel with the mylohyoid line. The ramus is almost vertical in direction, the angle measuring from 110&#176; to 120&#176;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<B>In old age</B> <A HREF="/107/illus185.html">(Fig. 185)</A> the bone becomes greatly reduced in size, for with the loss of the teeth the alveolar process is absorbed, and, consequently, the chief part of the bone is below the oblique line. The mandibular canal, with the mental foramen opening from it, is close to the alveolar border. The ramus is oblique in direction, the angle measures about 140&#176;, and the neck of the condyle is more or less bent backward.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5b. 9. The Hyoid Bone</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Os Hyoideum; Lingual Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>hyoid bone</B> is shaped like a horseshoe, and is suspended from the tips of the styloid processes of the temporal bones by the stylohyoid ligaments. It consists of five segments, viz., a <B>body,</B> two <B>greater cornua,</B> and two <B>lesser cornua.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body or Basihyal (<I>corpus oss. hyoidei</I>).</B>&#151;The body or central part is of a quadrilateral form. Its <B>anterior surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus186.html">(Fig. 186)</A> is convex and directed forward and upward. It is crossed in its upper half by a well-marked transverse ridge with a slight downward convexity, and in many cases a vertical median ridge divides it into two lateral halves. The portion of the vertical ridge above the transverse line is present in a majority of specimens, but the lower portion is evident only in rare cases. The anterior surface gives insertion to the Geniohyoideus in the greater part of its extent both above and below the transverse ridge; a portion of the origin of the Hyoglossus notches the lateral margin of the Geniohyoideus attachment. Below the transverse ridge the Mylohyoideus, Sternohyoideus, and Omohyoideus are inserted. The <B>posterior surface</B> is smooth, concave, directed backward and downward, and separated from the epiglottis by the hyothyroid membrane and a quantity of loose areolar tissue; a bursa intervenes between it and the hyothyroid membrane. The <B>superior border</B> is rounded, and gives attachment to the hyothyroid membrane and some aponeurotic fibers of the Genioglossus. The <B>inferior border</B> affords insertion medially to the Sternohyoideus and laterally to the Omohyoideus and occasionally a portion of the Thyreohyoideus. It also gives attachment to the Levator glandul&#230; thyreoide&#230;, when this muscle is present. In early life the <B>lateral borders</B> are connected to the greater cornua by synchondroses; after middle life usually by bony union.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus186.html"><IMG WIDTH=234 HEIGHT=160 SRC="/107/Images/small/image186.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i186"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>186&#150; </FONT>Hyoid bone. Anterior surface. Enlarged. (<A HREF="/107/illus186.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="178" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Greater Cornua or Thyrohyals (<I>cornua majora</I>).</B>&#151;The greater cornua project backward from the lateral borders of the body; they are flattened from above downward and diminish in size from before backward; each ends in a tubercle to which is fixed the lateral hyothyroid ligament. The <B>upper surface</B> is rough close to its lateral border, for muscular attachments: the largest of these are the origins of the Hyoglossus and Constrictor pharyngis medius which extend along the whole length of the cornu; the Digastricus and Stylohyoideus have small insertions in front of these near the junction of the body with the cornu. To the <B>medial border</B> the hyothyroid membrane is attached, while the anterior half of the <B>lateral border</B> gives insertion to the Thyreohyoideus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Lesser Cornua or Ceratohyals (<I>cornua minora</I>).</B>&#151;The lesser cornu are two small, conical eminences, attached by their bases to the angles of junction between the body and greater cornua. They are connected to the body of the bone by fibrous tissue, and occasionally to the greater cornua by distinct diarthrodial joints, which usually persist throughout life, but occasionally become ankylosed.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The lesser cornua are situated in the line of the transverse ridge on the body and appear to be morphological continuations of it (Parsons<A NAME="txt46">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/45.html#note46">46</A>). The apex of each cornu gives attachment to the stylohyoid ligament;<A NAME="txt47">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/45.html#note47">47</A> the Chondroglossus rises from the medial side of the base.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The hyoid is ossified from <I>six</I> centers: two for the body, and one for each cornu. Ossification commences in the greater cornua toward the end of fetal life, in the body shortly afterward, and in the lesser cornua during the first or second year after birth.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5c. The Exterior of the Skull</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The skull as a whole may be viewed from different points, and the views so obtained are termed the <B>norm&#230;</B> of the skull; thus, it may be examined from above (<B>norma verticalis</B>), from below (<B>norma basalis</B>), from the side (<B>norma lateralis</B>), from behind (<B>norma occipitalis</B>), or from the front (<B>norma frontalis</B>).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Norma Verticalis.</B>&#151;When viewed from above the outline presented varies greatly in different skulls; in some it is more or less oval, in others more nearly circular. The surface is traversed by three sutures, viz.: (1) the <B>coronal sutures,</B> nearly transverse is direction, between the frontal and parietals; (2) the <B>sagittal sutures,</B> medially placed, between the parietal bones, and deeply serrated in its anterior two-thirds; and (3) the upper part of the <B>lambdoidal suture,</B> between the parietals and the occipital. The point of junction of the sagittal and coronal suture is named the <B>bregma,</B> that of the sagittal and lambdoid sutures, the <B>lambda;</B> they indicate respectively the positions of the anterior and posterior fontanelles in the fetal skull. On either side of the sagittal suture are the <B>parietal eminence</B> and <B>parietal foramen</B>&#151;the latter, however, is frequently absent on one or both sides. The skull is often somewhat flattened in the neighborhood of the parietal foramina, and the term <B>obelion</B> is applied to that point of the sagittal suture which is on a level with the foramina. In front is the <B>glabella,</B> and on its lateral aspects are the <B>superciliary arches,</B> and above these the <B>frontal eminences.</B> Immediately above the glabella may be seen the remains of the <B>frontal suture;</B> in a small percentage of skulls this suture persists and extends along the middle line to the bregma. Passing backward and upward from the zygomatic processes of the frontal bone are the <B>temporal lines,</B> which mark the upper limits of the temporal foss&#230;. The zygomatic arches may or may not be seen projecting beyond the anterior portions of these lines.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="179" -->
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus187.html"><IMG WIDTH=359 HEIGHT=584 SRC="/107/Images/small/image187.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i187"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>187&#150; </FONT>Base of skull. Inferior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus187.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Norma Basalis <A HREF="/107/illus187.html">(Fig. 187</A>).</B>&#151;The inferior surface of the base of the skull, exclusive of the mandible, is bounded in front by the incisor teeth in the maxill&#230;; behind, <!-- PAGE NUM="180" -->by the superior nuchal lines of the occipital; and laterally by the alveolar arch, the lower border of the zygomatic bone, the zygomatic arch and an imaginary line extending from it to the mastoid process and extremity of the superior nuchal line of the occipital. It is formed by the palatine processes of the maxill&#230; and palatine bones, the vomer, the pterygoid processes, the under surfaces of the great wings, spinous processes, and part of the body of the sphenoid, the under surfaces of the squam&#230; and mastoid and petrous portions of the temporals, and the under surface of the occipital bone. The anterior part or hard palate projects below the level of the rest of the surface, and is bounded in front and laterally by the alveolar arch containing the sixteen teeth of the maxill&#230;. Immediately behind the incisor teeth is the <B>incisive foramen.</B> In this foramen are two lateral apertures, the openings of the <B>incisive canals</B> (<I>foramina of Stenson</I>) which transmit the anterior branches of the descending palatine vessels, and the nasopalatine nerves. Occasionally two additional canals are present in the incisive foramen; they are termed the <B>foramina of Scarpa</B> and are situated in the middle line; when present they transmit the nasopalatine nerves. The vault of the hard palate is concave, uneven, perforated by numerous foramina, marked by depressions for the palatine glands, and traversed by a crucial suture formed by the junction of the four bones of which it is composed. In the young skull a suture may be seen extending on either side from the incisive foramen to the interval between the lateral incisor and canine teeth, and marking off the os incisivum or premaxillary bone. At either posterior angle of the hard palate is the <B>greater palatine foramen,</B> for the transmission of the descending palatine vessels and anterior palatine nerve; and running forward and medialward from it a groove, for the same vessels and nerve. Behind the posterior palatine foramen is the <B>pyramidal process of the palatine bone,</B> perforated by one or more <B>lesser palatine foramina,</B> and marked by the commencement of a transverse ridge, for the attachment of the tendinous expansion of the Tensor veli palatini. Projecting backward from the center of the posterior border of the hard palate is the <B>posterior nasal spine,</B> for the attachment of the Musculus uvul&#230;. Behind and above the hard palate are the <B>choan&#230;,</B> measuring about 2.5 cm. in their vertical and 1.25 cm. in their transverse diameters. They are separated from one another by the vomer, and each is bounded above by the body of the sphenoid, below by the horizontal part of the palatine bone, and laterally by the medial pterygoid plate of the sphenoid. At the superior border of the vomer may be seen the expanded al&#230; of this bone, receiving between them the rostrum of the sphenoid. Near the lateral margins of the al&#230; of the vomer, at the roots of the pterygoid processes, are the <B>pharyngeal canals.</B> The pterygoid process presents near its base the <B>pterygoid canal,</B> for the transmission of a nerve and artery. The medial pterygoid plate is long and narrow; on the lateral side of its base is the <B>scaphoid fossa,</B> for the origin of the Tensor veli palatini, and at its lower extremity the <B>hamulus,</B> around which the tendon of this muscle turns. The lateral pterygoid plate is broad; its lateral surface forms the medial boundary of the infratemporal fossa, and affords attachment to the Pterygoideus externus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Behind the nasal cavities is the basilar portion of the occipital bone, presenting near its center the <B>pharyngeal tubercle</B> for the attachment of the fibrous raph&#233; of the pharynx, with depressions on either side for the insertions of the Rectus capitis anterior and Longus capitis. At the base of the lateral pterygoid plate is the <B>foramen ovale,</B> for the transmission of the mandibular nerve, the accessory meningeal artery, and sometimes the lesser superficial petrosal nerve; behind this are the <B>foramen spinosum</B> which transmits the middle meningeal vessels, and the prominent <B>spina angularis</B> (<I>sphenoidal spine</I>), which gives attachment to the sphenomandibular ligament and the Tensor veli palatini. Lateral to the spina angularis is the <B>mandibular fossa,</B> divided into two parts by the <B>petrotympanic fissure;</B> the anterior portion, concave, smooth bounded in front by the <B>articular tubercle,</B> <!-- PAGE NUM="181" -->serves for the articulation of the condyle of the mandible; the posterior portion, rough and bounded behind by the tympanic part of the temporal, is sometimes occupied by a part of the parotid gland. Emerging from between the lamin&#230; of the vaginal process of the tympanic part is the <B>styloid process;</B> and at the base of this process is the <B>stylomastoid foramen,</B> for the exit of the facial nerve, and entrance of the stylomastoid artery. Lateral to the stylomastoid foramen, between the tympanic part and the mastoid process, is the <B>tympanomastoid fissure,</B> for the auricular branch of the vagus. Upon the medial side of the mastoid process is the <B>mastoid notch</B> for the posterior belly of the Digastricus, and medial to the notch, the <B>occipital groove</B> for the occipital artery. At the base of the medial pterygoid plate is a large and somewhat triangular aperture, the <B>foramen lacerum,</B> bounded in front by the great wing of the sphenoid, behind by the apex of the petrous portion of the temporal bone, and medially by the body of the sphenoid and basilar portion of the occipital bone; it presents in front the posterior orifice of the <B>pterygoid canal;</B> behind, the aperture of the <B>carotid canal.</B> The lower part of this opening is filled up in the fresh state by a fibrocartilaginous plate, across the upper or cerebral surface of which the internal carotid artery passes. Lateral to this aperture is a groove, the <B>sulcus tub&#230; auditiv&#230;,</B> between the petrous part of the temporal and the great wing of the sphenoid. This sulcus is directed lateralward and backward from the root of the medial pterygoid plate and lodges the cartilaginous part of the auditory tube; it is continuous behind with the canal in the temporal bone which forms the bony part of the same tube. At the bottom of this sulcus is a narrow cleft, the <B>petrosphenoidal fissure,</B> which is occupied, in the fresh condition, by a plate of cartilage. Behind this fissure is the under surface of the petrous portion of the temporal bone, presenting, near its apex, the quadrilateral rough surface, part of which affords attachment to the Levator veli palatini; lateral to this surface is the orifice of the <B>carotid canal,</B> and medial to it, the depression leading to the <B>aqu&#230;ductus cochle&#230;,</B> the former transmitting the internal carotid artery and the carotid plexus of the sympathetic, the latter serving for the passage of a vein from the cochlea. Behind the carotid canal is the <B>jugular foramen,</B> a large aperture, formed in front by the petrous portion of the temporal, and behind by the occipital; it is generally larger on the right than on the left side, and may be subdivided into three compartments. The anterior compartment transmits the inferior petrosal sinus; the intermediate, the glossopharyngeal, vagus, and accessory nerves; the posterior, the transverse sinus and some meningeal branches from the occipital and ascending pharyngeal arteries. On the ridge of bone dividing the carotid canal from the jugular foramen is the <B>inferior tympanic canaliculus</B> for the transmission of the tympanic branch of the glossopharyngeal nerve; and on the wall of the jugular foramen, near the root of the styloid process, is the <B>mastoid canaliculus</B> for the passage of the auricular branch of the vagus nerve. Extending forward from the jugular foramen to the foramen lacerum is the <B>petro&#246;ccipital fissure</B> occupied, in the fresh state, by a plate of cartilage. Behind the basilar portion of the occipital bone is the <B>foramen magnum,</B> bounded laterally by the occipital condyles, the medial sides of which are rough for the attachment of the alar ligaments. Lateral to each condyle is the <B>jugular process</B> which gives attachment to the Rectus capitis lateralis muscle and the lateral atlanto&#246;ccipital ligament. The foramen magnum transmits the medulla oblongata and its membranes, the accessory nerves, the vertebral arteries, the anterior and posterior spinal arteries, and the ligaments connecting the occipital bone with the axis. The mid-points on the anterior and posterior margins of the foramen magnum are respectively termed the <B>basion</B> and the <B>opisthion.</B> In front of each condyle is the canal for the passage of the hypoglossal nerve and a meningeal artery. Behind each condyle is the <B>condyloid fossa,</B> perforated on one or both sides by the condyloid canal, for the transmission of a vein from the transverse sinus. Behind the foramen magnum <!-- PAGE NUM="182" -->is the <B>median nuchal line</B> ending above at the <B>external occipital protuberance,</B> while on either side are the <B>superior</B> and <B>inferior nuchal lines;</B> these, as well as the surfaces of bone between them, are rough for the attachment of the muscles which are enumerated on pages 129 and 130.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus188.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=231 SRC="/107/Images/small/image188.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i188"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>188&#150; </FONT>Side view of the skull. (<A HREF="/107/illus188.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Norma Lateralis <A HREF="/107/illus188.html">(Fig. 188</A>).</B>&#151;When viewed from the side the skull is seen to consist of the cranium above and behind, and of the face below and in front. The cranium is somewhat ovoid in shape, but its contour varies in different cases and depends largely on the length and height of the skull and on the degree of prominence of the superciliary arches and frontal eminences. Entering into its formation are the frontal, the parietal, the occipital, the temporal, and the great wing of the sphenoid. These bones are joined to one another and to the zygomatic by the following <B>sutures:</B> the <B>zygomaticotemporal</B> between the zygomatic process of the temporal and the temporal process of the zygomatic; the <B>zygomaticofrontal</B> uniting the zygomatic bone with the zygomatic process of the frontal; the sutures surrounding the great wing of the sphenoid, viz., the <B>sphenozygomatic</B> in front, the <B>sphenofrontal</B> and <B>sphenoparietal</B> above, and the <B>sphenosquamosal</B> behind. The sphenoparietal suture varies in length in different skulls, and is absent in those cases where the frontal articulates with the temporal squama. The point corresponding with the posterior end of the sphenoparietal suture is named the <B>pterion;</B> it is situated about 3 cm. behind, and a little above the level of the zygomatic process of the frontal bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="183" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>squamosal suture</B> arches backward from the pterion and connects the temporal squama with the lower border of the parietal: this suture is continuous behind with the short, nearly horizontal <B>parietomastoid suture,</B> which unites the mastoid process of the temporal with the region of the mastoid angle of the parietal. Extending from above downward and forward across the cranium are the <B>coronal</B> and <B>lambdoidal sutures;</B> the former connects the parietals with the frontal, the latter, the parietals with the occipital. The lambdoidal suture is continuous below with the <B>occipitomastoid suture</B> between the occipital and the mastoid portion of the temporal. In or near the last suture is the <B>mastoid foramen,</B> for the transmission of an emissary vein. The point of meeting of the parietomastoid, occipitomastoid, and lambdoidal sutures is known as the <B>asterion.</B> Immediately above the orbital margin is the <B>superciliary arch,</B> and, at a higher level, the <B>frontal eminence.</B> Near the center of the parietal bone is the <B>parietal eminence.</B> Posteriorly is the <B>external occipital protuberance,</B> from which the superior nuchal line may be followed forward to the mastoid process. Arching across the side of the cranium are the <B>temporal lines,</B> which mark the upper limit of the temporal fossa.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Temporal Fossa (<I>fossa temporalis</I>).</B>&#151;The temporal fossa is bounded above and behind by the temporal lines, which extend from the zygomatic process of the frontal bone upward and backward across the frontal and parietal bones, and then curve downward and forward to become continuous with the supramastoid crest and the posterior root of the zygomatic arch. The point where the upper temporal line cuts the coronal suture is named the <B>stephanion.</B> The temporal fossa is bounded in <I>front</I> by the frontal and zygomatic bones, and opening on the back of the latter is the <B>zygomaticotemporal foramen.</B> <I>Laterally</I> the fossa is limited by the zygomatic arch, formed by the zygomatic and temporal bones; <I>below,</I> it is separated from the infratemporal fossa by the <B>infratemporal crest</B> on the great wing of the sphenoid, and by a ridge, continuous with this crest, which is carried backward across the temporal squama to the anterior root of the zygomatic process. In front and below, the fossa communicates with the orbital cavity through the <B>inferior orbital</B> or <B>sphenomaxillary fissure.</B> The floor of the fossa is deeply concave in front and convex behind, and is formed by the zygomatic, frontal, parietal, sphenoid, and temporal bones. It is traversed by vascular furrows; one, usually well-marked, runs upward above and in front of the external acoustic meatus, and lodges the middle temporal artery. Two others, frequently indistinct, may be observed on the anterior part of the floor, and are for the anterior and posterior deep temporal arteries. The temporal fossa contains the Temporalis muscle and its vessels and nerves, together with the zygomaticotemporal nerve.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>zygomatic arch</B> is formed by the zygomatic process of the temporal and the temporal process of the zygomatic, the two being united by an oblique suture; the tendon of the Temporalis passes medial to the arch to gain insertion into the coronoid process of the mandible. The zygomatic process of the temporal arises by two roots, an anterior, directed inward in front of the mandibular fossa, where it expands to form the articular tubercle, and a posterior, which runs backward above the external acoustic meatus and is continuous with the supramastoid crest. The upper border of the arch gives attachment to the temporal fascia; the lower border and medial surface give origin to the Masseter.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Below the posterior root of the zygomatic arch is the elliptical orifice of the <B>external acoustic meatus,</B> bounded in front, below, and behind by the tympanic part of the temporal bone; to its outer margin the cartilaginous segment of the external acoustic meatus is attached. The small triangular area between the posterior root of the zygomatic arch and the postero-superior part of the orifice is termed the <B>suprameatal triangle,</B> on the anterior border of which a small spinous process, the <B>suprameatal spine,</B> is sometimes seen. Between the tympanic part and the articular tubercle is the <B>mandibular fossa,</B> divided into two parts by the <!-- PAGE NUM="184" -->petrotympanic fissure. The anterior and larger part of the fossa articulates with the condyle of the mandible and is limited behind by the external acoustic meatus: the posterior part sometimes lodges a portion of the parotid gland. The styloid process extends downward and forward for a variable distance from the lower part of the tympanic part, and gives attachment to the Styloglossus, Stylohyoideus, and Stylopharyngeus, and to the stylohyoid and stylomandibular ligaments. Projecting downward behind the external acoustic meatus is the mastoid process, to the outer surface of which the Sternocleidomastoideus, Splenius capitis, and Longissimus capitis are attached.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus189.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=251 SRC="/107/Images/small/image189.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i189"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>189&#150; </FONT>Left infratemporal fossa. (<A HREF="/107/illus189.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Infratemporal Fossa (<I>fossa infratemporalis; zygomatic fossa</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus189.html">(Fig. 189</A>).</B>&#151;The infratemporal fossa is an irregularly shaped cavity, situated below and medial to the <B>zygomatic arch.</B> It is bounded, in <I>front,</I> by the infratemporal surface of the maxilla and the ridge which descends from its zygomatic process; <I>behind,</I> by the articular tubercle of the temporal and the spinal angularis of the sphenoid; <I>above,</I> by the great wing of the sphenoid below the infratemporal crest, and by the under surface of the temporal squama; <I>below,</I> by the alveolar border of the maxilla; <I>medially,</I> by the lateral pterygoid plate. It contains the lower part of the Temporalis, the Pterygoidei internus and externus, the internal maxillary vessels, and the mandibular and maxillary nerves. The <B>foramen ovale</B> and <B>foramen spinosum</B> open on its roof, and the <B>alveolar canals</B> on its anterior wall. At its upper and medial part are two fissures, which together form a T-shaped fissure, the horizontal limb being named the inferior orbital, and the vertical one the pterygomaxillary.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>inferior orbital fissure</B> (<I>fissura orbitalis inferior; sphenomaxillary fissure</I>), horizontal in direction, opens into the lateral and back part of the orbit. It is bounded <I>above</I> by the lower border of the orbital surface of the great wing of the <!-- PAGE NUM="185" -->sphenoid; <I>below,</I> by the lateral border of the orbital surface of the maxilla and the orbital process of the palatine bone; <I>laterally,</I> by a small part of the zygomatic bone:<A NAME="txt48">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/46.html#note48">48</A> <I>medially,</I> it joins at right angles with the pterygomaxillary fissure. Through the inferior orbital fissure the orbit communicates with the temporal, infratemporal, and pterygopalatine foss&#230;; the fissure transmits the maxillary nerve and its zygomatic branch, the infraorbital vessels, the ascending branches from the sphenopalatine ganglion, and a vein which connects the inferior ophthalmic vein with the pterygoid venous plexus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>pterygomaxillary fissure</B> is vertical, and descends at right angles from the medial end of the preceding; it is a triangular interval, formed by the divergence of the maxilla from the pterygoid process of the sphenoid. It connects the infratemporal with the pterygopalatine fossa, and transmits the terminal part of the internal maxillary artery.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Pterygopalatine Fossa (<I>fossa pterygopalatina; sphenomaxillary fossa</I>).</B>&#151;The pterygopalatine fossa is a small, triangular space at the angle of junction of the inferior orbital and pterygomaxillary fissures, and placed beneath the apex of the orbit. It is bounded <I>above</I> by the under surface of the body of the sphenoid and by the orbital process of the palatine bone; in <I>front,</I> by the infratemporal surface of the maxilla; <I>behind,</I> by the base of the pterygoid process and lower part of the anterior surface of the great wing of the sphenoid; <I>medially,</I> by the vertical part of the palatine bone with its orbital and sphenoidal processes. This fossa communicates with the orbit by the inferior orbital fissure, with the nasal cavity by the sphenopalatine foramen, and with the infratemporal fossa by the pterygomaxillary fissure. Five foramina open into it. Of these, three are on the posterior wall, viz., the <B>foramen rotundum,</B> the <B>pterygoid canal,</B> and the <B>pharyngeal canal,</B> in this order downward and medialward. On the medial wall is the <B>sphenopalatine foramen,</B> and below is the superior orifice of the <B>pterygopalatine canal.</B> The fossa contains the maxillary nerve, the sphenopalatine ganglion, and the terminal part of the internal maxillary artery.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Norma Occipitalis.</B>&#151;When viewed from behind the cranium presents a more or less circular outline. In the middle line is the posterior part of the <B>sagittal suture</B> connecting the parietal bones; extending downward and lateralward from the hinder end of the sagittal suture is the deeply serrated <B>lambdoidal suture</B> joining the parietals to the occipital and continuous below with the <B>parietomastoid</B> and <B>occipitomastoid sutures;</B> it frequently contains one or more sutural bones. Near the middle of the occipital squama is the <B>external occipital protuberance</B> or <B>inion,</B> and extending lateralward from it on either side is the superior nuchal line, and above this the faintly marked highest nuchal line. The part of the squama above the inion and highest lines is named the <B>planum occipitale,</B> and is covered by the Occipitalis muscle; the part below is termed the <B>planum nuchale,</B> and is divided by the median nuchal line which runs downward and forward from the inion to the foramen magnum; this ridge gives attachment to the ligamentum nuch&#230;. The muscles attached to the planum nuchale are enumerated on p. 130. Below and in front are the mastoid processes, convex laterally and grooved medially by the mastoid notches. In or near the occipitomastoid suture is the <B>mastoid foramen</B> for the passage of the mastoid emissary vein.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Norma Frontalis <A HREF="/107/illus190.html">(Fig. 190</A>).</B>&#151;When viewed from the front the skull exhibits a somewhat oval outline, limited <I>above</I> by the frontal bone, <I>below</I> by the body of the mandible, and <I>laterally</I> by the zygomatic bones and the mandibular rami. The upper part, formed by the frontal squama, is smooth and convex. The lower part, made up of the bones of the face, is irregular; it is excavated laterally by the orbital cavities, and presents in the middle line the <B>anterior nasal aperture</B> leading to the <!-- PAGE NUM="186" -->nasal cavities, and below this the transverse slit between the upper and lower dental arcades. <I>Above,</I> the <B>frontal eminences</B> stand out more or less prominently, and beneath these are the <B>superciliary arches,</B> joined to one another in the middle by the <B>glabella.</B> On and above the glabella a trace of the <B>frontal suture</B> sometimes persists; beneath it is the frontonasal suture, the mid-point of which is termed the <B>nasion.</B> Behind and below the frontonasal suture the frontal articulates with the frontal process of the maxilla and with the lacrimal. Arching transversely below the superciliary arches is the upper part of the margin of the orbit, thin and prominent in its lateral two-thirds, rounded in its medial third, and presenting, at the junction of these two portions, the <B>supraorbital notch</B> or <B>foramen</B> for the supraorbital nerve and vessels. The supraorbital margin ends laterally in the zygomatic process which articulates with the zygomatic bone, and from it the temporal line extends upward and backward. Below the frontonasal suture is the bridge of the nose, convex from side to side, concavo-convex from above downward, and formed by the two nasal bones supported in the middle line by the perpendicular plate of the ethmoid, and laterally by the frontal processes of the maxill&#230; which are prolonged upward between the nasal and lacrimal bones and form the lower and <!-- PAGE NUM="187" -->medial part of the circumference of each orbit. Below the nasal bones and between the maxill&#230; is the anterior aperture of the nose, pyriform in shape, with the narrow end directed upward. Laterally this opening is bounded by sharp margins, to which the lateral and alar cartilages of the nose are attached; <I>below,</I> the margins are thicker and curve medialward and forward to end in the <B>anterior nasal spine.</B> On looking into the nasal cavity, the bony septum which separates the nasal cavities presents, in front, a large triangular deficiency; this, in the fresh state, is filled up by the cartilage of the nasal septum; on the lateral wall of each nasal cavity the anterior part of the inferior nasal concha is visible. Below and lateral to the anterior nasal aperture are the anterior surfaces of the maxill&#230;, each perforated, near the lower margin of the orbit, by the <B>infraorbital foramen</B> for the passage of the infraorbital nerve and vessels. Below and medial to this foramen is the canine eminence separating the incisive from the canine fossa. Beneath these foss&#230; are the alveolar processes of the maxill&#230; containing the upper teeth, which overlap the teeth of the mandible in front. The zygomatic bone on either side forms the prominence of the cheek, the lower and lateral portion of the orbital cavity, and the anterior part of the zygomatic arch. It articulates medially with the maxilla, behind with the zygomatic process of the temporal, and above with the great wing of the sphenoid and the zygomatic process of the frontal; it is perforated by the <B>zygomaticofacial foramen</B> for the passage of the zygomaticofacial nerve. On the body of the mandible is a median ridge, indicating the position of the symphysis; this ridge divides below to enclose the mental protuberance, the lateral angles of which constitute the mental tubercles. Below the incisor teeth <!-- PAGE NUM="188" -->is the <B>incisive fossa,</B> and beneath the second premolar tooth the <B>mental foramen</B> which transmits the mental nerve and vessels. The oblique line runs upward from the mental tubercle and is continuous behind with the anterior border of the ramus. The posterior border of the ramus runs downward and forward from the condyle to the angle, which is frequently more or less everted.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus190.html"><IMG WIDTH=291 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image190.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i190"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>190&#150; </FONT>The skull from the front. (<A HREF="/107/illus190.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus191.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=232 SRC="/107/Images/small/image191.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i191"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>191&#150; </FONT>Horizontal section of nasal and orbital cavities. (<A HREF="/107/illus191.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Orbits (<I>orbit&#230;</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus190.html">(Fig. 190</A>).</B>&#151;The orbits are two quadrilateral pyramidal cavities, situated at the upper and anterior part of the face, their bases being directed forward and lateralward, and their apices backward and medialward, so that their long axes, if continued backward, would meet over the body of the sphenoid. Each presents for examination a <B>roof,</B> a <B>floor,</B> a <B>medial</B> and a <B>lateral wall,</B> a <B>base,</B> and an <B>apex.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus192.html"><IMG WIDTH=325 HEIGHT=233 SRC="/107/Images/small/image192.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i192"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>192&#150; </FONT>Medial wall of left orbit. (<A HREF="/107/illus192.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>roof</B> is concave, directed downward, and slightly forward, and formed in <I>front</I> by the orbital plate of the frontal; <I>behind</I> by the small wing of the sphenoid. It presents <I>medially</I> the <B>trochlear fovea</B> for the attachment of the cartilaginous pulley of the Obliquus oculi superior; <I>laterally,</I> the <B>lacrimal fossa</B> for the lacrimal gland; and <I>posteriorly,</I> the suture between the frontal bone and the small wing of the sphenoid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>floor</B> is directed upward and lateralward, and is of less extent than the roof; it is formed chiefly by the orbital surface of the maxilla; in <I>front</I> and <I>laterally,</I> by the orbital process of the zygomatic bone, and <I>behind</I> and <I>medially,</I> to a small extent, by the orbital process of the palatine. At its medial angle is the upper opening of the nasolacrimal canal, immediately to the lateral side of which is a depression for the origin of the Obliquus oculi inferior. On its lateral part is the suture between the maxilla and zygomatic bone, and at its posterior part that between the maxilla and the orbital process of the palatine. Running forward near the middle of the floor is the <B>infraorbital groove,</B> ending in front in the infraorbital canal and transmitting the infraorbital nerve and vessels.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="189" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial wall</B> <A HREF="/107/illus192.html">(Fig. 192)</A> is nearly vertical, and is formed from before backward by the frontal process of the maxilla, the lacrimal, the lamina papyracea of the ethmoid, and a small part of the body of the sphenoid in front of the optic foramen. Sometimes the sphenoidal concha forms a small part of this wall (see page 152). It exhibits three vertical sutures, viz., the lacrimomaxillary, lacrimoethmoidal, and sphenoethmoidal. In front is seen the <B>lacrimal groove,</B> which lodges the lacrimal sac, and behind the groove is the <B>posterior lacrimal crest,</B> from which the lacrimal part of the Orbicularis oculi arises. At the junction of the medial wall and the roof are the frontomaxillary, frontolacrimal, frontoethmoidal, and sphenofrontal sutures. The point of junction of the anterior border of the lacrimal with the frontal is named the <B>dacryon.</B> In the frontoethmoidal suture are the <B>anterior</B> and <B>posterior ethmoidal foramina,</B> the former transmitting the nasociliary nerve and anterior ethmoidal vessels, the latter the posterior ethmoidal nerve and vessels.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="19"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;19</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral wall,</B> directed medialward and forward, is formed by the orbital process of the zygomatic and the orbital surface of the great wing of the sphenoid; these are united by the sphenozygomatic suture which terminates below at the front end of the inferior orbital fissure. On the orbital process of the zygomatic bone are the orbital tubercle (Whitnall) and the orifices of one or two canals which transmit the branches of the zygomatic nerve. Between the roof and the lateral wall, near the apex of the orbit, is the <B>superior orbital fissure.</B> Through this fissure the oculomotor, the trochlear, the ophthalmic division of the trigeminal, and the abducent nerves enter the orbital cavity, also some filaments from the cavernous plexus of the sympathetic and the orbital branches of the middle meningeal artery. Passing backward through the fissure are the ophthalmic vein and the recurrent branch from the lacrimal artery to the dura mater. The lateral wall and the floor are separated posteriorly by the <B>inferior orbital fissure</B> which transmits the maxillary nerve and its zygomatic branch, the infraorbital vessels, and the ascending branches from the sphenopalatine ganglion.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="20"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;20</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>base</B> of the orbit, quadrilateral in shape, is formed <I>above</I> by the supraorbital arch of the frontal bone, in which is the <B>supraorbital notch</B> or <B>foramen</B> for the passage of the supraorbital vessels and nerve; <I>below</I> by the zygomatic bone and maxilla, united by the zygomaticomaxillary suture; laterally by the zygomatic bone and the zygomatic process of the frontal joined by the zygomaticofrontal suture; medially by the frontal bone and the frontal process of the maxilla united by the frontomaxillary suture.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="21"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;21</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>apex,</B> situated at the back of the orbit, corresponds to the optic foramen<A NAME="txt49">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/46.html#note49">49</A> a short, cylindrical canal, which transmits the optic nerve and ophthalmic artery.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="22"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;22</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;It will thus be seen that there are nine openings communicating with each orbit, viz., the optic foramen, superior and inferior orbital fissures, supraorbital foramen, infraorbital canal, anterior and posterior ethmoidal foramina, zygomatic foramen, and the canal for the nasolacrimal duct.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="23"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;23</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>5d. The Interior of the Skull</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Inner Surface of the Skull-cap.</B>&#151;The inner surface of the skull-cap is concave and presents depressions for the convolutions of the cerebrum, together with numerous furrows for the lodgement of branches of the meningeal vessels. Along the middle line is a longitudinal groove, narrow in front, where it commences at the frontal crest, but broader behind; it lodges the superior sagittal sinus, and its margins afford attachment to the falx cerebri. On either side of it are several <!-- PAGE NUM="190" -->depressions for the arachnoid granulations, and at its back part, the openings of the <B>parietal foramina</B> when these are present. It is crossed, in front, by the <B>coronal suture,</B> and behind by the <B>lambdoidal,</B> while the <B>sagittal</B> lies in the medial plane between the parietal bones.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Upper Surface of the Base of the Skull <A HREF="/107/illus193.html">(Fig. 193</A>).</B>&#151;The upper surface of the base of the skull or floor of the cranial cavity presents three foss&#230;, called the <B>anterior, middle,</B> and <B>posterior cranial foss&#230;.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Anterior Fossa (<I>fossa cranii anterior</I>).</B>&#151;The floor of the anterior fossa is formed by the orbital plates of the frontal, the cribriform plate of the ethmoid, and the small wings and front part of the body of the sphenoid; it is limited behind by the posterior borders of the small wings of the sphenoid and by the anterior margin of the chiasmatic groove. It is traversed by the <B>frontoethmoidal, sphenoethmoidal,</B> and <B>sphenofrontal sutures.</B> Its lateral portions roof in the orbital cavities and support the frontal lobes of the cerebrum; they are convex and marked by depressions for the brain convolutions, and grooves for branches of the meningeal vessels. The central portion corresponds with the roof of the nasal cavity, and is markedly depressed on either side of the crista galli. It presents, in and near the median line, from before backward, the commencement of the <B>frontal crest</B> for the attachment of the falx cerebri; the <B>foramen cecum,</B> between the frontal bone and the crista galli of the ethmoid, which usually transmits a small vein from the nasal cavity to the superior sagittal sinus; behind the foramen cecum, the <B>crista galli,</B> the free margin of which affords attachment to the falx cerebri; on either side of the crista galli, the <B>olfactory groove</B> formed by the cribriform plate, which supports the olfactory bulb and presents foramina for the transmission of the olfactory nerves, and in front a slit-like opening for the nasociliary nerve. Lateral to either olfactory groove are the internal openings of the <B>anterior</B> and <B>posterior ethmoidal foramina;</B> the anterior, situated about the middle of the lateral margin of the olfactory groove, transmits the anterior ethmoidal vessels and the nasociliary nerve; the nerve runs in a groove along the lateral edge of the cribriform plate to the slit-like opening above mentioned; the posterior ethmoidal foramen opens at the back part of this margin under cover of the projecting lamina of the sphenoid, and transmits the posterior ethmoidal vessels and nerve. Farther back in the middle line is the <B>ethmoidal spine,</B> bounded behind by a slight elevation separating two shallow longitudinal grooves which support the olfactory lobes. Behind this is the anterior margin of the chiasmatic groove, running lateralward on either side to the upper margin of the optic foramen.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Middle Fossa (<I>fossa cranii media</I>).</B>&#151;The middle fossa, deeper than the preceding, is narrow in the middle, and wide at the sides of the skull. It is bounded in <I>front</I> by the posterior margins of the small wings of the sphenoid, the anterior clinoid processes, and the ridge forming the anterior margin of the chiasmatic groove; <I>behind,</I> by the superior angles of the petrous portions of the temporals and the dorsum sell&#230;; <I>laterally</I> by the temporal squam&#230;, sphenoidal angles of the parietals, and great wings of the sphenoid. It is traversed by the <B>squamosal, sphenoparietal, sphenosquamosal,</B> and <B>sphenopetrosal sutures.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The middle part of the fossa presents, in <I>front,</I> the <B>chiasmatic groove</B> and <B>tuberculum sell&#230;;</B> the chiasmatic groove ends on either side at the <B>optic foramen,</B> which transmits the optic nerve and ophthalmic artery to the orbital cavity. Behind the optic foramen the <B>anterior clinoid process</B> is directed backward and medialward and gives attachment to the tentorium cerebelli. Behind the tuberculum sell&#230; is a deep depression, the <B>sella turcica,</B> containing the <B>fossa hypophyseos,</B> which lodges the hypophysis, and presents on its anterior wall the <B>middle clinoid processes.</B> The sella turcica is bounded posteriorly by a quadrilateral plate of bone, the <B>dorsum sell&#230;,</B> the upper angles of which are surmounted by the <B>posterior clinoid processes:</B> these afford attachment to the tentorium cerebelli, and below each is a notch for <!-- PAGE NUM="191" -->the abducent nerve. On either side of the sella turcica is the <B>carotid groove,</B> which is broad, shallow, and curved somewhat like the italic letter <I>f.</I> It begins behind at the foramen lacerum, and ends on the medial side of the anterior clinoid process, where it is sometimes converted into a foramen (<I>carotico-clinoid</I>) by the union of the anterior with the middle clinoid process; posteriorly, it is bounded laterally <!-- PAGE NUM="192" -->by the <B>lingula.</B> This groove lodges the cavernous sinus and the internal carotid artery, the latter being surrounded by a plexus of sympathetic nerves.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus193.html"><IMG WIDTH=359 HEIGHT=528 SRC="/107/Images/small/image193.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i193"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>193&#150; </FONT>Base of the skull. Upper surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus193.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The lateral parts of the middle fossa are of considerable depth, and support the temporal lobes of the brain. They are marked by depressions for the brain convolutions and traversed by furrows for the anterior and posterior branches of the middle meningeal vessels. These furrows begin near the foramen spinosum, and the anterior runs forward and upward to the sphenoidal angle of the parietal, where it is sometimes converted into a bony canal; the posterior runs lateralward and backward across the temporal squama and passes on to the parietal near the middle of its lower border. The following apertures are also to be seen. In front is the <B>superior orbital fissure,</B> bounded above by the small wing, below, by the great wing, and medially, by the body of the sphenoid; it is usually completed laterally by the orbital plate of the frontal bone. It transmits to the orbital cavity the oculomotor, the trochlear, the ophthalmic division of the trigeminal, and the abducent nerves, some filaments from the cavernous plexus of the sympathetic, and the orbital branch of the middle meningeal artery; and from the orbital cavity a recurrent branch from the lacrimal artery to the dura mater, and the ophthalmic veins. Behind the medial end of the superior orbital fissure is the <B>foramen rotundum,</B> for the passage of the maxillary nerve. Behind and lateral to the foramen rotundum is the <B>foramen ovale,</B> which transmits the mandibular nerve, the accessory meningeal artery, and the lesser superficial petrosal nerve.<A NAME="txt50">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/47.html#note50">50</A> Medial to the foramen ovale is the <B>foramen Vesalii,</B> which varies in size in different individuals, and is often absent; when present, it opens below at the lateral side of the scaphoid fossa, and transmits a small vein. Lateral to the foramen ovale is the <B>foramen spinosum,</B> for the passage of the middle meningeal vessels, and a recurrent branch from the mandibular nerve. Medial to the foramen ovale is the <B>foramen lacerum;</B> in the fresh state the lower part of this aperture is filled up by a layer of fibrocartilage, while its upper and inner parts transmit the internal carotid artery surrounded by a plexus of sympathetic nerves. The nerve of the pterygoid canal and a meningeal branch from the ascending pharyngeal artery pierce the layer of fibrocartilage. On the anterior surface of the petrous portion of the temporal bone are seen the eminence caused by the projection of the superior semicircular canal; in front of and a little lateral to this a depression corresponding to the roof of the tympanic cavity; the groove leading to the <B>hiatus of the facial canal,</B> for the transmission of the greater superficial petrosal nerve and the petrosal branch of the middle meningeal artery; beneath it, the smaller groove, for the passage of the lesser superficial petrosal nerve; and, near the apex of the bone, the depression for the semilunar ganglion and the orifice of the carotid canal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Posterior Fossa (<I>fossa cranii posterior</I>).</B>&#151;The posterior fossa is the largest and deepest of the three. It is formed by the dorsum sell&#230; and clivus of the sphenoid, the occipital, the petrous and mastoid portions of the temporals, and the mastoid angles of the parietal bones; it is crossed by the <B>occipitomastoid</B> and the <B>parietomastoid sutures,</B> and lodges the cerebellum, pons, and medulla oblongata. It is separated from the middle fossa in and near the median line by the dorsum sell&#230; of the sphenoid and on either side by the superior angle of the petrous portion of the temporal bone. This angle gives attachment to the tentorum cerebelli, is grooved for the superior petrosal sinus, and presents at its medial end a notch upon which the trigeminal nerve rests. The fossa is limited behind by the grooves for the transverse sinuses. In its center is the <B>foramen magnum,</B> on either side of which is a rough tubercle for the attachment of the alar ligaments; a little above this tubercle is the canal, which transmits the hypoglossal nerve and a meningeal branch from the ascending pharyngeal artery. In front of the foramen magnum <!-- PAGE NUM="193" -->the basilar portion of the occipital and the posterior part of the body of the sphenoid form a grooved surface which supports the medulla oblongata and pons; in the young skull these bones are joined by a synchondrosis. This grooved surface is separated on either side from the petrous portion of the temporal by the <B>petro-occipital fissure,</B> which is occupied in the fresh state by a plate of cartilage; the fissure is continuous behind with the jugular foramen, and its margins are grooved for the inferior petrosal sinus. The <B>jugular foramen</B> is situated between the lateral part of the occipital and the petrous part of the temporal. The anterior portion of this foramen transmits the inferior petrosal sinus; the posterior portion, the transverse sinus and some meningeal branches from the occipital and ascending pharyngeal arteries; and the intermediate portion, the glossopharyngeal, vagus, and accessory nerves. Above the jugular foramen is the <B>internal acoustic meatus,</B> for the facial and acoustic nerves and internal auditory artery; behind and lateral to this is the slit-like opening leading into the aqu&#230;ductus vestibuli, which lodges the ductus endolymphaticus; while between these, and near the superior angle of the petrous portion, is a small triangular depression, the remains of the fossa subarcuata, which lodges a process of the dura mater and occasionally transmits a small vein. Behind the foramen magnum are the <B>inferior occipital foss&#230;,</B> which support the hemispheres of the cerebellum, separated from one another by the <B>internal occipital crest,</B> which serves for the attachment of the falx cerebelli, and lodges the occipital sinus. The posterior foss&#230; are surmounted by the deep grooves for the <B>transverse sinuses.</B> Each of these channels, in its passage to the jugular foramen, grooves the occipital, the mastoid angle of the parietal, the mastoid portion of the temporal, and the jugular process of the occipital, and ends at the back part of the jugular foramen. Where this sinus grooves the mastoid portion of the temporal, the orifice of the <B>mastoid foramen</B> may be seen; and, just previous to its termination, the <B>condyloid canal</B> opens into it; neither opening is constant.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus194.html"><IMG WIDTH=325 HEIGHT=210 SRC="/107/Images/small/image194.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i194"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>194&#150; </FONT>Sagittal section of skull. (<A HREF="/107/illus194.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="194" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Nasal Cavity (<I>cavum nasi; nasal fossa</I>).</B>&#151;The nasal cavities are two irregular spaces, situated one on either side of the middle line of the face, extending from the base of the cranium to the roof of the mouth, and separated from each other by a thin vertical septum. They open on the face through the pear-shaped <B>anterior nasal aperture,</B> and their posterior openings or <B>choan&#230;</B> communicate, in the fresh state, with the nasal part of the pharynx. They are much narrower above than below, and in the middle than at their anterior or posterior openings: their depth, which is considerable, is greatest in the middle. They communicate with the frontal, ethmoidal, sphenoidal, and maxillary sinuses. Each cavity is bounded by a <B>roof,</B> a <B>floor,</B> a <B>medial</B> and a <B>lateral wall.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>roof</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus195.html">Figs. 195,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus196.html">196</A>) is horizontal in its central part, but slopes downward in front and behind; it is formed in <I>front</I> by the nasal bone and the spine of the frontal; in the <I>middle,</I> by the cribriform plate of the ethmoid; and <I>behind,</I> by the body of the sphenoid, the sphenoidal concha, the ala of the vomer and the sphenoidal process of the palatine bone. In the cribriform plate of the ethmoid are the foramina for the olfactory nerves, and on the posterior part of the roof is the opening into the sphenoidal sinus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus195.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=216 SRC="/107/Images/small/image195.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i195"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>195&#150; </FONT>Medial wall of left nasal fossa. (<A HREF="/107/illus195.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>floor</B> is flattened from before backward and concave from side to side. It is formed by the palatine process of the maxilla and the horizontal part of the palatine bone; near its anterior end is the opening of the incisive canal.</</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
