</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus196.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=214 SRC="/107/Images/small/image196.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i196"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>196&#150; </FONT>Roof, floor, and lateral wall of left nasal cavity. (<A HREF="/107/illus196.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial wall</B> (<I>septum nasi</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus195.html">(Fig. 195),</A> is frequently deflected to one or other side, more often to the left than to the right. It is formed, in <I>front,</I> by the crest of the nasal bones and frontal spine; in the <I>middle,</I> by the perpendicular plate of the ethmoid; <I>behind,</I> by the vomer and the rostrum of the sphenoid; <I>below,</I> by the crest of the maxill&#230; and palatine bones. It presents, in front, a large, triangular notch, which receives the cartilage of the septum; and behind, the free edge of the vomer. Its surface is marked by numerous furrows for vessels <!-- PAGE NUM="195" -->and nerves and by the grooves for the nasopalatine nerve, and is traversed by sutures connecting the bones of which it is formed.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral wall</B> <A HREF="/107/illus196.html">(Fig. 196)</A> is formed, in front, by the frontal process of the maxilla and by the lacrimal bone; in the middle, by the ethmoid, maxilla, and inferior nasal concha; behind, by the vertical plate of the palatine bone, and the medial pterygoid plate of the sphenoid. On this wall are three irregular anteroposterior passages, termed the superior, middle, and inferior meatuses of the nose. The <B>superior meatus,</B> the smallest of the three, occupies the middle third of the lateral wall. It lies between the superior and middle nasal conch&#230;; the <B>sphenopalatine foramen</B> opens into it behind, and the <B>posterior ethmoidal cells</B> in front. The sphenoidal sinus opens into a recess, the <B>sphenoethmoidal recess,</B> which is placed above and behind the superior concha. The <B>middle meatus</B> is situated between the middle and inferior conch&#230;, and extends from the anterior to the posterior end of the latter. The lateral wall of this meatus can be satisfactorily studied only after the removal of the middle concha. On it is a curved fissure, the <B>hiatus semilunaris,</B> limited below by the edge of the uncinate process of the ethmoid and above by an elevation named the <B>bulla ethmoidalis;</B> the middle ethmoidal cells are contained within this bulla and open on or near to it. Through the hiatus semilunaris the meatus communicates with a curved passage termed the <B>infundibulum,</B> which communicates in front with the anterior ethmoidal cells and in rather more than fifty per cent. of skulls is continued upward as the <B>frontonasal duct</B> into the frontal air-sinus; when this continuity fails, the frontonasal duct opens directly into the anterior part of the meatus. Below the bulla ethmoidalis and hidden by the uncinate process of the ethmoid is the opening of the maxillary sinus (<B>ostium maxillare</B>); an accessory opening is frequently present above the posterior part of the inferior nasal concha. The <B>inferior meatus,</B> the largest of the three, is the space between the inferior concha and the floor of the nasal cavity. It extends almost the entire <!-- PAGE NUM="196" -->length of the lateral wall of the nose, is broader in front than behind, and presents anteriorly the lower <B>orifice of the nasolacrimal canal.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Anterior Nasal Aperture</B> <A HREF="/107/illus181.html">(Fig. 181)</A> is a heart-shaped or pyriform opening, whose long axis is vertical, and narrow end upward; in the recent state it is much contracted by the lateral and alar cartilages of the nose. It is bounded <I>above</I> by the inferior borders of the nasal bones; <I>laterally</I> by the thin, sharp margins which separate the anterior from the nasal surfaces of the maxill&#230;; and <I>below</I> by the same borders, where they curve medialward to join each other at the anterior nasal spine.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>choan&#230;</B> are each bounded <I>above</I> by the under surface of the body of the sphenoid and ala of the vomer; <I>below,</I> by the posterior border of the horizontal part of the palatine bone; <I>laterally,</I> by the medial pterygoid plate; they are separated from each other by the posterior border of the vomer.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Differences in the Skull Due to Age</B><B>At birth</B> the skull is large in proportion to the other parts of the skeleton, but its facial portion is small, and equals only about one-eighth of the bulk of the cranium as compared with one-half in the adult. The frontal and parietal eminences are prominent, and the greatest width of the skull is at the level of the latter; on the other hand, the glabella, superciliary arches, and mastoid processes are not developed. Ossification of the skull bones is not completed, and many of them, <I>e. g.,</I> the occipital, temporals, sphenoid, frontal, and mandible, consist of more than one piece. Unossified membranous intervals, termed <I>fontanelles,</I> are seen at the angles of the parietal bones; these fontanelles are six in number: two, an anterior and a posterior, are situated in the middle line, and two, an antero-lateral and a postero-lateral, on either side.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>anterior</I> or <I>bregmatic fontanelle</I> <A HREF="/107/illus197.html">(Fig. 197)</A> is the largest, and is placed at the junction of the sagittal, coronal, and frontal sutures; it is lozenge-shaped, and measures about 4 cm. in its antero-posterior and 2.5 cm. in its transverse diameter. The <I>posterior fontanelle</I> is triangular in form and is situated at the junction of the sagittal and lambdoidal sutures. The <I>lateral fontanelles</I> <A HREF="/107/illus198.html">(Fig. 198)</A> are small, irregular in shape, and correspond respectively with the sphenoidal and mastoid angles of the parietal bones. An additional fontanelle is sometimes seen in the sagittal suture at the region of the obelion. The fontanelles are usually closed by the growth and extension of the bones which surround them, but sometimes they are the sites of separate ossific centers which develop into sutural bones. The posterior and lateral fontanelles are obliterated within a month or two after birth, but the anterior is not completely closed until about the middle of the second year.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus197.html"><IMG WIDTH=175 HEIGHT=238 SRC="/107/Images/small/image197.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i197"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>197&#150; </FONT>Skull at birth, showing frontal and occipital fonticuli. (<A HREF="/107/illus197.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The smallness of the face at birth is mainly accounted for by the rudimentary condition of the maxill&#230; and mandible, the non-eruption of the teeth, and the small size of the maxillary air sinuses and nasal cavities. At birth the nasal cavities lie almost entirely between the orbits, and the lower border of the anterior nasal aperture is only a little below the level of the orbital floor. With the eruption of the deciduous teeth there is an enlargement of the face and jaws, and these changes are still more marked after the second dentition.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The skull grows rapidly from birth to the seventh year, by which time the foramen magnum and petrous parts of the temporals have reached their full size and the orbital cavities are only a little smaller than those of the adult. Growth is slow from the seventh year until the approach <!-- PAGE NUM="197" -->of puberty, when a second period of activity occurs: this results in an increase in all directions, but it is especially marked in the frontal and facial regions, where it is associated with the development of the air sinuses.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Obliteration of the sutures of the vault of the skull takes place as age advances. This process may commence between the ages of thirty and forty, and is first seen on the inner surface, and some ten years later on the outer surface of the skull. The dates given are, however, only approximate, as it is impossible to state with anything like accuracy the time at which the sutures are closed. Obliteration usually occurs first in the posterior part of the sagittal suture, next in the coronal, and then in the lambdoidal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="19"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;19</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In old age the skull generally becomes thinner and lighter, but in a small proportion of cases it increases in thickness and weight, owing to an hypertrophy of the inner table. The most striking feature of the old skull is the diminution in the size of the maxill&#230; and mandible consequent on the loss of the teeth and the absorption of the alveolar processes. This is associated with a marked reduction in the vertical measurement of the face and with an alteration in the angles of the mandible.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="20"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;20</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus198.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=177 SRC="/107/Images/small/image198.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i198"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>198&#150; </FONT>Skull at birth, showing sphenoidal and mastoid fonticuli. (<A HREF="/107/illus198.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Sexual Differences in the Skull</B>Until the age of puberty there is little difference between the skull of the female and that of the male. The skull of an adult female is, as a rule, lighter and smaller, and its cranial capacity about 10 per cent. less, than that of the male. Its walls are thinner and its muscular ridges less strongly marked; the glabella, superciliary arches, and mastoid processes are less prominent, and the corresponding air sinuses are small or rudimentary. The upper margin of the orbit is sharp, the forehead vertical, the frontal and parietal eminences prominent, and the vault somewhat flattened. The contour of the face is more rounded, the facial bones are smoother, and the maxill&#230; and mandible and their contained teeth smaller. From what has been said it will be seen that more of the infantile characteristics are retained in the skull of the adult female than in that of the adult male. A well-marked male or female skull can easily be recognized as such, but in some cases the respective characteristics are so indistinct that the determination of the sex may be difficult or impossible.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="21"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;21</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Craniology</B>Skulls vary in size and shape, and the term craniology is applied to the study of these variations. The capacity of the cranial cavity constitutes a good index of the size of the brain which it contained, and is most conveniently arrived at by filling the cavity with shot and measuring the contents in a graduated vessel. Skulls may be classified according to their capacities as follows:</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="22"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;22</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;1. <I>Microcephalic,</I> with a capacity of less than 1350 c.cm.&#151;<I>e.g.,</I> those of native Australians and Andaman Islanders.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="23"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;23</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;2. <I>Mesocephalic,</I> with a capacity of from 1350 c.cm. to 1450 c.cm.&#151;<I>e.g.,</I> those of African negroes and Chinese.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="24"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;24</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;3. <I>Megacephalic,</I> with a capacity of over 1450 c.cm.&#151;<I>e.g.,</I> those of Europeans, Japanese, and Eskimos.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="25"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;25</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="198" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In comparing the shape of one skull with that of another it is necessary to adopt some definite position in which the skulls should be placed during the process of examination. They should be so placed that a line carried through the lower margin of the orbit and upper margin of the external acoustic meatus is in the horizontal plane. The norm&#230; of one skull can then be compared with those of another, and the differences in contour and surface form noted. Further, it is necessary that the various linear measurements used to determine the shape of the skull should be made between definite and easily localized points on its surface. The principal points may be divided into two groups: (1) those in the median plane, and (2) those on either side of it.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="26"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;26</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<B>The Points in the Median Plane</B> are the:</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="27"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;27</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Mental Point.</I> The most prominent point of the chin.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="28"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;28</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Alveolar Point</I> or <I>Prosthion.</I> The central point of the anterior margin of the upper alveolar arch.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="29"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;29</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Subnasal Point.</I> The middle of the lower border of the anterior nasal aperture, at the base of the anterior nasal spine.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="30"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;30</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Nasion.</I> The central point of the frontonasal suture.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="31"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;31</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Glabella.</I> The point in the middle line at the level of the superciliary arches.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="32"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;32</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Ophryon.</I> The point in the middle line of the forehead at the level where the temporal lines most nearly approach each other.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="33"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;33</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Bregma.</I> The meeting point of the coronal and sagittal sutures.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="34"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;34</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Obelion.</I> A point in the sagittal suture on a level with the parietal foramina.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="35"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;35</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Lambda.</I> The point of junction of the sagittal and lambdoidal sutures.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="36"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;36</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Occipital Point.</I> The point in the middle line of the occipital bone farthest from the glabella.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="37"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;37</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Inion.</I> The external occipital protuberance.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="38"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;38</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Opisthion.</I> The mid-point of the posterior margin of the foramen magnum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="39"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;39</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Basion.</I> The mid-point of the anterior margin of the foramen magnum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="40"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;40</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<B>The Points on Either Side of the Median Plane</B> are the:</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="41"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;41</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Gonion.</I> The outer margin of the angle of the mandible.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="42"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;42</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Dacryon.</I> The point of union of the antero-superior angle of the lacrimal with the frontal bone and the frontal process of the maxilla.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="43"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;43</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Stephanion.</I> The point where the temporal line intersects the coronal suture.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="44"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;44</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Pterion.</I> The point where the great wing of the sphenoid joins the sphenoidal angle of the parietal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="45"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;45</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Auricular Point.</I> The center of the orifice of the external acoustic meatus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="46"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;46</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Asterion.</I> The point of meeting of the lambdoidal, masto&#246;ccipital, and mastoparietal sutures.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="47"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;47</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>horizontal circumference</I> of the cranium is measured in a plane passing through the glabella (Turner) or the ophryon (Flower) in front, and the occipital point behind; it averages about 50 cm. in the female and 52.5 cm. in the male.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="48"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;48</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>occipitofrontal</I> or <I>longitudinal arc</I> is measured from the nasion over the middle line of the vertex to the opisthion: while the <I>basinasal length</I> is the distance between the basion and the nasion. These two measurements, plus the antero-posterior diameter of the foramen magnum, represent the <I>vertical circumference</I> of the cranium.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="49"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;49</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>length</I> is measured from the glabella to the occipital point, while the <I>breadth</I> or greatest transverse diameter is usually found near the external acoustic meatus. The proportion of breadth to length (breadth X 100)/length is termed the <I>cephalic index</I> or <I>index of breadth.</I></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="50"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;50</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>height</I> is usually measured from the basion to the bregma, and the proportion of height to length (height X 100)/length constitutes the <I>vertical</I> or <I>height index.</I></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="51"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;51</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In studying the face the principal points to be noticed are the proportion of its length and breadth, the shape of the orbits and of the anterior nasal aperture, and the degree of projection of the jaws.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="52"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;52</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>length of the face</I> may be measured from the ophryon or nasion to the chin, or, if the mandible be wanting, to the alveolar point; while its <I>width</I> is represented by the distance between the zygomatic arches. By comparing the length with the width of the face, skulls may be divided into two groups; <I>dolichofacial</I> or <I>leptoprosope</I> (long faced) and <I>brachyfacial</I> or <I>chemoprosope</I> (short faced).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="53"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;53</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>orbital index</I> signifies the proportion which the orbital height bears to the orbital width, thus:

<TABLE ALIGN="CENTER" WIDTH="85%" CELLSPACING="0"><TR><TD>orbital height X 100/orbital width</TD></TR></TABLE></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="54"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;54</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <I>nasal index</I> expresses the proportion which the width of the anterior nasal aperture bears to the height of the nose, the latter being measured from the nasion to the lower margin of the nasal aperture, thus:<TABLE ALIGN="CENTER" WIDTH="85%" CELLSPACING="0"><TR><TD>nasal width X 100/nasal height</TD></TR></TABLE></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="55"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;55</I></A></FONT></TD></TR> <Page Num="199">

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The degree of projection of the jaws is determined by the <I>gnathic</I> or <I>alveolar index,</I> which represents the proportion between the basialveolar and basinasal lengths, thus:<TABLE ALIGN="CENTER" WIDTH="85%" CELLSPACING="0"><TR><TD>basialveolar length X 100/basinasal length</TD></TR></TABLE></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="56"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;56</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The following table, modified from that given by Duckworth,<A NAME="txt51">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/47.html#note51">51</A> illustrates how these different indices may be utilized in the classification of skulls:</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="57"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;57</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The chief function of the skull is to protect the brain, and therefore those portions of the skull which are most exposed to external violence are thicker than those which are shielded from injury by overlying muscles. Thus, the skull-cap is thick and dense, whereas the temporal squam&#230; being protected by the temporales muscles, and the inferior occipital foss&#230;, being shielded by the muscles at the back of the neck, are thin and fragile. Fracture of the skull is further prevented by its elasticity, its rounded shape, and its construction of a number of secondary elastic arches, each made up of a single bone. The manner in which vibrations are transmitted through the bones of the skull is also of importance as regards its protective mechanism, at all events as far as the base is concerned. In the vault, the bones being of a fairly equal thickness and density, vibrations are transmitted in a uniform manner in all directions, but in the base, owing to the varying thickness and density of the bones, this is not so; and therefore in this situation there are special buttresses which serve to carry the vibrations in certain definite directions. At the front of the skull, on either side, is the ridge which separates the anterior from the middle fossa of the base; and behind, the ridge or buttress which separates the middle from the posterior fossa; and if any violence is applied to the vault, the vibrations would be carried along these buttresses to the sella turcica, where they meet. This part has been termed the &#147;center of resistance,&#148; and here there is a special protective mechanism to guard the brain. The subarachnoid cavity at the base of the brain is dilated, and the cerebrospinal fluid which fills it acts as a water cushion to shield the brain from injury. In like manner, when violence is applied to the base of the skull, as in falls upon the feet, the vibrations are carried backward through the occipital crest, and forward through the basilar part of the occipital and body of the sphenoid to the vault of the skull.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="58"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;58</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus199.html"><IMG WIDTH=141 HEIGHT=84 SRC="/107/Images/small/image199.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i199"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>199&#150; </FONT>The premaxilla and its sutures. (After Albrecht.) (<A HREF="/107/illus199.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In connection with the bones of the face a common malformation is <I>cleft palate.</I> The cleft usually starts posteriorly, and its most elementary form is a bifid uvula; or the cleft may extend through the soft palate; or the posterior part of the whole of the hard palate may be involved, the cleft extending as far forward as the incisive foramen. In the severest forms, the cleft extends through the alveolus and passes between the incisive or premaxillary bone and the rest of the maxilla; that is to say, between the lateral incisor and canine teeth. In some instances, the cleft runs between the central and lateral incisor teeth; and this has induced some anatomists to believe that the premaxillary bone is developed from two centers <A HREF="/107/illus199.html">(Fig. 199)</A> and not from one, as was stated on p. 163. The medial segment, bearing a central incisor, is called an <I>endognathion;</I> the lateral segment, bearing the lateral incisor, is called a <I>mesognathion.</I> The cleft may affect one or both sides; if the latter, the central part is frequently displaced forward and remains united to the septum of the nose, the deficiency in the alveolus being complicated with a cleft <!-- PAGE NUM="200" -->in the lip (hare-lip). On examining a cleft palate in which the alveolus is not implicated, the cleft will generally appear to be in the median line, but occasionally is unilateral and in some cases bilateral. To understand this it must be borne in mind that three processes are concerned in the formation of the palate&#151;the palatine processes of the two maxill&#230;, which grow in horizontally and unite in the middle line, and the ethmovomerine process, which grows downward from the base of the skull and frontonasal process to unite with the palatine processes in the middle line. In those cases where the palatine processes fail to unite with each other and with the medial process, the cleft of the palate is median; where one palatine process unites with the medial septum, the other failing to do so, the cleft in the palate is unilateral. In some cases where the palatine processes fail to meet in the middle, the ethmovomerine process grows downward between them and thus produces a bilateral cleft. Occasionally there may be a hole in the middle line of the hard palate, the anterior part of the hard and the soft palate being perfect; this is rare, because, as a rule, the union of the various processes progresses from before backward, and therefore the posterior part of the palate is more frequently defective than the anterior.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="59"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;59</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6. The Extremities</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The bones by which the upper and lower limbs are attached to the trunk constitute respectively the shoulder and pelvic girdles. The <B>shoulder girdle</B> or <B>girdle of the superior extremity</B> is formed by the scapul&#230; and clavicles, and is imperfect in front and behind. In front, however, it is completed by the upper end of the sternum, with which the medial ends of the clavicles articulate. Behind, it is widely imperfect, the scapul&#230; being connected to the trunk by muscles only. The <B>pelvic girdle</B> or <B>girdle of the inferior extremity</B> is formed by the hip bones, which articulate with each other in front, at the symphysis pubis. It is imperfect behind, but the gap is filled in by the upper part of the sacrum. The pelvic girdle, with the sacrum, is a complete ring, massive and comparatively rigid, in marked contrast to the lightness and mobility of the shoulder girdle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6a. The Bones of the Upper Extremity. 1. <A HREF="/sv/062001.html">The Clavicle</A></B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Ossa Extremitatis Superioris) &amp; (Clavicula; Collar Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B><A HREF="/sv/062001.html">clavicle</A></B> (<A HREF="/107/illus200.html">Figs. 200,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus201.html">201</A>) forms the anterior portion of the shoulder girdle. It is a long bone, curved somewhat like the italic letter <I>f,</I> and placed nearly horizontally at the upper and anterior part of the thorax, immediately above the first rib. It articulates medially with the manubrium sterni, and laterally with the acromion of the scapula.
<A NAME="txt52">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/49.html#note52">52</A> It presents a double curvature, the convexity being directed forward at the sternal end, and the concavity at the scapular end. Its lateral third is flattened from above downward, while its medial two-thirds is of a rounded or prismatic form.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Lateral Third.</B>&#151;The lateral third has two surfaces, an upper and a lower; and two borders, an anterior and a posterior.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surface.</B>&#151;The <B>upper surface</B> is flat, rough, and marked by impressions for the attachments of the Deltoideus in front, and the Trapezius behind; between these impressions a small portion of the bone is subcutaneous. The <B>under surface</B> is flat. At its posterior border, near the point where the prismatic joins with the flattened portion, is a rough eminence, the <B>coracoid tuberosity</B> (<I>conoid tubercle</I>); this, in the natural position of the bone, surmounts the coracoid process of the scapula, and gives attachment to the conoid ligament. From this tuberosity an oblique ridge, the <B>oblique</B> or <B>trapezoid ridge,</B> runs forward and lateralward, and afford attachment to the trapezoid ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="201" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior border</B> is concave, thin, and rough, and gives attachment to the Deltoideus. The <B>posterior border</B> is convex, rough, thicker than the anterior, and gives attachment to the Trapezius.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Medial Two-thirds.</B>&#151;The medial two-thirds constitute the prismatic portion of the bone, which is curved so as to be convex in front, concave behind, and is marked by three borders, separating three surfaces.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus200.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=94 SRC="/107/Images/small/image200.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i200"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>200&#150; </FONT>Left clavicle. Superior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus200.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior border</B> is continuous with the anterior margin of the flat portion. Its lateral part is smooth, and corresponds to the interval between the attachments of the Pectoralis major and Deltoideus; its medial part forms the lower boundary of an elliptical surface for the attachment of the clavicular portion of the Pectoralis major, and approaches the posterior border of the bone. The <B>superior border</B> is continuous with the posterior margin of the flat portion, and separates the anterior from the posterior surface. Smooth and rounded laterally, it becomes rough toward the medial third for the attachment of the Sternocleidomastoideus, and ends at the upper angle of the sternal extremity. The <B>posterior</B> or <B>subclavian border</B> separates the posterior from the inferior surface, and extends from the coracoid tuberosity to the costal tuberosity; it forms the posterior boundary of the groove for the Subclavius, and gives attachment to a layer of cervical fascia which envelops the Omohyoideus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus201.html"><IMG WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=102 SRC="/107/Images/small/image201.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i201"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>201&#150; </FONT>Left clavicle. Inferior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus201.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface</B> is included between the superior and anterior borders. Its lateral part looks upward, and is continuous with the superior surface of the flattened portion; it is smooth, convex, and nearly subcutaneous, being covered only by the Platysma. Medially it is divided by a narrow subcutaneous area into two parts: a lower, elliptical in form, and directed forward, for the attachment of the Pectoralis major; and an upper for the attachment of the Sternocleidomastoideus. The <B>posterior</B> or <B>cervical surface</B> is smooth, and looks backward toward the root of the neck. It is limited, above, by the superior border; below, by the subclavian border; medially, by the margin of the sternal extremity; and laterally, by the coracoid tuberosity. It is concave medio-laterally, <!-- PAGE NUM="202" -->and is in relation, by its lower part, with the transverse scapular vessels. This surface, at the junction of the curves of the bone, is also in relation with the brachial plexus of nerves and the subclavian vessels. It gives attachment, near the sternal extremity, to part of the Sternohyoideus; and presents, near the middle, an oblique foramen directed lateralward, which transmits the chief nutrient artery of the bone. Sometimes there are two foramina on the posterior surface, or one on the posterior and another on the inferior surface. The <B>inferior</B> or <B>subclavian surface</B> is bounded, in front, by the anterior border; behind, by the subclavian border. It is narrowed medially, but gradually increases in width laterally, and is continuous with the under surface of the flat portion. On its medial part is a broad rough surface, the <B>costal tuberosity</B> (<I>rhomboid impression</I>), rather more than 2 cm. in length, for the attachment of the costoclavicular ligament. The rest of this surface is occupied by a groove, which gives attachment to the Subclavius; the coracoclavicular fascia, which splits to enclose the muscle, is attached to the margins of the groove. Not infrequently this groove is subdivided longitudinally by a line which gives attachment to the intermuscular septum of the Subclavius.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Sternal Extremity (<I>extremitas sternalis; internal extremity</I>).</B>&#151;The sternal extremity of the clavicle is triangular in form, directed medialward, and a little downward and forward; it presents an articular facet, concave from before backward, convex from above downward, which articulates with the manubrium sterni through the intervention of an articular disk. The lower part of the facet is continued on to the inferior surface of the bone as a small semi-oval area for articulation with the cartilage of the first rib. The circumference of the articular surface is rough, for the attachment of numerous ligaments; the upper angle gives attachment to the articular disk.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Acromial Extremity (<I>extremitas acromialis; outer extremity</I>).</B>&#151;The acromial extremity presents a small, flattened, oval surface directed obliquely downward, for articulation with the acromion of the scapula. The circumference of the articular facet is rough, especially above, for the attachment of the acromioclavicular ligaments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In the female, the clavicle is generally shorter, thinner, less curved, and smoother than in the male. In those persons who perform considerable manual labor it becomes thicker and more curved, and its ridges for muscular attachment are prominently marked.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The clavicle consists of cancellous tissue, enveloped by a compact layer, which is much thicker in the intermediate part than at the extremities of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The clavicle begins to ossify before any other bone in the body; it is ossified from <I>three</I> centers&#151;viz., two primary centers, a medial and a lateral, for the body, <A NAME="txt53">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/49.html#note53">53</A> which appear during the fifth or sixth week of fetal life; and a secondary center for the sternal end, which appears about the eighteenth or twentieth year, and unites with the rest of the bone about the twenty-fifth year.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6a. 2. The Scapula (Shoulder Blade)</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Shoulder Blade)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>scapula</B> forms the posterior part of the shoulder girdle. It is a flat, triangular bone, with two surfaces, three borders, and three angles.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>costal</B> or <B>ventral surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus202.html">(Fig. 202)</A> presents a broad concavity, the <B>subscapular fossa.</B> The medial two-thirds of the fossa are marked by several oblique ridges, which run lateralward and upward. The ridges give attachment to the tendinous insertions, and the surfaces between them to the fleshy fibers, of the Subscapularis. The lateral third of the fossa is smooth and covered by the fibers of this muscle. The fossa is separated from the vertebral border by smooth triangular areas at the medial and inferior angles, and in the interval between these by a narrow ridge which is often deficient. These triangular areas and the intervening ridge afford attachment to the Serratus anterior. At the upper part of the fossa is a transverse depression, where the bone appears to be bent on itself <!-- PAGE NUM="203" -->along a line at right angles to and passing through the center of the glenoid cavity, forming a considerable angle, called the <B>subscapular angle;</B> this gives greater strength to the body of the bone by its arched form, while the summit of the arch serves to support the spine and acromion.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>dorsal surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus203.html">(Fig. 203)</A> is arched from above downward, and is subdivided into two unequal parts by the spine; the portion above the spine is called the <B>supraspinatous fossa,</B> and that below it the <B>infraspinatous fossa.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>supraspinatous fossa,</B> the smaller of the two, is concave, smooth, and broader at its vertebral than at its humeral end; its medial two-thirds give origin to the Supraspinatus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>infraspinatous fossa</B> is much larger than the preceding; toward its vertebral margin a shallow concavity is seen at its upper part; its center presents a prominent convexity, while near the axillary border is a deep groove which runs from the upper toward the lower part. The medial two-thirds of the fossa give origin to the Infraspinatus; the lateral third is covered by this muscle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The dorsal surface is marked near the axillary border by an elevated ridge, which runs from the lower part of the glenoid cavity, downward and backward to the vertebral border, about 2.5 cm. above the inferior angle. The ridge serves for the attachment of a fibrous septum, which separates the Infraspinatus from the Teres major and Teres minor. The surface between the ridge and the axillary border is narrow in the upper two-thirds of its extent, and is crossed near its center by a groove for the passage of the scapular circumflex vessels; it affords attachment to the Teres minor. Its lower third presents a broader, somewhat triangular surface, which gives origin to the Teres major, and over which the Latissimus dorsi glides; frequently the latter muscle takes origin by a few fibers from this part. The broad and narrow portions above alluded to are separated by an oblique line, which runs from the axillary border, downward and backward, to meet the elevated ridge: to it is attached a fibrous septum which separates the Teres muscles from each other.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Spine (<I>spina scapul&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The spine is a prominent plate of bone, which crosses obliquely the medial four-fifths of the dorsal surface of the scapula at its upper part, and separates the supra- from the infraspinatous fossa. It begins at the vertical border by a smooth, triangular area over which the tendon of insertion of the lower part of the Trapezius glides, and, gradually becoming more elevated, ends in the acromion, which overhangs the shoulder-joint. The spine is triangular, and flattened from above downward, its apex being directed toward the vertebral border. It presents two surfaces and three borders. Its <B>superior surface</B> is concave; it assits in forming the supraspinatous fossa, and gives origin to part of the Supraspinatus. Its <B>inferior surface</B> forms part of the infraspinatous fossa, gives origin to a portion of the Infraspinatus, and presents near its center the orifice of a nutrient canal. Of the three borders, the <B>anterior</B> is attached to the dorsal surface of the bone; the <B>posterior,</B> or <B>crest of the spine,</B> is broad, and presents two lips and an intervening rough interval. The Trapezius is attached to the superior lip, and a rough tubercle is generally seen on that portion of the spine which receives the tendon of insertion of the lower part of this muscle. The Deltoideus is attached to the whole length of the inferior lip. The interval between the lips is subcutaneous and partly covered by the tendinous fibers of these muscles. The <B>lateral border,</B> or <B>base,</B> the shortest of the three, is slightly concave; its edge, thick and round, is continuous above with the under surface of the acromion, below with the neck of the scapula. It forms the medial boundary of the <B>great scapular notch,</B> which serves to connect the supra- and infraspinatous foss&#230;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Acromion.</B>&#151;The acromion forms the summit of the shoulder, and is a large, somewhat triangular or oblong process, flattened from behind forward, projecting at first lateralward, and then curving forward and upward, so as to overhang the <!-- PAGE NUM="204" -->glenoid cavity. Its <B>superior surface,</B> directed upward, backward, and lateralward, is convex, rough, and gives attachment to some fibers of the Deltoideus, and in the rest of its extent is subcutaneous. Its <B>inferior surface</B> is smooth and concave. Its <B>lateral border</B> is thick and irregular, and presents three or four tubercles for the tendinous origins of the Deltoideus. Its <B>medial border,</B> shorter than the lateral, is concave, gives attachment to a portion of the Trapezius, and presents about its center a small, oval surface for articulation with the acromial end of the clavicle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus202.html"><IMG WIDTH=241 HEIGHT=350 SRC="/107/Images/small/image202.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i202"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>202&#150; </FONT>Left scapula. Costal surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus202.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Its <B>apex,</B> which corresponds to the point of meeting of these two borders in front, is thin, and has attached to it the coracoacromial ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus203.html"><IMG WIDTH=295 HEIGHT=425 SRC="/107/Images/small/image203.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i203"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>203&#150; </FONT>Left scapula. Dorsal surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus203.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;Of the <I>three</I> borders of the scapula, the <B>superior</B> is the shortest and thinnest; it is concave, and extends from the medial angle to the base of the coracoid process. At its lateral part is a deep, semicircular notch, the <B>scapular notch,</B> <!-- PAGE NUM="205" -->formed partly by the base of the coracoid process. This notch is converted into a foramen by the superior transverse ligament, and serves for the passage of the suprascapular nerve; sometimes the ligament is ossified. The adjacent part of the superior border affords attachment to the Omohyoideus. The <B>axillary border</B> is the thickest of the three. It begins above at the lower margin of the glenoid cavity, and inclines obliquely downward and backward to the inferior angle. Immediately below the glenoid cavity is a rough impression, the <B>infraglenoid tuberosity,</B> about 2.5 cm. in length, which gives origin to the long head of the Triceps brachii; in front of this is a longitudinal groove, which extends as far as the <!-- PAGE NUM="206" -->lower third of this border, and affords origin to part of the Subscapularis. The inferior third is thin and sharp, and serves for the attachment of a few fibers of the Teres major behind, and of the Subscapularis in front. The <B>vertebral border</B> is the longest of the three, and extends from the medial to the inferior angle. It is arched, intermediate in thickness between the superior and the axillary borders, and the portion of it above the spine forms an obtuse angle with the part below. This border presents an anterior and a posterior lip, and an intermediate narrow area. The anterior lip affords attachment to the Serratus anterior; the posterior lip, to the Supraspinatus above the spine, the Infraspinatus below; the area between the two lips, to the Levator scapul&#230; above the triangular surface at the commencement of the spine, to the Rhomboideus minor on the edge of that surface, and to the Rhomboideus major below it; this last is attached by means of a fibrous arch, connected above to the lower part of the triangular surface at the base of the spine, and below to the lower part of the border.</</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus204.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=194 SRC="/107/Images/small/image204.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i204"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>204&#150; </FONT>Posterior view of the thorax and shoulder girdle. (Morris.) (<A HREF="/107/illus204.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Angles.</B>&#151;Of the <I>three</I> angles, the <B>medial,</B> formed by the junction of the superior and vertebral borders, is thin, smooth, rounded, inclined somewhat lateralward, and gives attachment to a few fibers of the Levator scapul&#230;. The <B>inferior angle,</B> thick and rough, is formed by the union of the vertebral and axillary borders; its dorsal surface affords attachment to the Teres major and frequently to a few <!-- PAGE NUM="207" -->fibers of the Latissimus dorsi. The <B>lateral angle</B> is the thickest part of the bone, and is sometimes called the head of the scapula. On it is a shallow pyriform, articular surface, the <B>glenoid cavity,</B> which is directed lateralward and forward and articulates with the head of the humerus; it is broader below than above and its vertical diameter is the longest. The surface is covered with cartilage in the fresh state; and its margins, slightly raised, give attachment to a fibrocartilaginous structure, the <B>glenoidal labrum,</B> which deepens the cavity. At its apex is a slight elevation, the <B>supraglenoid tuberosity,</B> to which the long head of the Biceps brachii is attached. The <B>neck</B> of the scapula is the slightly constricted portion which surrounds the head; it is more distinct below and behind than above and in front.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Coracoid Process (<I>processus coracoideus</I>).</B>&#151;The coracoid process is a thick curved process attached by a broad base to the upper part of the neck of the scapula; it runs at first upward and medialward; then, becoming smaller, it changes its direction, and projects forward and lateralward. The ascending portion, flattened from before backward, presents in front a smooth concave surface, across which the Subscapularis passes. The horizontal portion is flattened from above downward; its upper surface is convex and irregular, and gives attachment to the Pectoralis minor; its under surface is smooth; its medial and lateral borders are rough; the former gives attachment to the Pectoralis minor and the latter to the coracoacromial ligament; the apex is embraced by the conjoined tendon of origin of the Coracobrachialis and short head of the Biceps brachii and gives attachment to the coracoclavicular fascia. On the medial part of the root of the coracoid process is a rough impression for the attachment of the conoid ligament; and running from it obliquely forward and lateralward, on to the upper surface of the horizontal portion, is an elevated ridge for the attachment of the trapezoid ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus205.html"><IMG WIDTH=140 HEIGHT=325 SRC="/107/Images/small/image205.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i205"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>205&#150; </FONT>Left scapula. Lateral view. (<A HREF="/107/illus205.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The head, processes, and the thickened parts of the bone, contain cancellous tissue; the rest consists of a thin layer of compact tissue. The central part of the supraspinatous <!-- PAGE NUM="208" -->fossa and the upper part of the infraspinatous fossa, but especially the former, are usually so thin as to be semitransparent; occasionally the bone is found wanting in this situation, and the adjacent muscles are separated only by fibrous tissue.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification <A HREF="/107/illus206.html">(Fig. 206</A>).</B>&#151;The scapula is ossified from <I>seven</I> or more centers: one for the body, two for the coracoid process, two for the acromion, one for the vertebral border, and one for the inferior angle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Ossification of the body begins about the second month of fetal life, by the formation of an irregular quadrilateral plate of bone, immediately behind the glenoid cavity. This plate extends so as to form the chief part of the bone, the spine growing up from its dorsal surface about the third month. At birth, a large part of the scapula is osseous, but the glenoid cavity, the coracoid process, the acromion, the vertebral border, and the inferior angle are cartilaginous. From the fifteenth to the eighteenth month after birth, ossification takes place in the middle of the coracoid process, which as a rule becomes joined with the rest of the bone about the fifteenth year. Between the fourteenth and twentieth years, ossification of the remaining parts takes place in quick succession, and usually in the following order; first, in the root of the coracoid process, in the form of a broad scale; secondly, near the base of the acromion; thirdly, in the inferior angle and contiguous part of the vertebral border; fourthly, near the extremity of the acromion; fifthly, in the vertebral border. The base of the acromion is formed by an extension from the spine; the two separate nuclei of the acromion unite, and then join with the extension from the spine. The upper third of the glenoid cavity is ossified from a separate center (subcoracoid), which makes its appearance between the tenth and eleventh years and joins between the sixteenth and the eighteenth. Further, an epiphysical plate appears for the lower part of the glenoid cavity, while the tip of the coracoid process frequently presents a separate nucleus. These various epiphyses are joined to the bone by the twenty-fifth year. Failure of bony union between the acromion and spine sometimes occurs, the junction being effected by fibrous tissue, or by an imperfect articulation; in some cases of supposed fracture of the acromion with ligamentous union, it is probable that the detached segment was never united to the rest of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus206.html"><IMG WIDTH=169 HEIGHT=225 SRC="/107/Images/small/image206.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i206"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>206&#150; </FONT>Plan of ossification of the scapula. From seven centers. (<A HREF="/107/illus206.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="209" -->


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6a. 3. The Humerus</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Arm Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>humerus</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus207.html">Figs. 207,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus208.html">208</A>) is the longest and largest bone of the upper extremity; it is divisible into a <B>body</B> and <B>two extremities.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Upper Extremity.</B>&#151;The upper extremity consists of a large rounded <I>head</I> joined to the body by a constricted portion called the <B>neck,</B> and two eminences, the <B>greater</B> and <B>lesser tubercles.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Head (<I>caput humeri</I>).</B>&#151;The head, nearly hemispherical in form,
<A NAME="txt54">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/51.html#note54">54</A> is directed upward, medialward, and a little backward, and articulates with the glenoid cavity of the scapula. The circumference of its articular surface is slightly constricted and is termed the <B>anatomical neck,</B> in contradistinction to a constriction below the tubercles called the <B>surgical neck</B> which is frequently the seat of fracture. Fracture of the anatomical neck rarely occurs.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Anatomical Neck</B> (<I>collum anatomicum</I>) is obliquely directed, forming an obtuse angle with the body. It is best marked in the lower half of its circumference; in the upper half it is represented by a narrow groove separating the head from the tubercles. It affords attachment to the articular capsule of the shoulder-joint, and is perforated by numerous vascular foramina.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Greater Tubercle (<I>tuberculum majus; greater tuberosity</I>).</B>&#151;The greater tubercle is situated lateral to the head and lesser tubercle. Its upper surface is rounded and marked by three flat impressions: the highest of these gives insertion to the Supraspinatus; the middle to the Infraspinatus; the lowest one, and the body of the bone for about 2.5 cm. below it, to the Teres minor. The lateral surface of the greater tubercle is convex, rough, and continuous with the lateral surface of the body.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Lesser Tubercle (<I>tuberculum minus; lesser tuberosity</I>).</B>&#151;The lesser tubercle, although smaller, is more prominent than the greater: it is situated in front, and is directed medialward and forward. Above and in front it presents an impression for the insertion of the tendon of the Subscapularis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The tubercles are separated from each other by a deep groove, the <B>intertubercular groove</B> (<I>bicipital groove</I>), which lodges the long tendon of the Biceps brachii and transmits a branch of the anterior humeral circumflex artery to the shoulder-joint. It runs obliquely downward, and ends near the junction of the upper with the middle third of the bone. In the fresh state its upper part is covered with a thin layer of cartilage, lined by a prolongation of the synovial membrane of the shoulder-joint; its lower portion gives insertion to the tendon of the Latissimus dorsi. It is deep and narrow above, and becomes shallow and a little broader as it descends. Its lips are called, respectively, the <B>crests of the greater and lesser tubercles</B> (<I>bicipital ridges</I>), and form the upper parts of the anterior and medial borders of the body of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body or Shaft (<I>corpus humeri</I>).</B>&#151;The body is almost cylindrical in the upper half of its extent, prismatic and flattened below, and has three borders and three surfaces.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior border</B> runs from the front of the greater tubercle above to the coronoid fossa below, separating the antero-medial from the antero-lateral surface. Its upper part is a prominent ridge, the crest of the greater tubercle; it serves for the insertion of the tendon of the Pectoralis major. About its center it forms the anterior boundary of the deltoid tuberosity; below, it is smooth and rounded, affording attachment to the Brachialis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral border</B> runs from the back part of the greater tubercle to the 
<!-- PAGE NUM="210" -->
<!-- PAGE NUM="211" -->lateral epicondyle, and separates the anterolateral from the posterior surface. Its upper half is rounded and indistinctly marked, serving for the attachment of the lower part of the insertion of the Teres minor, and below this giving origin to the lateral head of the Triceps brachii; its center is traversed by a broad but shallow oblique depression, the <B>radial sulcus</B> (<I>musculospiral groove</I>). Its lower part forms a prominent, rough margin, a little curved from behind forward, the <B>lateral supracondylar ridge,</B> which presents an anterior lip for the origin of the Brachioradialis above, and Extensor carpi radialis longus below, a posterior lip for the Triceps brachii, and an intermediate ridge for the attachment of the lateral intermuscular septum.</</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus207.html"><IMG WIDTH=326 HEIGHT=617 SRC="/107/Images/small/image207.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i207"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>207&#150; </FONT>Left humerus. Anterior view. (<A HREF="/107/illus207.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial border</B> extends from the lesser tubercle to the medial epicondyle. Its upper third consists of a prominent ridge, the <B>crest of the lesser tubercle,</B> which gives insertion to the tendon of the Teres major. About its center is a slight impression for the insertion of the Coracobrachialis, and just below this is the entrance of the nutrient canal, directed downward; sometimes there is a second nutrient canal at the commencement of the radial sulcus. The inferior third of this border is raised into a slight ridge, the <B>medial supracondylar ridge,</B> which becomes very prominent below; it presents an anterior lip for the origins of the Brachialis and Pronator teres, a posterior lip for the medial head of the Triceps brachii, and an intermediate ridge for the attachment of the medial intermuscular septum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>antero-lateral surface</B> is directed lateralward above, where it is smooth, rounded, and covered by the Deltoideus; forward and lateralward below, where it is slightly concave from above downward, and gives origin to part of the Brachialis. About the middle of this surface is a rough, triangular elevation, the <B>deltoid tuberosity</B> for the insertion of the Deltoideus; below this is the <B>radial sulcus,</B> directed obliquely from behind, forward, and downward, and transmitting the radial nerve and profunda artery.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus208.html"><IMG WIDTH=128 HEIGHT=500 SRC="/107/Images/small/image208.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i208"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>208&#150; </FONT>Left humerus. Posterior view. (<A HREF="/107/illus208.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>antero-medial surface,</B> less extensive than the antero-lateral, is directed medialward above, forward and medialward below; its upper part is narrow, and forms the floor of the intertubercular groove which gives insertion to the tendon of the Latissimus dorsi; its middle part is slightly rough for the attachment of some of
<!-- PAGE NUM="212" -->the fibers of the tendon of insertion of the Coracobrachialis; its lower part is smooth, concave from above downward, and gives origin to the Brachialis.
<A NAME="txt55">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/51.html#note55">55</A></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>posterior surface</B> appears somewhat twisted, so that its upper part is directed a little medialward, its lower part backward and a little lateralward. Nearly the whole of this surface is covered by the lateral and medial heads of the Triceps brachii, the former arising above, the latter below the radial sulcus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Lower Extremity.</B>&#151;The lower extremity is flattened from before backward, and curved slightly forward; it ends below in a broad, articular surface, which is divided into two parts by a slight ridge. Projecting on either side are the lateral and medial epicondyles. The <B>articular surface</B> extends a little lower than the epicondyles, and is curved slightly forward; its medial extremity occupies a lower level than the lateral. The lateral portion of this surface consists of a smooth, rounded eminence, named the <B>capitulum of the humerus;</B> it articulates with the cupshaped depression on the head of the radius, and is limited to the front and lower part of the bone. On the medial side of this eminence is a shallow groove, in which is received the medial margin of the head of the radius. Above the front part of the capitulum is a slight depression, the <B>radial fossa,</B> which receives the anterior border of the head of the radius, when the forearm is flexed. The medial portion of the articular surface is named the <B>trochlea,</B> and presents a deep depression between two well-marked borders; it is convex from before backward, concave from side to side, and occupies the anterior, lower, and posterior parts of the extremity. The lateral border separates it from the groove which articulates with the margin of the head of the radius. The medial border is thicker, of greater length, and consequently more prominent, than the lateral. The grooved portion of the articular surface fits accurately within the semilunar notch of the ulna; it is broader and deeper on the posterior than on the anterior aspect of the bone, and is inclined obliquely downward and forward toward the medial side. Above the front part of the trochlea is a small depression, the <B>coronoid fossa,</B> which receives the coronoid process of the ulna during flexion of the forearm. Above the back part of the trochlea is a deep triangular depression, the <B>olecranon fossa,</B> in which the summit of the olecranon is received in extension of the forearm. These foss&#230; are separated from one another by a thin, transparent lamina of bone, which is sometimes perforated by a <B>supratrochlear foramen;</B> they are lined in the fresh state by the synovial membrane of the elbow-joint, and their margins afford attachment to the anterior and posterior ligaments of this articulation. The <B>lateral epicondyle</B> is a small, tuberculated eminence, curved a little forward, and giving attachment to the radial collateral ligament of the elbow-joint, and to a tendon common to the origin of the Supinator and some of the Extensor muscles. The <B>medial epicondyle,</B> larger and more prominent than the lateral, is directed a little backward; it gives attachment to the ulnar collateral ligament of the elbow-joint, to the Pronator teres, and to a common tendon of origin of some of the Flexor muscles of the forearm; the ulnar nerve runs in a groove on the back of this epicondyle. The epicondyles are continuous above with the supracondylar ridges.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The extremities consist of cancellous tissue, covered with a thin, compact layer <A HREF="/107/illus209.html">(Fig. 209</A>); the body is composed of a cylinder of compact tissue, thicker at the center than toward the extremities, and contains a large medullary canal which extends along its whole length.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="213" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification (<A HREF="/107/illus210.html">Figs. 210,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus211.html">211</A>).</B>&#151;The humerus is ossified from <I>eight</I> centers, one for each of the following parts: the body, the head, the greater tubercle, the lesser tubercle, the capitulum, the trochlea, and one for each epicondyle. The center for the body appears near the middle of the bone in the eighth week of fetal life, and soon extends toward the extremities. At birth the humerus is ossified in nearly its whole length, only the extremities remaining cartilaginous. During the first year, sometimes before birth, ossification commences in the head of the bone, and during the third year the center for the greater tubercle, and during the fifth that for the lesser tubercle, make their appearance. By the sixth year the centers for the head and tubercles have joined, so as to form a single large epiphysis, which fuses with the body about the twentieth year. The lower end of the humerus is ossified as follows. At the end of the second year ossification begins in the capitulum, and extends medialward, to form the chief part of the articular end of the bone; the center for the medial part of the trochlea appears about the age of twelve. Ossification begins in the medial epicondyle about the fifth year, and in the lateral about the thirteenth or fourteenth year. About the sixteenth or seventeenth year, the lateral epicondyle and both portions of the articulating surface, having already joined, unite with the body, and at the eighteenth year the medial epicondyle becomes joined to it.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus209.html"><IMG WIDTH=137 HEIGHT=200 SRC="/107/Images/small/image209.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i209"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>209&#150; </FONT>Longitudinal section of head of left humerus. (<A HREF="/107/illus209.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus210.html"><IMG WIDTH=173 HEIGHT=298 SRC="/107/Images/small/image210.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i210"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>210&#150; </FONT>Plan of ossification of the humerus. (<A HREF="/107/illus210.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus211.html"><IMG WIDTH=94 HEIGHT=200 SRC="/107/Images/small/image211.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i211"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>211&#150; </FONT>Epiphysial lines of humerus in a young adult. Anterior aspect. The lines of attachment of the articular capsules are in blue. (<A HREF="/107/illus211.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="214" -->


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6a. 4. The Ulna</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Elbow Bone)</B><A NAME="txt56">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/52.html#note56">56</A></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>ulna</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus212.html">Figs. 212,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus213.html">213</A>) is a long bone, prismatic in form, placed at the medial side of the forearm, parallel with the radius. It is divisible into a <B>body</B> and <B>two extremities.</B> Its upper extremity, of great thickness and strength, forms a large part of the elbow-joint; the bone diminishes in size from above downward, its lower extremity being very small, and excluded from the wrist-joint by the interposition of an articular disk.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Upper Extremity (<I>proximal extremity</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus212.html">(Fig. 212</A>).</B>&#151;The upper extremity presents two curved processes, the <B>olecranon</B> and the <B>coronoid process;</B> and two concave, articular cavities, the <B>semilunar</B> and <B>radial notches.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Olecranon (<I>olecranon process</I>).</B>&#151;The olecranon is a large, thick, curved eminence, situated at the upper and back part of the ulna. It is bent forward at the summit so as to present a prominent lip which is received into the olecranon fossa of the humerus in extension of the forearm. Its <B>base</B> is contracted where it joins the body and the narrowest part of the upper end of the ulna. Its <B>posterior surface,</B> directed backward, is triangular, smooth, subcutaneous, and covered by a bursa. Its <B>superior surface</B> is of quadrilateral form, marked behind by a rough impression for the insertion of the Triceps brachii; and in front, near the margin, by a slight transverse groove for the attachment of part of the posterior ligament of the elbow-joint.
