<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Inferior Ramus (<I>ramus inferior oss. ischii; ascending ramus</I>).</B>&#151;The inferior ramus is the thin, flattened part of the ischium, which ascends from the superior ramus, and joins the inferior ramus of the pubis&#151;the junction being indicated in the adult by a raised line. The <B>outer surface</B> is uneven for the origin of the Obturator externus and some of the fibers of the Adductor magnus; its <B>inner surface</B> forms part of the anterior wall of the pelvis. Its <B>medial border</B> is thick, rough, slightly everted, forms part of the outlet of the pelvis, and presents two ridges and an intervening space. The ridges are continuous with similar ones on the inferior ramus of the pubis: to the outer is attached the deep layer of the superficial perineal fascia (<I>fascia of Colles</I>), and to the inner the inferior fascia of the urogenital diaphragm. If these two ridges be traced downward, they will be found to join with each other just behind the point of origin of the Transversus perin&#230;i; here the two layers of fascia are continuous behind the posterior border of the muscle. To the intervening space, just in front of the point of junction of the ridges, the
<!-- PAGE NUM="236" -->Transversus perin&#230;i is attached, and in front of this a portion of the crus penis vel clitoridis and the Ischiocavernosus. Its <B>lateral border</B> is thin and sharp, and forms part of the medial margin of the obturator foramen.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Pubis (<I>os pubis</I>).</B>&#151;The pubis, the anterior part of the hip bone, is divisible into a <B>body,</B> a <B>superior</B> and an <B>inferior ramus.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body (<I>corpus oss. pubis</I>).</B>&#151;The body forms one-fifth of the acetabulum, contributing by its <B>external surface</B> both to the lunate surface and the acetabular fossa. Its <B>internal surface</B> enters into the formation of the wall of the lesser pelvis and gives origin to a portion of the Obturator internus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Superior Ramus (<I>ramus superior oss. pubis; ascending ramus</I>).</B>&#151;The superior ramus extends from the body to the median plane where it articulates with its fellow of the opposite side. It is conveniently described in two portions, viz., a medial flattened part and a narrow lateral prismoid portion.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Medial Portion</B> of the superior ramus, formerly described as the body of the pubis, is somewhat quadrilateral in shape, and presents for examination two surfaces and three borders. The <B>anterior surface</B> is rough, directed downward and outward, and serves for the origin of various muscles. The Adductor longus arises from the upper and medial angle, immediately below the crest; lower down, the Obturator externus, the Adductor brevis, and the upper part of the Gracilis take origin. The <B>posterior surface,</B> convex from above downward, concave from side to side, is smooth, and forms part of the anterior wall of the pelvis. It gives origin to the Levator ani and Obturator internus, and attachment to the puboprostatic ligaments and to a few muscular fibers prolonged from the bladder. The <B>upper border</B> presents a prominent tubercle, the <B>pubic tubercle</B> (<I>pubic spine</I>), which projects forward; the inferior crus of the <B>subcutaneous inguinal ring</B> (<I>external abdominal ring</I>), and the <B>inguinal ligament</B> (<I>Poupart&#146;s ligament</I>) are attached to it. Passing upward and lateralward from the pubic tubercle is a well-defined ridge, forming a part of the pectineal line which marks the brim of the lesser pelvis: to it are attached a portion of the <B>inguinal falx</B> (<I>conjoined tendon of Obliquus internus and Transversus</I>), the <B>lacunar ligament</B> (<I>Gimbernat&#146;s ligament</I>), and the <B>reflected inguinal ligament</B> (<I>triangular fascia</I>). Medial to the pubic tubercle is the <B>crest,</B> which extends from this process to the medial end of the bone. It affords attachment to the inguinal falx, and to the Rectus abdominis and Pyramidalis. The point of junction of the crest with the medial border of the bone is called the <B>angle;</B> to it, as well as to the symphysis, the superior crus of the subcutaneous inguinal ring is attached. The <B>medial border</B> is articular; it is oval, and is marked by eight or nine transverse ridges, or a series of nipple-like processes arranged in rows, separated by grooves; they serve for the attachment of a thin layer of cartilage, which intervenes between it and the interpubic fibrocartilaginous lamina. The <B>lateral border</B> presents a sharp margin, the <B>obturator crest,</B> which forms part of the circumference of the obturator foramen and affords attachment to the obturator membrane.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Lateral Portion</B> of the ascending ramus has three surfaces: superior, inferior, andposterior. The <B>superior surface</B> presents a continuation of the pectineal line, already mentioned as commencing at the pubic tubercle. In front of this line, the surface of bone is triangular in form, wider laterally than medially, and is covered by the Pectineus. The surface is bounded, laterally, by a rough eminence, the <B>iliopectineal eminence,</B> which serves to indicate the point of junction of the ilium and pubis, and below by a prominent ridge which extends from the acetabular notch to the pubic tubercle. The <B>inferior surface</B> forms the upper boundary of the obturator foramen, and presents, laterally, a broad and deep, oblique groove, for the passage of the obturator vessels and nerve; and medially, a sharp margin, the <B>obturator crest,</B> forming part of the circumference of the obturator foramen, and giving attachment to the obturator membrane. The <B>posterior surface</B> constitutes
<!-- PAGE NUM="237" -->part of the anterior boundary of the lesser pelvis. It is smooth, convex from above downward, and affords origin to some fibers of the Obturator internus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Inferior Ramus (<I>ramus inferior oss. pubis; descending ramus</I>).</B>&#151;The inferior ramus is thin and flattened. It passes lateralward and downward from the medial end of the superior ramus; it becomes narrower as it descends and joins with the inferior ramus of the ischium below the obturator foramen. Its <B>anterior surface</B> is rough, for the origin of muscles&#151;the Gracilis along its medial border, a portion of the Obturator externus where it enters into the formation of the obturator foramen, and between these two, the Adductores brevis and magnus, the former being the more medial. The <B>posterior surface</B> is smooth, and gives origin to the Obturator internus, and, close to the medial margin, to the Constrictor urethr&#230;. The <B>medial border</B> is thick, rough, and everted, especially in females. It presents two ridges, separated by an intervening space. The ridges extend downward, and are continuous with similar ridges on the inferior ramus of the ischium; to the external is attached the fascia of Colles, and to the internal the inferior fascia of the urogenital diaphragm. The <B>lateral border</B> is thin and sharp, forms part of the circumference of the obturator foramen, and gives attachment to the obturator membrane.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Acetabulum (<I>cotyloid cavity</I>).</B>&#151;The acetabulum is a deep, cup-shaped, hemispherical depression, directed downward, lateralward, and forward. It is formed medially by the pubis, above by the ilium, laterally and below by the ischium; a little less than two-fifths is contributed by the ilium, a little more than two-fifths by the ischium, and the remaining fifth by the pubis. It is bounded by a prominent uneven rim, which is thick and strong above, and serves for the attachment of the <B>glenoidal labrum</B> (<I>cotyloid ligament</I>), which contracts its orifice, and deepens the surface for articulation. It presents below a deep notch, the <B>acetabular notch,</B> which is continuous with a circular non-articular depression, the <B>acetabular fossa,</B> at the bottom of the cavity: this depression is perforated by numerous apertures, and lodges a mass of fat. The notch is converted into a foramen by the transverse ligament; through the foramen nutrient vessels and nerves enter the joint; the margins of the notch serve for the attachment of the ligamentum teres. The rest of the acetabulum is formed by a curved articular surface, the <B>lunate surface,</B> for articulation with the head of the femur.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="19"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;19</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Obturator Foramen (<I>foramen obturatum; thyroid foramen</I>).</B>&#151;The obturator foramen is a large aperture, situated between the ischium and pubis. In the male it is large and of an oval form, its longest diameter slanting obliquely from before backward; in the female it is smaller, and more triangular. It is bounded by a thin, uneven margin, to which a strong membrane is attached, and presents, superiorly, a deep groove, the <B>obturator groove,</B> which runs from the pelvis obliquely medialward and downward. This groove is converted into a canal by a ligamentous band, a specialized part of the obturator membrane, attached to two tubercles: one, the <B>posterior obturator tubercle,</B> on the medial border of the ischium, just in front of the acetabular notch; the other, the <B>anterior obturator tubercle,</B> on the obturator crest of the superior ramus of the pubis. Through the canal the obturator vessels and nerve pass out of the pelvis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="20"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;20</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The thicker parts of the bone consist of cancellous tissue, enclosed between two layers of compact tissue; the thinner parts, as at the bottom of the acetabulum and center of the iliac fossa, are usually semitransparent, and composed entirely of compact tissue.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="21"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;21</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification <A HREF="/107/illus237.html">(Fig. 237</A>).</B>&#151;The hip bone is ossified from <I>eight</I> centers: <I>three</I> primary&#151;one each for the ilium, ischium, and pubis; and <I>five</I> secondary&#151;one each for the crest of the ilium, the anterior inferior spine (said to occur more frequently in the male than in the female), the tuberosity of the ischium, the pubic symphysis (more frequent in the female than in the male), and one or more for the Y-shaped piece at the bottom of the acetabulum. The centers appear in the following order: in the lower part of the ilium, immediately above the greater sciatic notch, about the eighth or ninth week of fetal life; in the superior ramus of the ischium, about the third month; <!-- PAGE NUM="238" -->in the superior ramus of the pubis, between the fourth and fifth months. At birth, the three primary centers are quite separate, the crest, the bottom of the acetabulum, the ischial tuberosity, and the inferior rami of the ischium and pubis being still cartilaginous. By the seventh or eighth year, the inferior rami of the pubis and ischium are almost completely united by bone. About the thirteenth or fourteenth year, the three primary centers have extended their growth into the bottom of the acetabulum, and are there separated from each other by a Y-shaped portion of cartilage, which now presents traces of ossification, often by two or more centers. One of these, the <I>os acetabuli,</I> appears about the age of twelve, between the ilium and pubis, and fuses with them about the age of eighteen; it forms the pubic part of the acetabulum. The ilium and ischium then become joined, and lastly the pubis and ischium, through the intervention of this Y-shaped portion. At about the age of puberty, ossification takes place in each of the remaining portions, and they join with the rest of the bone between the twentieth and twenty-fifth years. Separate centers are frequently found for the pubic tubercle and the ischial spine, and for the crest and angle of the pubis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="22"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;22</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The hip bone articulates with its fellow of the opposite side, and with the sacrum and femur.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="23"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;23</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus237.html"><IMG WIDTH=229 HEIGHT=266 SRC="/107/Images/small/image237.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i237"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>237&#150; </FONT>Plan of ossification of the hip bone. The three primary centers unite through a Y-shaped piece about puberty. Epiphyses appear about puberty, and unite about twenty-fifth year. (<A HREF="/107/illus237.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6c. 2. The Pelvis</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The <B>pelvis,</B> so called from its resemblance to a basin, is a bony ring, interposed between the movable vertebr&#230; of the vertebral column which it supports, and the lower limbs upon which it rests; it is stronger and more massively constructed than the wall of the cranial or thoracic cavities, and is composed of four bones: the two <B>hip bones</B> laterally and in front and the <B>sacrum</B> and <B>coccyx</B> behind.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The pelvis is divided by an oblique plane passing through the prominence of the sacrum, the arcuate and pectineal lines, and the upper margin of the symphysis pubis, into the greater and the lesser pelvis. The circumference of this plane is termed the <B>linea terminalis</B> or <B>pelvic brim.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Greater or False Pelvis (<I>pelvis major</I>).</B>&#151;The greater pelvis is the expanded portion of the cavity situated above and in front of the pelvic brim. It is bounded on either side by the ilium; in <I>front</I> it is incomplete, presenting a wide interval between the anterior borders of the ilia, which is filled up in the fresh state by <!-- PAGE NUM="239" -->the parietes of the abdomen; behind is a deep notch on either side between the ilium and the base of the sacrum. It supports the intestines, and transmits part of their weight to the anterior wall of the abdomen.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Lesser or True Pelvis (<I>pelvis minor</I>).</B>&#151;The lesser pelvis is that part of the pelvic cavity which is situated below and behind the pelvic brim. Its bony walls are more complete than those of the greater pelvis. For convenience of description, it is divided into an <B>inlet</B> bounded by the superior circumference, and <B>outlet</B> bounded by the inferior circumference, and a <B>cavity.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Superior Circumference.</B>&#151;The superior circumference forms the brim of the pelvis, the included space being called the <B>superior aperture</B> or <B>inlet</B> (<I>apertura pelvis</I> [<I>minoris</I>] <I>superior</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus238.html">(Fig. 238</A>). It is formed laterally by the pectineal and arcuate lines, in front by the crests of the pubes, and behind by the anterior margin of the base of the sacrum and sacrovertebral angle. The superior aperture is somewhat heart-shaped, obtusely pointed in front, diverging on either side, and encroached upon behind by the projection forward of the promontory of the sacrum. It has three principal diameters: antero-posterior, transverse, and oblique. The <B>anteroposterior</B> or <B>conjugate diameter</B> extends from the sacrovertebral angle to the symphysis pubis; its average measurement is about 110 mm. in the female. The <B>transverse diameter</B> extends across the greatest width of the superior aperture, from the middle of the brim on one side to the same point on the opposite; its average measurement is about 135 mm. in the female. The <B>oblique diameter</B> extends from the iliopectineal eminence of one side to the sacroiliac articulation of the opposite side; its average measurement is about 125 mm. in the female.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus238.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=162 SRC="/107/Images/small/image238.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i238"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>238&#150; </FONT>Diameters of superior aperture of lesser pelvis (female). (<A HREF="/107/illus238.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>cavity</B> of the lesser pelvis is bounded in front and below by the pubic symphysis and the superior rami of the pubes; above and behind, by the pelvic surfaces of the sacrum and coccyx, which, curving forward above and below, contract the superior and inferior apertures of the cavity; laterally, by a broad, smooth, quadrangular area of bone, corresponding to the inner surfaces of the body and superior ramus of the ischium and that part of the ilium which is below the arcuate line. From this description it will be seen that the cavity of the lesser pelvis is a short, curved canal, considerably deeper on its posterior than on its anterior wall. It contains, in the fresh subject, the pelvic colon, rectum, bladder, and some of the organs of generation. The rectum is placed at the back of the pelvis, in the curve of the sacrum and coccyx; the bladder is in front, behind the pubic symphysis. In the female the uterus and vagina occupy the interval between these viscera.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="240" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Lower Circumference.</B>&#151;The lower circumference of the pelvis is very irregular; the space enclosed by it is named the <B>inferior aperture</B> or <B>outlet</B> (<I>apertura pelvis</I> [<I>minoris</I>] <I>inferior</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus239.html">(Fig. 239),</A> and is bounded behind by the point of the coccyx, and laterally by the ischial tuberosities. These eminences are separated by three notches: one in front, the <B>pubic arch,</B> formed by the convergence of the inferior rami of the ischium and pubis on either side. The other notches, one on either side, are formed by the sacrum and coccyx behind, the ischium in front, and the ilium above; they are called the <B>sciatic notches;</B> in the natural state they are converted into foramina by the sacrotuberous and sacrospinous ligaments. When the ligaments are <I>in situ,</I> the inferior aperture of the pelvis is lozenge-shaped, bounded, in front, by the pubic arcuate ligament and the inferior rami of the pubes and ischia; laterally, by the ischial tuberosities; and behind, by the sacrotuberous ligaments and the tip of the coccyx.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus239.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=166 SRC="/107/Images/small/image239.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i239"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>239&#150; </FONT>Diameters of inferior aperture of lesser pelvis (female). (<A HREF="/107/illus239.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The diameters of the outlet of the pelvis are two, antero-posterior and transverse. The <B>antero-posterior diameter</B> extends from the tip of the coccyx to the lower part of the pubic symphysis; its measurement is from 90 to 115 mm. in the female. It varies with the length of the coccyx, and is capable of increase or diminution, on account of the mobility of that bone. The <B>transverse diameter,</B> measured between the posterior parts of the ischial tuberosities, is about 115 mm. in the female.<A NAME="txt58">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/58.html#note58">58</A></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus240.html"><IMG WIDTH=148 HEIGHT=211 SRC="/107/Images/small/image240.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i240"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>240&#150; </FONT>Median sagittal section of pelvis. (<A HREF="/107/illus240.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Axes <A HREF="/107/illus240.html">(Fig. 240</A>).</B>&#151;A line at right angles to the plane of the superior aperture at its center would, if prolonged, pass through the umbilicus above and the middle of the coccyx below; the axis of the superior aperture is therefore directed downward and backward. The axis of the inferior aperture, produced upward, would touch the base of the sacrum, and is also directed downward, and slightly backward. The axis of the cavity&#151;<I>i. e.,</I> an axis at right angles to a series of planes between those of the superior and inferior apertures <!-- PAGE NUM="241" -->&#151;is curved like the cavity itself: this curve corresponds to the concavity of the sacrum and coccyx, the extremities being indicated by the central points of the superior and inferior apertures. A knowledge of the direction of these axes serves to explain the course of the fetus in its passage through the pelvis during parturition.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Position of the Pelvis <A HREF="/107/illus240.html">(Fig. 240</A>).</B>&#151;In the erect posture, the pelvis is placed obliquely with regard to the trunk: the plane of the superior aperture forms an angle of from 50&#176; to 60&#176;, and that of the inferior aperture one of about 15&#176; with the horizontal plane. The pelvic surface of the symphysis pubis looks upward and backward, the concavity of the sacrum and coccyx downward and forward. The position of the pelvis in the erect posture may be indicated by holding it so that the anterior superior iliac spines and the front of the top of the symphysis pubis are in the same vertical plane.</</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus241.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=202 SRC="/107/Images/small/image241.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i241"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>241&#150; </FONT>Male pelvis. (<A HREF="/107/illus241.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Differences between the Male and Female Pelves.</B>&#151;The <I>female</I> pelvis <A HREF="/107/illus242.html">(Fig. 242)</A> is distinguished from that of the <I>male</I> <A HREF="/107/illus241.html">(Fig. 241)</A> by its bones being more delicate and its depth less. The whole pelvis is less massive, and its muscular impressions are slightly marked. The ilia are less sloped, and the anterior iliac spines more widely separated; hence the greater lateral prominence of the hips. The preauricular sulcus is more commonly present and better marked. The superior aperture of the lesser pelvis is larger in the female than in the male; it is more nearly circular, and its obliquity is greater. The cavity is shallower and wider; the sacrum is shorter wider, and its upper part is less curved; the obturator foramina are triangular in shape and smaller in size than in the male. The inferior aperture is larger and the coccyx more movable. The sciatic notches are wider and shallower, and the spines of the ischia project less inward. The acetabula are smaller and look more distinctly forward (Derry<A NAME="txt59">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/58.html#note59">59</A>). The ischial tuberosities and the acetabula are wider apart, and the former are more everted. The pubic symphysis is less deep, and the pubic arch is wider and more rounded than in the male, where it is an angle rather than an arch.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="242" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The size of the pelvis varies not only in the two sexes, but also in different members of the same sex, and does not appear to be influenced in any way by the height of the individual. Women of short stature, as a rule, have broad pelves. Occasionally the pelvis is equally contracted in all its dimensions, so much so that all its diameters measure 12.5 mm. less than the average, and this even in well-formed women of average height. The principal divergences, however, are found at the superior aperture, and affect the relation of the antero-posterior to the transverse diameter. Thus the superior aperture may be elliptical either in a transverse or an antero-posterior direction, the transverse diameter in the former, and the antero-posterior in the latter, greatly exceeding the other diameters; in other instances it is almost circular.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus242.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=160 SRC="/107/Images/small/image242.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i242"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>242&#150; </FONT>Female pelvis. (<A HREF="/107/illus242.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;In the fetus, and for several years after birth, the pelvis is smaller in proportion than in the adult, and the projection of the sacrovertebral angle less marked. The characteristic differences between the male and female pelvis are distinctly indicated as early as the fourth month of fetal life.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Abnormalities.</B>&#151;There is arrest of development in the bones of the pelvis in cases of extroversion of the bladder; the anterior part of the pelvic girdle is deficient, the superior rami of the pubes are imperfectly developed, and the symphysis is absent. &#147;The pubic bones are separated to the extent of from two to four inches, the superior rami shortened and directed forward, and the obturator foramen diminished in size, narrowed, and turned outward. The iliac bones are straightened out more than normal. The sacrum is very peculiar. The lateral curve, instead of being concave, is flattened out or even convex, with the iliosacral facets turned more outward than normal, while the vertical curve is straightened.&#148;<A NAME="txt60">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/58.html#note60">60</A></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6c. 3. The Femur</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Thigh Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>femur</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus244.html">Figs. 244,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus245.html">245</A>), the longest and strongest bone in the skeleton, is almost perfectly cylindrical in the greater part of its extent. In the erect posture it is not vertical, being separated above from its fellow by a considerable interval, which corresponds to the breadth of the pelvis, but inclining gradually downward and medialward, so as to approach its fellow toward its lower part, for the purpose of bringing the knee-joint near the line of gravity of the body. The degree of this inclination varies in different persons, and is greater in the female than in the male, <!-- PAGE NUM="243" -->on account of the greater breadth of the pelvis. The femur, like other long bones, is divisible into a <B>body</B> and <B>two extremities.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Upper Extremity (<I>proximal extremity,</I> <A HREF="/107/illus243.html">Fig. 243</A>).</B>&#151;The upper extremity presents for examination a <B>head,</B> a <B>neck,</B> a <B>greater</B> and a <B>lesser trochanter.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Head (<I>caput femoris</I>).</B>&#151;The head which is globular and forms rather more than a hemisphere, is directed upward, medialward, and a little forward, the greater part of its convexity being above and in front. Its surface is smooth, coated with cartilage in the fresh state, except over an ovoid depression, the <B>fovea capitis femoris,</B> which is situated a little below and behind the center of the head, and gives attachment to the ligamentum teres.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Neck (<I>collum femoris</I>).</B>&#151;The neck is a flattened pyramidal process of bone, connecting the head with the body, and forming with the latter a wide angle opening medialward. The angle is widest in infancy, and becomes lessened during growth, so that at puberty it forms a gentle curve from the axis of the body of the bone. In the adult, the neck forms an angle of about 125&#176; with the body, but this varies in inverse proportion to the development of the pelvis and the stature. In the female, in consequence of the increased width of the pelvis, the neck of the femur forms more nearly a right angle with the body than it does in the male. The angle decreases during the period of growth, but after full growth has been attained it does not usually undergo any change, even in old age; it varies considerably in different persons of the same age. It is smaller in short than in long bones, and when the pelvis is wide. In addition to projecting upward and medialward from the body of the femur, the neck also projects somewhat forward; the amount of this forward projection is extremely variable, but on an average is from 12&#176; to 14&#176;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus243.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=188 SRC="/107/Images/small/image243.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i243"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>243&#150; </FONT>Upper extremity of right femur viewed from behind and above. (<A HREF="/107/illus243.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The neck is flattened from before backward, contracted in the middle, and broader laterally than medially. The vertical diameter of the lateral half is increased by the obliquity of the lower edge, which slopes downward to join the body at the level of the lesser trochanter, so that it measures one-third more than the antero-posterior diameter. The medial half is smaller and of a more circular shape. The <B>anterior surface</B> of the neck is perforated by numerous vascular foramina. Along the upper part of the line of junction of the anterior surface with the head is a shallow groove, best marked in elderly subjects; this <!-- PAGE NUM="244" -->groove lodges the orbicular fibers of the capsule of the hip-joint. The <B>posterior surface</B> is smooth, and is broader and more concave than the anterior: the posterior part of the capsule of the hip-joint is attached to it about 1 cm. above the intertrochanteric crest. The <B>superior border</B> is short and thick, and ends laterally at the greater trochanter; its surface is perforated by large foramina. The <B>inferior border,</B> long and narrow, curves a little backward, to end at the lesser trochanter.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Trochanters.</B>&#151;The trochanters are prominent processes which afford leverage to the muscles that rotate the thigh on its axis. They are two in number, the greater and the lesser.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus244.html"><IMG WIDTH=233 HEIGHT=626 SRC="/107/Images/small/image244.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i244"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>244&#150; </FONT>Right femur. Anterior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus244.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Greater Trochanter</B> (<I>trochanter major; great trochanter</I>) is a large, irregular, quadrilateral eminence, situated at the junction of the neck with the upper part of the body. It is directed a little lateralward and backward, and, in the adult, is about 1 cm. lower than the head. It has two surfaces and four borders. The <B>lateral surface,</B> quadrilateral in form, is broad, rough, convex, and marked by a diagonal impression, which extends from the postero-superior to the antero-inferior angle, and serves for the insertion of the tendon of the Glut&#230;us medius. Above the impression is a triangular surface, sometimes rough for part of the tendon of the same muscle, sometimes smooth for the interposition of a bursa between the tendon and the bone. Below and behind the diagonal impression is a smooth, triangular surface, over which the tendon of the Glut&#230;us maximus plays, a bursa being interposed. The <B>medial surface,</B> of much less extent than the lateral, presents at its base a deep depression, the <B>trochanteric fossa</B> (<I>digital fossa</I>), for the insertion of the tendon of the Obturator externus, and above and in front of this an impression for the insertion of the Obsturator <!-- PAGE NUM="245" -->internus and Gemelli. The <B>superior border</B> is free; it is thick and irregular, and marked near the center by an impression for the insertion of the Piriformis. The <B>inferior border</B> corresponds to the line of junction of the base of the trochanter with the lateral surface of the body; it is marked by a rough, prominent, slightly curved ridge, which gives origin to the upper part of the Vastus lateralis. The <B>anterior border</B> is prominent and somewhat irregular; it affords insertion at its lateral part to the Glut&#230;us minimus. The <B>posterior border</B> is very prominent and appears as a free, rounded edge, which bounds the back part of the trochanteric fossa.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>Lesser Trochanter</B> (<I>trochanter minor; small trochanter</I>) is a conical eminence, which varies in size in different subjects; it projects from the lower and back part of the base of the neck. From its apex three well-marked borders extend; two of these are above&#151;a <B>medial</B> continuous with the lower border of the neck, a <B>lateral</B> with the intertrochanteric crest; the <B>inferior border</B> is continuous with the middle division of the linea aspera. The <B>summit</B> of the trochanter is rough, and gives insertion to the tendon of the Psoas major.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;A prominence, of variable size, occurs at the junction of the upper part of the neck with the greater trochanter, and is called the <B>tubercle of the femur;</B> it is the point of meeting of five muscles: the Glut&#230;us minimus laterally, the Vastus lateralis below, and the tendon of the Obturator internus and two Gemelli above. Running obliquely downward and medialward from the tubercle is the <B>intertrochanteric line</B> (<I>spiral line of the femur</I>); it winds around the medial side of the body of the bone, below the lesser trochanter, <!-- PAGE NUM="246" -->and ends about 5 cm. below this eminence in the linea aspera. Its upper half is rough, and affords attachment to the iliofemoral ligament of the hip-joint; its lower half is less prominent, and gives origin to the upper part of the Vastus medialis. Running obliquely downward and medialward from the summit of the greater trochanter on the posterior surface of the neck is a prominent ridge, the <B>intertrochanteric crest.</B> Its upper half forms the posterior border of the greater trochanter, and its lower half runs downward and medialward to the lesser trochanter. A slight ridge is sometimes seen commencing about the middle of the intertrochanteric&#146; crest, and reaching vertically downward for about 5 cm. along the back part of the body: it is called the <B>linea quadrata,</B> and gives attachment to the Quadratus femoris and a few fibers of the Adductor magnus. Generally there is merely a slight thickening about the middle of the intertrochanteric crest, marking the attachment of the upper part of the Quadratus femoris.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus245.html"><IMG WIDTH=238 HEIGHT=635 SRC="/107/Images/small/image245.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i245"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>245&#150; </FONT>Right femur. Posterior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus245.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body or Shaft (<I>corpus femoris</I>).</B>&#151;The body, almost cylindrical in form, is a little broader above than in the center, broadest and somewhat flattened from before backward below. It is slightly arched, so as to be convex in front, and concave behind, where it is strengthened by a prominent longitudinal ridge, the <B>linea aspera.</B> It presents for examination three borders, separating three surfaces. Of the borders, one, the linea aspera, is posterior, one is medial, and the other, lateral.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>linea aspera</B> <A HREF="/107/illus245.html">(Fig. 245)</A> is a prominent longitudinal ridge or crest, on the middle third of the bone, presenting a medial and a lateral lip, and a narrow rough, intermediate line. Above, the linea aspera is prolonged by three ridges. The lateral ridge is very rough, and runs almost vertically upward to the base of the greater trochanter. It is termed the <B>gluteal tuberosity,</B> and gives attachment to part of the Glut&#230;us maximus: its upper part is often elongated into a roughened crest, on which a more or less well-marked, rounded tubercle, the <B>third trochanter,</B> is occasionally developed. The intermediate ridge or <B>pectineal line</B> is continued to the base of the lesser trochanter and gives attachment to the Pectineus; the medial ridge is lost in the intertrochanteric line; between these two a portion of the Iliacus is inserted. Below, the linea aspera is prolonged into two ridges, enclosing between them a triangular area, the <B>popliteal surface,</B> upon which the popliteal artery rests. Of these two ridges, the lateral is the more prominent, and descends to the summit of the lateral condyle. The medial is less marked, especially at its upper part, where it is crossed by the femoral artery. It ends below at the summit of the medial condyle, in a small tubercle, the <B>adductor tubercle,</B> which affords insertion to the tendon of the Adductor magnus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;From the <B>medial lip</B> of the linea aspera and its prolongations above and below, the Vastus medialis arises; and from the <B>lateral lip</B> and its upward prolongation, the Vastus lateralis takes origin. The Adductor magnus is inserted into the linea aspera, and to its lateral prolongation above, and its medial prolongation below. Between the Vastus lateralis and the Adductor magnus two muscles are attached&#151;viz., the Glut&#230;us maximus inserted above, and the short head of the Biceps femoris arising below. Between the Adductor magnus and the Vastus medialis four muscles are inserted: the Iliacus and Pectineus above; the Adductor brevis and Adductor longus below. The linea aspera is perforated a little below its center by the nutrient canal, which is directed obliquely upward.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The other two <I>borders</I> of the femur are only slightly marked: the <B>lateral border</B> extends from the antero-inferior angle of the greater trochanter to the anterior extremity of the lateral condyle; the <B>medial border</B> from the intertrochanteric line, at a point opposite the lesser trochanter, to the anterior extremity of the medial condyle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>anterior surface</B> includes that portion of the shaft which is situated between the lateral and medial borders. It is smooth, convex, broader above and below than in the center. From the upper three-fourths of this surface the Vastus intermedius arises; the lower fourth is separated from the muscle by the intervention <!-- PAGE NUM="247" -->of the synovial membrane of the knee-joint and a bursa; from the upper part of it the Articularis genu takes origin. The <B>lateral surface</B> includes the portion between the lateral border and the linea aspera; it is continuous above with the corresponding surface of the greater trochanter, below with that of the lateral condyle: from its upper three-fourths the Vastus intermedius takes origin. The <B>medial surface</B> includes the portion between the medial border and the linea aspera; it is continuous above with the lower border of the neck, below with the medial side of the medial condyle: it is covered by the Vastus medialis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus246.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=108 SRC="/107/Images/small/image246.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i246"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>246&#150; </FONT>Lower extremity of right femur viewed from below. (<A HREF="/107/illus246.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Lower Extremity (<I>distal extremity</I>), <A HREF="/107/illus246.html">(Fig. 246</A>).</B>&#151;The lower extremity, larger than the upper, is somewhat cuboid in form, but its transverse diameter is greater than its antero-posterior; it consists of two oblong eminences known as the <B>condyles.</B> In front, the condyles are but slightly prominent, and are separated from one another by a smooth shallow articular depression called the <B>patellar surface;</B> behind, they project considerably, and the interval between them forms a deep notch, the <B>intercondyloid fossa.</B> The <B>lateral condyle</B> is the more prominent and is the broader both in its antero-posterior and transverse diameters, the <B>medial condyle</B> is the longer and, when the femur is held with its body perpendicular, projects to a lower level. When, however, the femur is in its natural oblique position the lower surfaces of the two condyles lie practically in the same horizontal plane. The condyles are not quite parallel with one another; the long axis of the lateral is almost directly antero-posterior, but that of the medial runs backward and medialward. Their opposed surfaces are small, rough, and concave, and form the walls of the intercondyloid fossa. This fossa is limited above by a ridge, the <B>intercondyloid line,</B> and below by the central part of the posterior margin of the patellar surface. The posterior cruciate ligament of the knee-joint is attached to the lower and front part of the medial wall of the fossa and the anterior cruciate ligament to an impression on the upper and back part of its lateral wall. Each condyle is surmounted by an elevation, the epicondyle. The <B>medial epicondyle</B> is a large convex eminence to which the tibial collateral ligament of the knee-joint is attached. At its upper part is the adductor tubercle, already referred to, and behind it is a rough impression which gives origin to the medial head of the Gastrocnemius. The <B>lateral epicondyle,</B> smaller and less prominent than the medial, gives attachment to the fibular collateral ligament of the knee-joint. Directly below it is a small depression from which a smooth well-marked groove curves obliquely upward and backward to the posterior extremity of the condyle. This groove is separated from the articular surface of the condyle by a prominent lip across which a second, shallower groove runs vertically downward from the depression. In the fresh state these grooves are covered with cartilage. The Popliteus arises from the depression; its tendon lies in the oblique groove when the knee is flexed and in the vertical <!-- PAGE NUM="248" -->groove when the knee is extended. Above and behind the lateral epicondyle is an area for the origin of the lateral head of the Gastrocnemius, above and to the medial side of which the Plantaris arises.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>articular surface</B> of the lower end of the femur occupies the anterior, inferior, and posterior surfaces of the condyles. Its front part is named the <B>patellar surface</B> and articulates with the patella; it presents a median groove which extends downward to the intercondyloid fossa and two convexities, the lateral of which is broader, more prominent, and extends farther upward than the medial. The lower and posterior parts of the articular surface constitute the <B>tibial surfaces</B> for articulation with the corresponding condyles of the tibia and menisci. These surfaces are separated from one another by the intercondyloid fossa and from the patellar surface by faint grooves which extend obliquely across the condyles. The lateral groove is the better marked; it runs lateralward and forward from the front part of the intercondyloid fossa, and expands to form a triangular depression. When the knee-joint is fully extended, the triangular depression rests upon the anterior portion of the lateral meniscus, and the medial part of the groove comes into contact with the medial margin of the lateral articular surface of the tibia in front of the lateral tubercle of the tibial intercondyloid eminence. The medial groove is less distinct than the lateral. It does not reach as far as the intercondyloid fossa and therefore exists only on the medial part of the condyle; it receives the anterior edge of the medial meniscus when the knee-joint is extended. Where the groove ceases laterally the patellar surface is seen to be continued backward as a semilunar area close to the anterior part of the intercondyloid fossa; this semilunar area articulates with the medial vertical facet of the patella in forced flexion of the knee-joint. The tibial surfaces of the condyles are convex from side to side and from before backward. Each presents a double curve, its posterior segment being an arc of a circle, its anterior, part of a cycloid.<A NAME="txt61">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/59.html#note61">61</A></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Architecture of the Femur.</B>&#151;Koch <A NAME="txt62">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/59.html#note62">62</A> by mathematical analysis has &#147;shown that in every part of the femur there is a remarkable adaptation of the inner structure of the bone to the machanical requirements due to the load on the femur-head. The various parts of the femur taken together form a single mechanical structure wonderfully well-adapted for the efficient, economical transmission of the loads from the acetabulum to the tibia; a structure in which every element contributes its modicum of strength in the manner required by theoretical mechanics for maximum efficiency.&#148; &#147;The internal structure is everywhere so formed as to provide in an efficient manner for all the internal stresses which occur due to the load on the femur-head. Throughout the femur, with the load on the femur-head, the bony material is arranged in the paths of the maximum internal stresses, which are thereby resisted with the greatest efficiency, and hence with maximum economy of material.&#148; &#147;The conclusion is inevitable that the inner structure and outer form of the femur are governed by the conditions of maximum stress to which the bone is subjected normally by the preponderant load on the femur-head; that is, by the body weight transmitted to the femur-head through the acetabulum.&#148; &#147;The femur obeys the mechanical laws that govern other elastic bodies under stress; the relation between the computed internal stresses due to the load on the femur-head, and the internal structure of the different portions of the femur is in very close agreement with the theoretical relations that should exist between stress and structure for maximum economy and efficiency; and, therefore, it is believed that the following laws of bone structure have been demonstrated for the femur:</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;1. The inner structure and external form of human bone are closely adapted to the mechanical conditions existing at every point in the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;2. The inner architecture of normal bone is determined by definite and exact requirements of mathematical and mechanical laws to produce a maximum of strength with a minimum of material.&#148;</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="19"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;19</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Inner Architecture of the Upper Femur.</B>&#151;&#147;The spongy bone of the upper femur (to the lower limit of the lesser trochanter) is composed of two distinct systems of trabecul&#230; arranged in curved paths: one, which has its origin in the medial (inner) side of the shaft and curving upward <!-- PAGE NUM="249" -->in a fan-like radiation to the opposite side of the bone; the other, having origin in the lateral (outer) portion of the shaft and arching upward and medially to end in the upper surface of the greater trochanter, neck and head. These two systems intersect each other at right angles.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="20"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;20</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<I>A. Medial</I> (<I>Compressive</I>) <I>System of Trabecul&#230;.</I>&#151;As the compact bone of the medial (inner) part of the shaft nears the head of the femur it gradually becomes thinner and finally reaches the articular surface of the head as a very thin layer. From a point at about the lower level of the lesser trochanter, 2 1/2 to 3 inches from the lower limit of the articular surface of the head, the trabecul&#230; branch off from the shaft in smooth curves, spreading radially to cross to the opposite side in two well-defined groups: a lower, or secondary group, and an upper, or principal group.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="21"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;21</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus247.html"><IMG WIDTH=213 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image247.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i247"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>247&#150; </FONT>Frontal longitudinal midsection of upper femur. (<A HREF="/107/illus247.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<I>a. The Secondary Compressive Group.</I>&#151;This group of trabecul&#230; leaves the inner border of the shaft beginning at about the level of the lesser trochanter, and for a distance of almost 2 inches along the curving shaft, with which the separate trabecul&#230; make an angle of about 45 degrees. <!-- PAGE NUM="250" -->They curve outwardly and upwardly to cross in radiating smooth curves to the opposite side. The lower filaments end in the region of the greater trochanter: the adjacent filaments above these pursue a more nearly vertical course and end in the upper portion of the neck of the femur. The trabecul&#230; of this group are thin and with wide spaces between them. As they traverse the space between the medial and lateral surfaces of the bone they cross at right angles the system of curved trabecul&#230; which arise from the lateral (outer) portion of the shaft. (<A HREF="/107/illus247.html">Figs. 247</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus249.html">249.</A>)</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="22"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;22</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<I>b. The Principal Compressive Group.</I>&#151;This group of trabecul&#230; (<A HREF="/107/illus247.html">Figs. 247</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus249.html">249.</A>) springs from the medial portion of the shaft just above the group above-described, and spreads upward and in slightly radial smooth curved lines to reach the upper portion of the articular surface of the head of the femur. These trabecul&#230; are placed very closely together and are the thickest ones seen in the upper femur. They are a prolongation of the shaft from which they spring in straight lines which gradually curve to meet at right-angles the articular surface. There is no change as they cross the epiphyseal line. They also intersect at right-angles the system of lines which rise from the lateral side of the femur.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="23"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;23</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus248.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=236 SRC="/107/Images/small/image248.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i248"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>248&#150; </FONT>Diagram of the lines of stress in the upper femur, based upon the mathematical analysis of the right femur. These result from the combination of the different kinds of stresses at each point in the femur. (After Koch.) (<A HREF="/107/illus248.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;This system of principal and secondary compressive trabecul&#230; corresponds in position and in curvature with the lines of maximum compressive stress, which were traced out in the mathematical analysis of this portion of the femur. (<A HREF="/107/illus247.html">Figs. 247</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus250.html">250.</A>)</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="24"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;24</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<I>B. Lateral</I> (<I>Tensile</I>) <I>System of Trabecul&#230;.</I>&#151;As the compact bone of the outer portion of the shaft approaches the greater trochanter it gradually decreases in thickness. Beginning at a point about 1 inch below the level of the lower border of the greater trochanter, numerous thin trabecul&#230; are given off from the outer portion of the shaft. These trabecul&#230; lie in three distinct groups.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="25"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;25</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<I>c. The Greater Trochanter Group.</I>&#151;These trabecul&#230; rise from the outer part of the shaft just below the greater trochanter and rise in thin, curving lines to cross the region of the greater trochanter and end in its upper surface. Some of these filaments are poorly defined. This group <!-- PAGE NUM="251" --><!-- PAGE NUM="252" -->intersects the trabecul&#230; of group (<I>a</I>) which rise from the opposite side. The trabecul&#230; of this group evidently carry small stresses, as is shown by their slenderness.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="26"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;26</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus249.html"><IMG WIDTH=86 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image249.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i249"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>249&#150; </FONT>Frontal longitudinal midsection of left femur. Taken from the same subject as the one that was analyzed and shown in <A HREF="/107/illus248.html">Figs. 248</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus250.html">250.</A> 4/9 of natural size. (After Koch.) (<A HREF="/107/illus249.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus250.html"><IMG WIDTH=142 HEIGHT=400 SRC="/107/Images/small/image250.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i250"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>250&#150; </FONT>Diagram of the computed lines of maximum stress in the normal femur. The section numbers 2, 4, 6, 8, etc., show the positions of the transverse sections analyzed. The amounts of the maximum tensile and compressive stress at the various sections are given for a load of 100 pounds on the femur-head. For the standing position (&#147;at attention&#148;) these stresses are multiplied by 0.6, for walking by 1.6 and for running by 3.2. (After Koch.) (<A HREF="/107/illus250.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<I>d. The Principal Tensile Group.</I>&#151;This group springs from the outer part of the shaft immediately below group <I>c,</I> and curves convexly upward and inward in nearly parallel lines across the neck of the femur and ends in the inferior portion of the head. These trabecul&#230; are somewhat thinner and more, widely spaced than those of the principal compressive group (<I>b</I>). All the trabecul&#230; of this group cross those of groups (<I>a</I>) and (<I>b</I>) at right angles. This group is the most important of the lateral system (tensile) and, as will be shown later, the greatest tensile stresses of the upper femur are carried by the trabecul&#230; of this group.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="27"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;27</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<I>e. The Secondary Tensile Group.</I>&#151;This group consists of the trabecul&#230; which spring from the outer side of the shaft and lie below those of the preceding group. They curve upward and medially across the axis of the femur and end more or less irregularly after crossing the midline, but a number of these filaments end in the medial portion of the shaft and neck. They cross at right angles the trabecul&#230; of group (<I>a</I>).</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="28"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;28</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus251.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=239 SRC="/107/Images/small/image251.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i251"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>251&#150; </FONT>Intensity of the maximum tensile and compressive stresses in the upper femur. Computed for the load of 100 pounds on the right femur. Corresponds to the upper part of Fig. 250. (After Koch.) (<A HREF="/107/illus251.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;In general, the trabecul&#230; of the tensile system are lighter in structure than those of the compressive system in corresponding positions. The significance of the difference in thickness of these two systems is that the thickness of the trabecul&#230; varies with the intensity of the stresses at any given point. Comparison of <A HREF="/107/illus247.html">Fig. 247</A> with <A HREF="/107/illus251.html">Fig. 251</A> will show that the trabecul&#230; of the compressive system carry heavier stresses than those of the tensile system in corresponding positions. For example, the maximum tensile stress at section 8 <A HREF="/107/illus251.html">(Fig. 251)</A> in the outermost fiber is 771 pounds per square inch, and at the corresponding point on the compressive side the compressive stress is 954 pounds per square inch. Similar comparisons may be made at other points, which confirm the conclusion that the thickness and closeness of spacing of the trabecul&#230; varies in proportion to the intensity of the stresses carried by them.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="29"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;29</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;It will be seen that the trabecul&#230; lie exactly in the paths of the maximum tensile and compressive stresses (compare <A HREF="/107/illus247.html">Figs. 247,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus248.html">248</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus251.html">251</A>), and hence these trabecul&#230; carry these stresses in the most economical manner. This is in accordance with the well-recognized principle of mechanics that the most direct manner of transmitting stress is in the direction in which the stress acts.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="30"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;30</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="253" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<A HREF="/107/illus249.html">Fig. 249</A> shows a longitudinal frontal section through the left femur, which is the mate of the right femur on which the mathematical analysis was made. In this midsection the system of tensile trabecul&#230;, which rises from the lateral (outer) part of the shaft and crosses over the central area to end in the medial portion of the shaft, neck and head, is clearly shown. This figure also shows the compressive system of trabecul&#230; which rises on the medial portion of the shaft and crosses the central area to end in the head, neck and greater trochanter. By comparing the position of these two systems of trabecul&#230; shown in <A HREF="/107/illus249.html">Fig. 249</A> with the lines of maximum and minimum stresses shown in <A HREF="/107/illus248.html">Figs. 248</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus250.html">250</A> it is seen that the tensile system of trabecul&#230; corresponds exactly with the position of the lines of maximum and minimum tensile stresses which were determined by mathematical analysis. In a similar manner, the compressive system of trabecul&#230; in <A HREF="/107/illus249.html">Fig. 249</A> corresponds exactly with the lines of maximum and minimum compressive stresses computed by mathematical analysis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="31"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;31</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;The amount of vertical shear varies almost uniformly from a maximum of 90 pounds (90 percent. of the load on the femur-head) midway between sections 4 and 6, to a minimum of &#151;5.7 pounds at section 18&#148; <A HREF="/107/illus251.html">(Fig. 251</A>). There is a gradual diminution of the spongy bone from section 6 to section 18 parallel with the diminished intensities of the vertical shear.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="32"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;32</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;1. The trabecul&#230; of the upper femur, as shown in frontal sections, are arranged in two general systems, compressive and tensile, which correspond in position with the lines of maximum and minimum stresses in the femur determined by the mathematical analysis of the femur as a mechanical structure.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="33"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;33</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;2. The thickness and spacing of the trabecul&#230; vary with the intensity of the maximum stresses at various points in the upper femur, being thickest and most closely spaced in the regions where the greatest stresses occur.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="34"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;34</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;3. The amount of bony material in the spongy bone of the upper femur varies in proportion to the intensity of the shearing force at the various sections.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="35"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;35</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;4. The arrangement of the trabecul&#230; in the positions of maximum stresses is such that the greatest strength is secured with a minimum of material.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="36"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;36</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Significance of the Inner Architecture of the Shaft.</I>&#151;1. Economy for resisting shear. The shearing stresses are at a minimum in the shaft. &#147;It is clear that a minimum amount of material will be required to resist the shearing stresses.&#148; As horizontal and vertical shearing stresses are most efficiently resisted by material placed near the neutral plane, in this region a minimum amount of material will be needed near the neutral axis. In the shaft there is very little if any material in the central space, practically the only material near the neutral plane being in the compact bone, but lying at a distance from the neutral axis. This conforms to the requirement of mechanics for economy, as a minimum of material is provided for resisting shearing stresses where these stresses are a minimum.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="37"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;37</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;2. Economy for resisting bending moment. &#147;The bending moment increases from a minimum at section 4 to a maximum between sections 16 and 18, then gradually decreases almost uniformly to 0 near section 75.&#148; &#147;To resist bending moment stresses most effectively the material should be as far from the neutral axis as possible.&#148; It is evident that the hollow shaft of the femur is an efficient structure for resisting bending moment stresses, all of the material in the shaft being relatively at a considerable distance from the neutral axis. It is evident that the hollow shaft provides efficiently for resisting bending moment not only due to the load on the femur-head, but from any other loads tending to produce bending in other planes.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="38"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;38</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;3. Economy for resisting axial stress.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="39"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;39</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The inner architecture of the shaft is adapted to resist in the most efficient manner the combined action of the minimal shearing forces and the axial and maximum bending stresses.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="40"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;40</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The structure of the shaft is such as to secure great strength with a relatively small amount of material.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="41"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;41</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Distal Portion of the Femur.</B>&#151;In frontal section <A HREF="/107/illus249.html">(Fig. 249)</A> in the distal 6 inches of the femur &#147;there are to be seen two main systems of trabecul&#230;, a longitudinal and a transverse system. The trabecul&#230; of the former rise from the inner wall of the shaft and continue in perfectly straight lines parallel to the axis of the shaft and proceed to the epiphyseal line, whence they continue in more or less curved lines to meet the articular surface of the knee-joint at right angles at every point. Near the center there are a few thin, delicate, longitudinal trabecul&#230; which spring from the longitudinal trabecul&#230; just described, to which they are joined by fine transverse filaments that lie in planes parallel to the sagittal plane.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="42"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;42</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;The trabecul&#230; of the transverse system are somewhat lighter in structure than those of the longitudinal system, and consist of numerous trabecul&#230; at right angles to the latter.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="43"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;43</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;As the distal end of the femur is approached the shaft gradually becomes thinner until the articular surface is reached, where there remains only a thin shell of compact bone. With the gradual thinning of the compact bone of the shaft, there is a simultaneous increase in the amount of the spongy bone, and a gradual flaring of the femur which gives this portion of the bone a gradually increasing gross area of cross-section.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="44"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;44</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;There is a marked thickening of the shell of bone in the region of the intercondyloid fossa where the anterior and posterior crucial ligaments are attached. This thickened area is about <!-- PAGE NUM="254" -->0.4 inch in diameter and consists of compact bone from which a number of thick trabecul&#230; pass at right angles to the main longitudinal system. The inner structure of the bone is here evidently adapted to the efficient distribution of the stresses arising from this ligamentary attachment.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="45"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;45</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus252.html"><IMG WIDTH=149 HEIGHT=225 SRC="/107/Images/small/image252.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i252"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>252&#150; </FONT>Plan of ossification of the femur. From five centers. (<A HREF="/107/illus252.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;Near the distal end of the femur the longitudinal trabecul&#230; gradually assume curved paths and end perpendicularly to the articular surface at every point. Such a structure is in accordance with the principles of mechanics, as stresses can be communicated through a frictionless joint only in a direction perpendicular to the joint surface at every point.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="46"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;46</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;With practically no increase in the amount of bony material used, there is a greatly increased stability produced by the expansion of the lower femur from a hollow shaft of compact bone to a structure of much larger cross-section almost entirely composed of spongy bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="47"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;47</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus253.html"><IMG WIDTH=105 HEIGHT=200 SRC="/107/Images/small/image253.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i253"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>253&#150; </FONT>Epiphysial lines of femur in a young adult. Anterior aspect. The lines of attachment of the articular capsules are in blue. (<A HREF="/107/illus253.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus254.html"><IMG WIDTH=119 HEIGHT=200 SRC="/107/Images/small/image254.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i254"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>254&#150; </FONT>Epiphysial lines of femur in a young adult. Posterior aspect. The lines of attachment of the articular capsules are in blue. (<A HREF="/107/illus254.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;&#147;<I>Significance of the Inner Architecture of the Distal Part of the Femur.</I>&#151;The function of the lower end of the femur is to transmit through a hinged joint the loads carried by the femur. For stability the width of the bearing on which the hinge action occurs should be relatively large. For economy of material the expansion of the end bearing should be as lightly constructed as is consistent with proper strength. In accordance with the principles of mechanics&#133; &#133;, <!-- PAGE NUM="255" -->the most efficient manner in which stresses are transmitted is by the arrangement of the resisting material in lines parallel to the direction in which the stresses occur and in the paths taken by the stresses. Theoretically the most efficient manner to attain these objects would be to prolong the innermost filaments of the bone as straight lines parallel to the longitudinal axis of the bone, and gradually to flare the outer shell of compact bone outward, and continuing to give off filaments of bone parallel to the longitudinal axis as the distal end of the femur is approached. These filaments should be well-braced transversely and each should carry its proportionate part of the total load, parallel to the longitudinal axis, transmitting it eventually to the articular surface, and in a direction perpendicular to that surface.&#148;</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="48"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;48</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Referring to <A HREF="/107/illus249.html">Fig. 249,</A> it is seen that the large expansion of the bone is produced by the gradual transition of the hollow shaft of compact bone to cancellated bone, resulting in the production of a much larger volume. The trabecul&#230; are given off from the shaft in lines parallel to the longitudinal axis, and are braced transversely by two series of trabecul&#230; at right angles to each other, in the same manner as required theoretically for economy.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="49"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;49</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;Although the action of the muscles exerts an appreciable effect on the stresses in the femur, it is relatively small and very complex to analyze and has not been considered in the above analysis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="50"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;50</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification (<A HREF="/107/illus252.html">Figs. 252,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus253.html">253,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus254.html">254</A>).</B>&#151;The femur is ossified from <I>five</I> centers: one for the body, one for the head, one for each trochanter, and one for the lower extremity. Of all the long bones, except the clavicle, it is the first to show traces of ossification; this commences in the middle of the body, at about the seventh week of fetal life, and rapidly extends upward and downward. The centers in the epiphyses appear in the following order: in the lower end of the bone, at the ninth month of fetal life (from this center the condyles and epicondyles are formed); in the head, at the end of the first year after birth; in the greater trochanter, during the fourth year; and in the lesser trochanter, between the thirteenth and fourteenth years.
