The order in which the epiphyses are joined to the body is the reverse of that of their appearance; they are not united until after puberty, the lesser trochanter being first joined, then the greater, then the head, and, lastly, the inferior extremity, which is not united until the twentieth year.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="51"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;51</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6c. 4. The Patella</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Knee Cap)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>patella</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus255.html">Figs. 255,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus256.html">256</A>) is a flat, triangular bone, situated on the front of the knee-joint. It is usually regarded as a sesamoid bone, developed in the tendon of the Quadriceps femoris, and resembles these bones (1) in being developed in a tendon; (2) in its center of ossification presenting a knotty or tuberculated outline; (3) in being composed mainly of dense cancellous tissue. It serves to protect the front of the joint, and increases the leverage of the Quadriceps femoris by making it act at a greater angle. It has an anterior and a posterior surface three borders, and an apex.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus255.html"><IMG WIDTH=98 HEIGHT=100 SRC="/107/Images/small/image255.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i255"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>255&#150; </FONT>Right patella. Anterior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus255.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus256.html"><IMG WIDTH=98 HEIGHT=100 SRC="/107/Images/small/image256.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i256"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>256&#150; </FONT>Right patella. Posterior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus256.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface</B> is convex, perforated by small apertures for the passage of nutrient vessels, and marked by numerous rough, longitudinal stri&#230;. This surface is covered, in the recent state, by an expansion from the tendon of the Quadriceps femoris, which is continuous below with the superficial fibers of the ligamentum patell&#230;. It is separated from the integument by a bursa. The <B>posterior surface</B> presents above a smooth, oval, articular area, divided into two facets by a vertical ridge; the ridge corresponds to the groove on the patellar surface of the femur, and the facets to the medial and lateral parts of the same surface; the lateral facet is the broader and deeper. Below the articular surface is a rough, convex, non-articular area, the lower half of which gives attachment to the ligamentum patell&#230;; the upper half is separated from the head of the tibia by adipose tissue.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>base</B> or <B>superior border</B> is thick, and sloped from behind, downward, and forward: it gives attachment to that portion of the Quadriceps femoris <!-- PAGE NUM="256" -->which is derived from the Rectus femoris and Vastus intermedius. The <B>medial</B> and <B>lateral borders</B> are thinner and converge below: they give attachment to those portions of the Quadriceps femoris which are derived from the Vasti lateralis and medialis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Apex.</B>&#151;The apex is pointed, and gives attachment to the ligamentum patell&#230;.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The patella consists of a nearly uniform dense cancellous tissue, covered by a thin compact lamina. The cancelli immediately beneath the anterior surface are arranged parallel with it. In the rest of the bone they radiate from the articular surface toward the other parts of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The patella is ossified from a single center, which usually makes its appearance in the second or third year, but may be delayed until the sixth year. More rarely, the bone is developed by two centers, placed side by side. Ossification is completed about the age of puberty.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulation.</B>&#151;The patella articulates with the femur.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6c. 5. The Tibia</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Shin Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>tibia</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus258.html">Figs. 258,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus259.html">259</A>) is situated at the medial side of the leg, and, excepting the femur, is the longest bone of the skeleton. It is prismoid in form, expanded above, where it enters into the knee-joint, contracted in the lower third, and again enlarged but to a lesser extent below. In the male, its direction is vertical, and parallel with the bone of the opposite side; but in the female it has a slightly oblique direction downward and lateralward, to compensate for the greater obliquity of the femur. It has a <B>body</B> and <B>two extremities.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus257.html"><IMG WIDTH=162 HEIGHT=162 SRC="/107/Images/small/image257.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i257"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>257&#150; </FONT>Upper surface of right tibia. (<A HREF="/107/illus257.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Upper Extremity (<I>proximal extremity</I>).</B>&#151;The upper extremity is large, and expanded into two eminences, the <B>medial</B> and <B>lateral condyles.</B> The <B>superior articular surface</B> presents two smooth articular facets <A HREF="/107/illus257.html">(Fig. 257</A>). The medial facet, oval in shape, is slightly concave from side to side, and from before backward. The lateral, nearly circular, is concave from side to side, but slightly convex from before backward, especially at its posterior part, where it is prolonged on to the posterior surface for a short distance. The central portions of these facets articulate with the condyles of the femur, while their peripheral portions support the menisci of the knee-joint, which here intervene between the two bones. Between the articular facets, but nearer the posterior than the anterior aspect of the bone, is the <B>intercondyloid eminence</B> (<I>spine of tibia</I>), surmounted on either side by a prominent tubercle, on to the sides of which the articular facets are prolonged; in front of and behind the intercondyloid eminence are rough depressions for the attachment of the anterior and posterior cruciate ligaments and the menisci. The <B>anterior surfaces</B> of the condyles are continuous with one another, forming a large somewhat flattened area; this area is triangular, broad above, and perforated by large vascular foramina; narrow below where it ends in a large oblong elevation, the <B>tuberosity of the tibia,</B> which gives attachment to the ligamentum patell&#230;; a bursa intervenes between the deep surface of the ligament and the part of the bone immediately above the tuberosity. <I>Posteriorly,</I> the condyles are separated from each other by a shallow depression, the <B>posterior intercondyloid fossa,</B> which gives attachment to part of the posterior cruciate ligament of the knee-joint. The <B>medial condyle</B> presents posteriorly a deep transverse groove, for the insertion of the tendon of <!-- PAGE NUM="257" -->the Semimembranosus. Its <I>medial surface</I> is convex, rough, and prominent; it gives attachment to the tibial collateral ligament. The <B>lateral condyle</B> presents posteriorly a flat articular facet, nearly circular in form, directed downward, backward, and lateralward, for articulation with the head of the fibula. Its <I>lateral surface</I> is convex, rough, and prominent in front: on it is an eminence, situated on a level with the upper border of the tuberosity and at the junction of its anterior and lateral surfaces, for the attachment of the iliotibial band. Just below this a part of the Extensor digitorum longus takes origin and a slip from the tendon of the Biceps femoris is inserted.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body or Shaft (<I>corpus tibi&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The body has three borders and three surfaces.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior crest</B> or <B>border,</B> the most prominent of the three, commences above at the tuberosity, and ends below at the anterior margin of the medial malleolus. It is sinuous and prominent in the upper two-thirds of its extent, but smooth and rounded below; it gives attachment to the deep fascia of the leg.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial border</B> is smooth and rounded above and below, but more prominent in the center; it begins at the back part of the medial condyle, and ends at the posterior border of the medial malleolus; its upper part gives attachment to the tibial collateral ligament of the knee-joint to the extent of about 5 cm., and insertion to some fibers of the Popliteus; from its middle third some fibers of the Soleus and Flexor digitorum longus take origin.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>interosseous crest</B> or <B>lateral border</B> is thin and prominent, especially its central part, and gives attachment to the interosseous membrane; it commences above in front of the fibular articular facet, and <!-- PAGE NUM="258" -->bifurcates below, to form the boundaries of a triangular rough surface, for the attachment of the interosseous ligament connecting the tibia and fibula.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus258.html"><IMG WIDTH=173 HEIGHT=500 SRC="/107/Images/small/image258.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i258"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>258&#150; </FONT>Bones of the right leg. Anterior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus258.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>medial surface</B> is smooth, convex, and broader above than below; its upper third, directed forward and medialward, is covered by the aponeurosis derived from the tendon of the Sartorius, and by the tendons of the Gracilis and Semitendinosus, all of which are inserted nearly as far forward as the anterior crest; in the rest of its extent it is subcutaneous.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral surface</B> is narrower than the medial; its upper two-thirds present a shallow groove for the origin of the Tibialis anterior; its lower third is smooth, convex, curves gradually forward to the anterior aspect of the bone, and is covered by the tendons of the Tibialis anterior, Extensor hallucis longus, and Extensor digitorum longus, arranged in this order from the medial side.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus259.html"><IMG WIDTH=183 HEIGHT=500 SRC="/107/Images/small/image259.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i259"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>259&#150; </FONT>Bones of the right leg. Posterior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus259.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>posterior surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus259.html">(Fig. 259)</A> presents, at its upper part, a prominent ridge, the <B>popliteal line,</B> which extends obliquely downward from the back part of the articular facet for the fibula to the medial border, at the junction of its upper and middle thirds; it marks the lower limit of the insertion of the Popliteus, serves for the attachment of the fascia covering this muscle, and gives origin to part of the Soleus, Flexor digitorum longus, and Tibialis posterior. The triangular area, above this line, gives insertion to the Popliteus. The middle third of the posterior surface is divided by a vertical ridge into two parts; the ridge begins at the popliteal line and is well-marked above, but indistinct below; the medial and broader portion gives origin to the Flexor digitorum longus, the lateral and narrower to part of the Tibialis posterior. The <!-- PAGE NUM="259" -->remaining part of the posterior surface is smooth and covered by the Tibialis posterior, Flexor digitorum longus, and Flexor hallucis longus. Immediately below the popliteal line is the <B>nutrient foramen,</B> which is large and directed obliquely downward.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Lower Extremity (<I>distal extremity</I>).</B>&#151;The lower extremity, much smaller than the upper, presents five surfaces; it is prolonged downward on its medial side as a strong process, the <B>medial malleolus.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>inferior articular surface</B> is quadrilateral, and smooth for articulation with the talus. It is concave from before backward, broader in front than behind, and traversed from before backward by a slight elevation, separating two depressions. It is continuous with that on the medial malleolus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus260.html"><IMG WIDTH=172 HEIGHT=225 SRC="/107/Images/small/image260.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i260"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>260&#150; </FONT>Plan of ossification of the tibia. From three centers. (<A HREF="/107/illus260.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus261.html"><IMG WIDTH=115 HEIGHT=200 SRC="/107/Images/small/image261.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i261"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>261&#150; </FONT>Epiphysial lines of tibia and fibula in a young adult. Anterior aspect. (<A HREF="/107/illus261.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>anterior surface</B> of the lower extremity is smooth and rounded above, and covered by the tendons of the Extensor muscles; its lower margin presents a rough transverse depression for the attachment of the articular capsule of the ankle-joint.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>posterior surface</B> is traversed by a shallow groove directed obliquely downward and medialward, continuous with a similar groove on the posterior surface of the talus and serving for the passage of the tendon of the Flexor hallucis longus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral surface</B> presents a triangular rough depression for the attachment of the inferior interosseous ligament connecting it with the fibula; the lower part of this depression is smooth, covered with cartilage in the fresh state, and articulates with the fibula. The surface is bounded by two prominent borders, continuous above with the interosseous crest; they afford attachment to the anterior and posterior ligaments of the lateral malleolus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial surface</B> is prolonged downward to form a strong pyramidal process, flattened from without inward&#151;the <B>medial malleolus.</B> The <I>medial surface</I> of this process is convex and subcutaneous; its <I>lateral</I> or <I>articular surface</I> is smooth and slightly concave, and articulates with the talus; its <I>anterior border</I> is rough, for <!-- PAGE NUM="260" -->the attachment of the anterior fibers of the deltoid ligament of the ankle-joint; its <I>posterior border</I> presents a broad groove, the <B>malleolar sulcus,</B> directed obliquely downward and medialward, and occasionally double; this sulcus lodges the tendons of the Tibialis posterior and Flexor digitorum longus. The <I>summit</I> of the medial malleolus is marked by a rough depression behind, for the attachment of the deltoid ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Structure.</B>&#151;The structure of the tibia is like that of the other long bones. The compact wall of the body is thickest at the junction of the middle and lower thirds of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The tibia is ossified from <I>three</I> centers (<A HREF="/107/illus260.html">Figs. 260,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus261.html">261</A>): one for the body and one for either extremity. Ossification begins in the center of the body, about the seventh week of fetal life, and gradually extends toward the extremities. The center for the upper epiphysis appears before or shortly after birth; it is flattened in form, and has a thin tongue-shaped process in front, which forms the tuberosity <A HREF="/107/illus260.html">(Fig. 260</A>); that for the lower epiphysis appears in the second year. The lower epiphysis joins the body at about the eighteenth, and the upper one joins about the twentieth year. Two additional centers occasionally exist, one for the tongue-shaped process of the upper epiphysis, which forms the tuberosity, and one for the medial malleolus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6c. 6. The Fibula</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Calf Bone)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>fibula</B> (<A HREF="/107/illus258.html">Figs. 258,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus259.html">259</A>) is placed on the lateral side of the tibia, with which it is connected above and below. It is the smaller of the two bones, and, in proportion to its length, the most slender of all the long bones. Its upper extremity is small, placed toward the back of the head of the tibia, below the level of the knee-joint, and excluded from the formation of this joint. Its lower extremity inclines a little forward, so as to be on a plane anterior to that of the upper end; it projects below the tibia, and forms the lateral part of the ankle-joint. The bone has a <B>body</B> and <B>two extremities.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Upper Extremity or Head (<I>capitulum fibul&#156;; proximal extremity</I>).</B>&#151;The upper extremity is of an irregular quadrate form, presenting above a flattened articular surface, directed upward, forward, and medialward, for articulation with a corresponding surface on the lateral condyle of the tibia. On the lateral side is a thick and rough prominence continued behind into a pointed eminence, the <B>apex</B> (<I>styloid process</I>), which projects upward from the posterior part of the head. The prominence, at its upper and lateral part, gives attachment to the tendon of the Biceps femoris and to the fibular collateral ligament of the knee-joint, the ligament dividing the tendon into two parts. The remaining part of the circumference of the head is rough, for the attachment of muscles and ligaments. It presents in front a tubercle for the origin of the upper and anterior fibers of the Peron&#230;us longus, and a surface for the attachment of the anterior ligament of the head; and behind, another tubercle, for the attachment of the posterior ligament of the head and the origin of the upper fibers of the Soleus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body or Shaft (<I>corpus fibul&#230;</I>).</B>&#151;The body presents four borders&#151;the antero-lateral, the antero-medial, the postero-lateral, and the postero-medial; and four surfaces&#151;anterior, posterior, medial, and lateral.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Borders.</B>&#151;The <B>antero-lateral border</B> begins above in front of the head, runs vertically downward to a little below the middle of the bone, and then curving somewhat lateralward, bifurcates so as to embrace a triangular subcutaneous surface immediately above the lateral malleolus. This border gives attachment to an intermuscular septum, which separates the Extensor muscles on the anterior surface of the leg from the Peron&#230;i longus and brevis on the lateral surface.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>antero-medial border,</B> or <B>interosseous crest,</B> is situated close to the medial side of the preceding, and runs nearly parallel with it in the upper third of its extent, but diverges from it in the lower two-thirds. It begins above just beneath the head of the bone (sometimes it is quite indistinct for about 2.5 cm. below the head), and ends at the apex of a rough triangular surface immediately above the <!-- PAGE NUM="261" -->articular facet of the lateral malleolus. It serves for the attachment of the interosseous membrane, which separates the Extensor muscles in front from the Flexor muscles behind.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>postero-lateral border</B> is prominent; it begins above at the apex, and ends below in the posterior border of the lateral malleolus. It is directed lateralward above, backward in the middle of its course, backward, and a little medialward below, and gives attachment to an aponeurosis which separates the Peron&#230;i on the lateral surface from the Flexor muscles on the posterior surface.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>postero-medial border,</B> sometimes called the <B>oblique line,</B> begins above at the medial side of the head, and ends by becoming continuous with the interosseous crest at the lower fourth of the bone. It is well-marked and prominent at the upper and middle parts of the bone. It gives attachment to an aponeurosis which separates the Tibialis posterior from the Soleus and Flexor hallucis longus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus262.html"><IMG WIDTH=151 HEIGHT=200 SRC="/107/Images/small/image262.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i262"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>262&#150; </FONT>Lower extremity of right fibula. Medial aspect. (<A HREF="/107/illus262.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus263.html"><IMG WIDTH=122 HEIGHT=225 SRC="/107/Images/small/image263.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i263"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>263&#150; </FONT>Plan of ossification of the fibula. From three centers. (<A HREF="/107/illus263.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface</B> is the interval between the antero-lateral and antero-medial borders. It is extremely narrow and flat in the upper third of its extent; broader and grooved longitudinally in its lower third; it serves for the origin of three muscles: the Extensor digitorum longus, Extensor hallucis longus, and Peron&#230;us tertius.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>posterior surface</B> is the space included between the postero-lateral and the postero-medial borders; it is continuous below with the triangular area above the articular surface of the lateral malleolus; it is directed backward above, backward and medialward at its middle, directly medialward below. Its upper third is rough, for the origin of the Soleus; its lower part presents a triangular surface, connected to the tibia by a strong interosseous ligament; the intervening part of the surface is covered by the fibers of origin of the Flexor hallucis longus. Near the middle of this surface is the nutrient foramen, which is directed downward.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial surface</B> is the interval included between the antero-medial and the postero-medial borders. It is grooved for the origin of the Tibialis posterior.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="262" -->

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral surface</B> is the space between the antero-lateral and postero-lateral borders. It is broad, and often deeply grooved; it is directed lateralward in the upper two-thirds of its course, backward in the lower third, where it is continuous with the posterior border of the lateral malleolus. This surface gives origin to the Peron&#230;i longus and brevis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Lower Extremity or Lateral Malleolus (<I>malleolus lateralis; distal extremity; external malleolus</I>).</B>&#151;The lower extremity is of a pyramidal form, and somewhat flattened from side to side; it descends to a lower level than the medial malleolus. The <B>lateral surface</B> is convex, subcutaneous, and continuous with the triangular, subcutaneous surface on the lateral side of the body. The <B>medial surface</B> <A HREF="/107/illus262.html">(Fig. 262)</A> presents in front a smooth triangular surface, convex from above downward, which articulates with a corresponding surface on the lateral side of the talus. Behind and beneath the articular surface is a rough depression, which gives attachment to the posterior talofibular ligament. The <B>anterior border</B> is thick and rough, and marked below by a depression for the attachment of the anterior talofibular ligament. The <B>posterior border</B> is broad and presents the shallow <B>malleolar sulcus,</B> for the passage of the tendons of the Peron&#230;i longus and brevis. The <B>summit</B> is rounded, and give attachment to the clacaneofibular ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification.</B>&#151;The fibula is ossified from <I>three</I> centers <A HREF="/107/illus263.html">(Fig. 263):</A> one for the body, and one for either end. Ossification begins in the body about the eighth week of fetal life, and extends toward the extremities. At birth the ends are cartilaginous. Ossification commences in the lower end in the second year, and in the upper about the fourth year. The lower epiphysis, the first to ossify, unites with the body about the twentieth year; the upper epiphysis joins about the twenty-fifth year.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus264.html"><IMG WIDTH=135 HEIGHT=225 SRC="/107/Images/small/image264.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i264"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>264&#150; </FONT>Left calcaneus, superior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus264.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus265.html"><IMG WIDTH=130 HEIGHT=250 SRC="/107/Images/small/image265.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i265"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>265&#150; </FONT>Left calcaneus, inferior surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus265.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6d. The Foot. 1. The Tarsus</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The skeleton of the foot (<A HREF="/107/illus268.html">Figs. 268</A> and <A HREF="/107/illus269.html">269</A>) consists of three parts: the <B>tarsus, metatarsus,</B> and <B>phalanges.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="263" -->

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;<DIV ALIGN="center"><B>The Tarsus (Ossa Tarsi)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>tarsal bones</B> are seven in number, viz., the <B>calcaneus, talus, cuboid, navicular,</B> and the <B>first, second,</B> and <B>third cuneiforms.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Calcaneus (<I>os calcis</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus264.html">(Figs. 264</A> <A HREF="/107/illus267.html">to 267</A>).</B>&#151;The calcaneus is the largest of the tarsal bones. It is situated at the lower and back part of the foot, serving to transmit the weight of the body to the ground, and forming a strong lever for the muscles of the calf. It is irregularly cuboidal in form, having its long axis directed forward and lateralward; it presents for examination six surfaces.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus266.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=140 SRC="/107/Images/small/image266.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i266"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>266&#150; </FONT>Left calcaneus, lateral surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus266.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus267.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=147 SRC="/107/Images/small/image267.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i267"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>267&#150; </FONT>Left calcaneus, medial surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus267.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>superior surface</B> extends behind on to that part of the bone which projects backward to form the heel. This varies in length in different individuals, is convex from side to side, concave from before backward, and supports a mass of fat placed in front of the tendo calcaneus. In front of this area is a large usually somewhat oval-shaped facet, the <B>posterior articular surface,</B> which looks upward and forward; it is convex from behind forward, and articulates with the posterior calcaneal facet on the under surface of the talus. It is bounded anteriorly by a deep depression which is continued backward and medialward in the form of a groove, the <B>calcaneal sulcus.</B> In the articulated foot this sulcus lies below a similar one on the under surface of the talus, and the two form a canal (<B>sinus tarsi</B>) for the
<!-- PAGE NUM="264" -->
<!-- PAGE NUM="265" -->
<!-- PAGE NUM="266" -->lodgement of the interosseous talocalcaneal ligament. In front and to the medial side of this groove is an elongated facet, concave from behind forward, and with its long axis directed forward and lateralward. This facet is frequently divided into two by a notch: of the two, the posterior, and larger is termed the <B>middle articular surface;</B> it is supported on a projecting process of bone, the <B>sustentaculum tali,</B> and articulates with the middle calcaneal facet on the under surface of the talus; the <B>anterior articular surface</B> is placed on the anterior part of the body, and articulates with the anterior calcaneal facet on the talus. The upper surface, anterior and lateral to the facets, is rough for the attachment of ligaments and for the origin of the Extensor digitorum brevis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus268.html"><IMG WIDTH=319 HEIGHT=597 SRC="/107/Images/small/image268.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i268"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>268&#150; </FONT>Bones of the right foot. Dorsal surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus268.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus269.html"><IMG WIDTH=324 HEIGHT=592 SRC="/107/Images/small/image269.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i269"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>269&#150; </FONT>Bones of the right foot. Plantar surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus269.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>inferior</B> or <B>plantar surface</B> is uneven, wider behind than in front, and convex from side to side; it is bounded posteriorly by a transverse elevation, the <B>calcaneal tuberosity,</B> which is depressed in the middle and prolonged at either end into a process; the <B>lateral process,</B> small, prominent, and rounded, gives origin to part of the Abductor digiti quinti; the <B>medial process,</B> broader and larger, gives attachment, by its prominent medial margin, to the Abductor hallucis, and in front to the Flexor digitorum brevis and the plantar aponeurosis; the depression between the processes gives origin to the Abductor digiti quinti. The rough surface in front of the processes gives attachment to the long plantar ligament, and to the lateral head of the Quadratus plant&#230; while to a prominent tubercle nearer the anterior part of this surface, as well as to a transverse groove in front of the tubercle, is attached the plantar calcaneocuboid ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral surface</B> is broad behind and narrow in front, flat and almost subcutaneous; near its center is a tubercle, for the attachment of the calcaneofibular ligament. At its upper and anterior part, this surface gives attachment to the lateral talocalcaneal ligament; and in front of the tubercle it presents a narrow surface marked by two oblique grooves. The grooves are separated by an elevated ridge, or tubercle, the <B>trochlear process</B> (<I>peroneal tubercle</I>), which varies much in size in different bones. The <B>superior groove</B> transmits the tendon of the Peron&#230;us brevis; the <B>inferior groove,</B> that of the Peron&#230;us longus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial surface</B> is deeply concave; it is directed obliquely downward and forward, and serves for the transmission of the plantar vessels and nerves into the sole of the foot; it affords origin to part of the Quadratus plant&#230;. At its upper and forepart is a horizontal eminence, the <B>sustentaculum tali,</B> which gives attachment to a slip of the tendon of the Tibialis posterior. This eminence is concave above, and articulates with the middle calcaneal articular surface of the talus; below, it is grooved for the tendon of the Flexor hallucis longus; its anterior margin gives attachment to the plantar calcaneonavicular ligament, and its medial, to a part of the deltoid ligament of the ankle-joint.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>anterior</B> or <B>cuboid articular surface</B> is of a somewhat triangular form. It is concave from above downward and lateralward, and convex in a direction at right angles to this. Its medial border gives attachment to the plantar calcaneonavicular ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>posterior surface</B> is prominent, convex, wider below than above, and divisible into three areas. The lowest of these is rough, and covered by the fatty and fibrous tissue of the heel; the middle, also rough, gives insertion to the tendo calcaneus and Plantaris; while the highest is smooth, and is covered by a bursa which intervenes between it and the tendo calcaneus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="9"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;9</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The calcaneus articulates with two bones: the talus and cuboid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="10"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;10</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><B>The Talus (<I>astragalus; ankle bone</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus270.html">(Figs. 270</A> <A HREF="/107/illus273.html">to 273</A>).</B>&#151;The talus is the second largest of the tarsal bones. It occupies the middle and upper part of the tarsus, supporting the tibia above, resting upon the calcaneus below, articulating on either side with the malleoli, and in front with the navicular. It consists of a <B>body,</B> a <B>neck,</B> and a <B>head</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="11"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;11</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="267" -->
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Body (<I>corpus tali</I>).</B>&#151;The <B>superior surface</B> of the body presents, behind, a smooth trochlear surface, the <B>trochlea,</B> for articulation with the tibia. The trochlea is broader in front than behind, convex from before backward, slightly concave from side to side: in front it is continuous with the upper surface of the neck of the bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="12"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;12</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus270.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=203 SRC="/107/Images/small/image270.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i270"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>270&#150; </FONT>Left talus, from above. (<A HREF="/107/illus270.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus271.html"><IMG WIDTH=200 HEIGHT=214 SRC="/107/Images/small/image271.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i271"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>271&#150; </FONT>Left talus, from below. (<A HREF="/107/illus271.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>inferior surface</B> presents two articular areas, the posterior and middle calcaneal surfaces, separated from one another by a deep groove, the <B>sulcus tali.</B> The groove runs obliquely forward and lateralward, becoming gradually broader and deeper in front: in the articulated foot it lies above a similar groove upon the upper surface of the calcaneus, and forms, with it, a canal (<B>sinus tarsi</B>) filled <!-- PAGE NUM="268" -->up in the fresh state by the interosseous talocalcaneal ligament. The <B>posterior calcaneal articular surface</B> is large and of an oval or oblong form. It articulates with the corresponding facet on the upper surface of the calcaneus,<A NAME="txt63">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/63.html#note63">63</A> and is deeply concave in the direction of its long axis which runs forward and lateralward at an angle of about 45&#176; with the median plane of the body. The <B>middle calcaneal articular surface</B> is small, oval in form and slightly convex; it articulates with the upper surface of the sustentaculum tali of the calcaneus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="13"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;13</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>medial surface</B> presents at its upper part a pear-shaped articular facet for the medial malleolus, continuous above with the trochlea; below the articular surface is a rough depression for the attachment of the deep portion of the deltoid ligament of the ankle-joint.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="14"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;14</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus272.html"><IMG WIDTH=250 HEIGHT=113 SRC="/107/Images/small/image272.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i272"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>272&#150; </FONT>Left talus, medial surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus272.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus273.html"><IMG WIDTH=225 HEIGHT=131 SRC="/107/Images/small/image273.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i273"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>273&#150; </FONT>Left talus, lateral surface. (<A HREF="/107/illus273.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>lateral surface</B> carries a large triangular facet, concave from above downward, for articulation with the lateral malleolus; its anterior half is continuous above with the trochlea; and in front of it is a rough depression for the attachment of the anterior talofibular ligament. Between the posterior half of the lateral border of the trochlea and the posterior part of the base of the fibular articular surface is a triangular facet (Fawcett
<A NAME="txt64">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/63.html#note64">64</A>) which comes into contact with the transverse inferior tibiofibular ligament during flexion of the ankle-joint; below the base of this facet is a groove which affords attachment to the posterior talofibular ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="15"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;15</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>posterior surface</B> is narrow, and traversed by a groove running obliquely <!-- PAGE NUM="269" -->downward and medialward, and transmitting the tendon of the Flexor hallucis longus. Lateral to the groove is a prominent tubercle, the <B>posterior process,</B> to which the posterior talofibular ligament is attached; this process is sometimes separated from the rest of the talus, and is then known as the <B>os trigonum.</B> Medial to the groove is a second smaller tubercle.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="16"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;16</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Neck (<I>collum tali</I>).</B>&#151;The neck is directed forward and medialward, and comprises the constricted portion of the bone between the body and the oval head. Its <B>upper</B> and <B>medial surfaces</B> are rough, for the attachment of ligaments; its <B>lateral surface</B> is concave and is continuous below with the deep groove for the interosseous talocalcaneal ligament.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="17"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;17</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Head (<I>caput tali</I>).</B>&#151;The head looks forward and medialward; its <B>anterior articular</B> or <B>navicular surface</B> is large, oval, and convex. Its <B>inferior surface</B> has two facets, which are best seen in the fresh condition. The medial, situated in front of the middle calcaneal facet, is convex, triangular, or semi-oval in shape, and rests on the plantar calcaneonavicular ligament; the lateral, named the <B>anterior calcaneal articular surface,</B> is somewhat flattened, and articulates with the facet on the upper surface of the anterior part of the calcaneus.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="18"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;18</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The talus articulates with <I>four</I> bones: tibia, fibula, calcaneus, and navicular.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="19"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;19</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Cuboid Bone (<I>os cuboideum</I>) (<A HREF="/107/illus274.html">Figs. 274,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus275.html">275</A>).</B>&#151;The cuboid bone is placed on the lateral side of the foot, in front of the calcaneus, and behind the fourth and fifth metatarsal bones. It is of a pyramidal shape, its base being directed medialward.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="20"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;20</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus274.html"><IMG WIDTH=203 HEIGHT=103 SRC="/107/Images/small/image274.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i274"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>274&#150; </FONT>The left cuboid. Antero-medial view. (<A HREF="/107/illus274.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus275.html"><IMG WIDTH=139 HEIGHT=102 SRC="/107/Images/small/image275.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i275"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>275&#150; </FONT>The left cuboid. Postero-lateral view. (<A HREF="/107/illus275.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>dorsal surface,</B> directed upward and lateralward, is rough, for the attachment of ligaments. The <B>plantar surface</B> presents in front a deep groove, the <B>peroneal sulcus,</B> which runs obliquely forward and medialward; it lodges the tendon of the Peron&#230;us longus, and is bounded behind by a prominent ridge, to which the long plantar ligament is attached. The ridge ends laterally in an eminence, the <B>tuberosity,</B> the surface of which presents an oval facet; on this facet glides the sesamoid bone or cartilage frequently found in the tendon of the Peron&#230;us longus. The surface of bone behind the groove is rough, for the attachment of the plantar calcaneocuboid ligament, a few fibers of the Flexor hallucis brevis, and a fasciculus from the tendon of the Tibialis posterior. The <B>lateral surface</B> presents a deep notch formed by the commencement of the peroneal sulcus. The <B>posterior surface</B> is smooth, triangular, and concavo-convex, for articulation with the anterior surface of the calcaneus; its infero-medial angle projects backward as a process which underlies and supports the anterior end of the calcaneus. The <B>anterior surface,</B> of smaller size, but also irregularly triangular, is divided by a vertical ridge into two facets: the medial, quadrilateral in form, articulates with the fourth metatarsal; the lateral, larger and more triangular, articulates with the fifth. The <B>medial surface</B> is broad, irregularly quadrilateral, and presents at its middle and upper part a smooth oval facet, for articulation with the third <!-- PAGE NUM="270" -->cuneiform; and behind this (occasionally) a smaller facet, for articulation with the navicular; it is rough in the rest of its extent, for the attachment of strong interosseous ligaments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="21"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;21</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The cuboid articulates with <I>four</I> bones: the calcaneus, third cuneiform, and fourth and fifth metatarsals; occasionally with a fifth, the navicular.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="22"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;22</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Navicular Bone (<I>os naviculare pedis; scaphoid bone</I>) (<A HREF="/107/illus276.html">Figs. 276,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus277.html">277</A>).</B>&#151;The navicular bone is situated at the medial side of the tarsus, between the talus behind and the cuneiform bones in front.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="23"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;23</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus276.html"><IMG WIDTH=183 HEIGHT=125 SRC="/107/Images/small/image276.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i276"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>276&#150; </FONT>The left navicular. Antero-lateral view. (<A HREF="/107/illus276.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus277.html"><IMG WIDTH=126 HEIGHT=112 SRC="/107/Images/small/image277.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i277"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>277&#150; </FONT>The left navicular. Postero-medial view. (<A HREF="/107/illus277.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface</B> is convex from side to side, and subdivided by two ridges into three facets, for articulation with the three cuneiform bones. The <B>posterior surface</B> is oval, concave, broader laterally than medially, and articulates with the rounded head of the talus. The <B>dorsal surface</B> is convex from side to side, and rough for the attachment of ligaments. The <B>plantar surface</B> is irregular, and also rough for the attachment of ligaments. The <B>medial surface</B> presents a rounded <B>tuberosity,</B> the lower part of which gives attachment to part of the tendon of the Tibialis posterior. The <B>lateral surface</B> is rough and irregular for the attachment of ligaments, and occasionally presents a small facet for articulation with the cuboid bone.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="24"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;24</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The navicular articulates with <I>four</I> bones: the talus and the three cuneiforms; occasionally with a fifth, the cuboid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="25"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;25</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The First Cuneiform Bone (<I>os cuneiform primum; internalcuneiform</I>) (<A HREF="/107/illus278.html">Figs. 278,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus279.html">279</A>).</B>&#151;The first cuneiform bone is the largest of the three cuneiforms. It is situated at the medial side of the foot, between the navicular behind and the base of the first metatarsal in front.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="26"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;26</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus278.html"><IMG WIDTH=87 HEIGHT=150 SRC="/107/Images/small/image278.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i278"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>278&#150; </FONT>The left first cuneiform. Antero-medial view. (<A HREF="/107/illus278.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus279.html"><IMG WIDTH=114 HEIGHT=148 SRC="/107/Images/small/image279.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i279"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>279&#150; </FONT>The left first cuneiform. Postero-lateral view. (<A HREF="/107/illus279.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>medial surface</B> is subcutaneous, broad, and quadrilateral; at its anterior plantar angle is a smooth oval impression, into which part of the tendon of the Tibialis anterior is inserted; in the rest of its extent it is rough for the attachment of ligaments. The <B>lateral surface</B> is concave, presenting, along its superior and posterior borders a narrow L-shaped surface, the vertical limb and posterior part of the horizontal limb of which articulate with the second cuneiform, while the anterior part of the horizontal limb articulates with the second metatarsal <!-- PAGE NUM="271" -->bone: the rest of this surface is rough for the attachment of ligaments and part of the tendon of the Peron&#230;us longus. The <B>anterior surface,</B> kidney-shaped and much larger than the posterior, articulates with the first metatarsal bone. The <B>posterior surface</B> is triangular, concave, and articulates with the most medial and largest of the three facets on the anterior surface of the navicular. The <B>plantar surface</B> is rough, and forms the base of the wedge; at its back part is a tuberosity for the insertion of part of the tendon of the Tibialis posterior. It also gives insertion in front to part of the tendon of the Tibialis anterior. The <B>dorsal surface</B> is the narrow end of the wedge, and is directed upward and lateralward; it is rough for the attachment of ligaments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="27"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;27</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The first cuneiform articulates with <I>four</I> bones: the navicular, second cuneiform, and first and second metatarsals.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="28"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;28</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Second Cuneiform Bone (<I>os cuneiforme secundum; middle cuneiform</I>) (<A HREF="/107/illus280.html">Figs. 280,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus281.html">281</A>).</B>&#151;The second cuneiform bone, the smallest of the three, is of very regular wedge-like form, the thin end being directed downward. It is situated between the other two cuneiforms, and articulates with the navicular behind, and the second metatarsal in front.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="29"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;29</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface,</B> triangular in form, and narrower than the posterior, articulates with the base of the second metatarsal bone. The <B>posterior surface,</B> also triangular, articulates with the intermediate facet on the anterior surface of the navicular. The <B>medial surface</B> carries an L-shaped articular facet, running along the superior and posterior borders, for articulation with the first cuneiform, and is rough in the rest of its extent for the attachment of ligaments. The <B>lateral surface</B> presents posteriorly a smooth facet for articulation with the third cuneiform bone. The <B>dorsal surface</B> forms the base of the wedge; it is quadrilateral and rough for the attachment of ligaments. The <B>plantar surface,</B> sharp and tuberculated, is also rough for the attachment of ligaments, and for the insertion of a slip from the tendon of the Tibialis posterior.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="30"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;30</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus280.html"><IMG WIDTH=92 HEIGHT=100 SRC="/107/Images/small/image280.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i280"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>280&#150; </FONT>The left second cuneiform. Antero-medial view. (<A HREF="/107/illus280.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus281.html"><IMG WIDTH=94 HEIGHT=97 SRC="/107/Images/small/image281.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i281"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>281&#150; </FONT>The left second cuneiform. Postero-lateral view. (<A HREF="/107/illus281.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The second cuneiform articulates with <I>four</I> bones: the navicular, first and third cuneiforms, and second metatarsal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="31"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;31</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Third Cuneiform Bone (<I>os cuneiforme tertium; external cuneiform</I>) (<A HREF="/107/illus282.html">Figs. 282,</A> <A HREF="/107/illus283.html">283</A>).</B>&#151;The third cuneiform bone, intermediate in size between the two preceding, is wedge-shaped, the base being uppermost. It occupies the center of the front row of the tarsal bones, between the second cuneiform medially, the cuboid laterally, the navicular behind, and the third metatarsal in front.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="32"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;32</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Surfaces.</B>&#151;The <B>anterior surface,</B> triangular in form, articulates with the third metatarsal bone. The <B>posterior surface</B> articulates with the lateral facet on the anterior surface of the navicular, and is rough below for the attachment of ligamentous fibers. The <B>medial surface</B> presents an anterior and a posterior articular facet, separated by a rough depression: the anterior, sometimes divided, articulates with the lateral side of the base of the second metatarsal bone; the posterior skirts the posterior border, and articulates with the second cuneiform; the rough depression gives attachment to an interosseous ligament. The <B>lateral surface</B> also presents two articular facets, separated by a rough non-articular area; the anterior facet, situated at the superior angle of the bone, is small and semi-oval in shape, and articulates with the medial side of the base of the fourth metatarsal bone; <!-- PAGE NUM="272" -->the posterior and larger one is triangular or oval, and articulates with the cuboid; the rough, non-articular area serves for the attachment of an interosseous ligament. The three facets for articulation with the three metatarsal bones are continuous with one another; those for articulation with the second cuneiform and navicular are also continuous, but that for articulation with the cuboid is usually separate. The <B>dorsal surface</B> is of an oblong form, its postero-lateral angle being prolonged backward. The <B>plantar surface</B> is a rounded margin, and serves for the attachment of part of the tendon of the Tibialis posterior, part of the Flexor hallucis brevis, and ligaments.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="33"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;33</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The third cuneiform articulates with <I>six</I> bones: the navicular, second cuneiform, cuboid, and second, third, and fourth metatarsals.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="34"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;34</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus282.html"><IMG WIDTH=156 HEIGHT=96 SRC="/107/Images/small/image282.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i282"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>282&#150; </FONT>The left third cuneiform. Postero-medial view. (<A HREF="/107/illus282.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus283.html"><IMG WIDTH=104 HEIGHT=114 SRC="/107/Images/small/image283.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i283"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>283&#150; </FONT>The third left cuneiform. Antero-lateral view. (<A HREF="/107/illus283.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6d. 2. The Metatarsus</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD>The <B>metatarsus</B> consists of five bones which are numbered from the medial side (<I>ossa metatarsalia</I> I.-V.); each presents for examination a <B>body</B> and <B>two extremities.</B></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Common Characteristics of the Metatarsal Bones.</B>&#151;The body is prismoid in form, tapers gradually from the tarsal to the phalangeal extremity, and is curved longitudinally, so as to be concave below, slightly convex above. The <B>base</B> or <B>posterior extremity</B> is wedge-shaped, articulating proximally with the tarsal bones, and by its sides with the contiguous metatarsal bones: its dorsal and plantar surfaces are rough for the attachment of ligaments. The <B>head</B> or <B>anterior extremity</B> presents a convex articular surface, oblong from above downward, and extending farther backward below than above. Its sides are flattened, and on each is a depression, surmounted by a tubercle, for ligamentous attachment. Its plantar surface is grooved antero-posteriorly for the passage of the Flexor tendons, and marked on either side by an articular eminence continuous with the terminal articular surface.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Characteristics of the Individual Metatarsal Bones. &#151; The First Metatarsal Bone (<I>os metatarsale I; metatarsal bone of the great toe</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus284.html">(Fig. 284</A>).</B>&#151;The first metatarsal bone is remarkable for its great thickness, and is the shortest of the metatarsal bones. The <B>body</B> is strong, and of well-marked prismoid form. The <B>base</B> presents, as a rule, no articular facets on its sides, but occasionally on the lateral side there is an oval facet, by which it articulates with the second metatarsal. Its proximal articular surface is of large size and kidney-shaped; its circumference is grooved, for the tarsometatarsal ligaments, and medially gives insertion to part of the tendon of the Tibialis anterior; its plantar angle presents a rough oval prominence for the insertion of the tendon of the Peron&#230;us longus. The <B>head</B> is large; on its plantar surface are two grooved facets, on which glide sesamoid bones; the facets are separated by a smooth elevation.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="273" -->
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus284.html"><IMG WIDTH=160 HEIGHT=225 SRC="/107/Images/small/image284.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i284"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>284&#150; </FONT>The first metatarsal. (Left.) (<A HREF="/107/illus284.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus285.html"><IMG WIDTH=144 HEIGHT=225 SRC="/107/Images/small/image285.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i285"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>285&#150; </FONT>The second metatarsal. (Left.) (<A HREF="/107/illus285.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus286.html"><IMG WIDTH=145 HEIGHT=200 SRC="/107/Images/small/image286.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i286"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>286&#150; </FONT>The third metatarsal. (Left.) (<A HREF="/107/illus286.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus287.html"><IMG WIDTH=150 HEIGHT=200 SRC="/107/Images/small/image287.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i287"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>287&#150; </FONT>The fourth metatarsal. (Left.) (<A HREF="/107/illus287.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Second Metatarsal Bone (<I>os metatarsale II</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus285.html">(Fig. 285</A>).</B>&#151;The second metatarsal bone is the longest of the metatarsal bones, being prolonged backward into the recess formed by the three cuneiform bones. Its <B>base</B> is broad above, narrow and rough below. It presents four articular surfaces: one behind, of a triangular form, for articulation with the second cuneiform; one at the upper part <!-- PAGE NUM="274" -->of its medial surface, for articulation with the first cuneiform; and two on its lateral surface, an upper and lower, separated by a rough non-articular interval. Each of these lateral articular surfaces is divided into two by a vertical ridge; the two anterior facets articulate with the third metatarsal; the two posterior (sometimes continuous) with the third cuneiform. A fifth facet is occasionally present for articulation with the first metatarsal; it is oval in shape, and is situated on the medial side of the body near the base.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Third Metatarsal Bone (<I>os metatarsale III</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus286.html">(Fig. 286</A>).</B>&#151;The third metatarsal bone articulates proximally, by means of a triangular smooth surface, with the third cuneiform; medially, by two facets, with the second metatarsal; and laterally, by a single facet, with the fourth metatarsal. This last facet is situated at the dorsal angle of the base.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus288.html"><IMG WIDTH=133 HEIGHT=187 SRC="/107/Images/small/image288.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i288"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>288&#150; </FONT>The fifth metatarsal. (Left.) (<A HREF="/107/illus288.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Fourth Metatarsal Bone (<I>os metatarsale IV</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus287.html">(Fig. 287</A>).</B>&#151;The fourth metatarsal bone is smaller in size than the preceding; its <B>base</B> presents an oblique quadrilateral surface for articulation with the cuboid; a smooth facet on the medial side, divided by a ridge into an anterior portion for articulation with the third metatarsal, and a posterior portion for articulation with the third cuneiform; on the lateral side a single facet, for articulation with the fifth metatarsal.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><script type="text/javascript"><!--
google_ad_client = "ca-pub-5691076175788952";
/* rect */
google_ad_slot = "9207548925";
google_ad_width = 300;
google_ad_height = 250;
//-->
</script>
<script type="text/javascript"
src="http://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/pagead/show_ads.js">
</script></DIV></TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

</TD><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;</TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>The Fifth Metatarsal Bone (<I>os metatarsale V</I>) <A HREF="/107/illus288.html">(Fig. 288</A>).</B>&#151;The fifth metatarsal bone is recognized by a rough eminence, the <B>tuberosity,</B> on the lateral side of its base. The <B>base</B> articulates behind, by a triangular surface cut obliquely in a transverse direction, with the cuboid; and medially, with the fourth metatarsal. On the medial part of its dorsal surface is inserted the tendon of the Peron&#230;us tertius and on the dorsal surface of the tuberosity that of the Peron&#230;us brevis. A strong band of the plantar aponeurosis connects the projecting part of the tuberosity with the lateral process of the tuberosity of the calcaneus. The plantar surface of the base is grooved for the tendon of the Abductor digiti quinti, and gives origin to the Flexor digiti quinti brevis.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;The base of each metatarsal bone articulates with one or more of the tarsal bones, and the head with one of the first row of phalanges. The first metatarsal articulates with the first cuneiform, the second with all three cuneiforms, the third with the third cuneiform, the fourth with the third cuneiform and the cuboid, and the fifth with the cuboid.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!-- PAGE NUM="275" -->


</TABLE>


<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER">Henry Gray&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">(1821&#150;1865).</FONT>&nbsp;&nbsp;Anatomy of the Human Body.&nbsp;&nbsp;<FONT SIZE="-1">1918.</FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT COLOR="#9C9C63" SIZE="+1"><B>6d. 3. The Phalanges of the Foot</B></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN" "http://www.w3.org/TR/REC-html40/loose.dtd">


<TR><TD><DIV ALIGN="center"><B>(Phalanges Digitorum Pedis)</B></DIV><BR><BR>The <B>phalanges</B> of the foot correspond, in number and general arrangement, with those of the hand; there are two in the great toe, and three in each of the other toes. They differ from them, however, in their size, the bodies being much reduced in length, and, especially in the first row, laterally compressed.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="1"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>First Row.</B>&#151;The <B>body</B> of each is compressed from side to side, convex above, concave below. The <B>base</B> is concave; and the <B>head</B> presents a trochlear surface for articulation with the second phalanx.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="2"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Second Row.</B>&#151;The phalanges of the second row are remarkably small and short, but rather broader than those of the first row.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="3"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;3</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>ungual phalanges,</B> in form, resemble those of the fingers; but they are smaller and are flattened from above downward; each presents a broad base for articulation with the corresponding bone of the second row, and an expanded distal extremity for the support of the nail and end of the toe.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="4"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;4</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<!--- FIG ---><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><A HREF="/107/illus289.html"><IMG WIDTH=246 HEIGHT=300 SRC="/107/Images/small/image289.jpg" BORDER="0"></A><A NAME="i289"></A></CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD><BR><CENTER><FONT SIZE="-1">F<FONT SIZE="-2">IG. </FONT>289&#150; </FONT>Plan of ossification of the foot. (<A HREF="/107/illus289.html">See enlarged image</A>)</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Articulations.</B>&#151;In the second, third, fourth, and fifth toes the phalanges of the first row articulate behind with the metatarsal bones, and in front with the second phalanges, which in their turn articulate with the first and third: the ungual phalanges articulate with the second.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="5"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;5</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>
<TR><TD>&nbsp;</TD></TR><TR><TD><B>Ossification of the Bones of the Foot <A HREF="/107/illus289.html">(Fig. 289</A>).</B>&#151;The <B>tarsal bones</B> are each ossified from a <I>single</I> center, excepting the calcaneus, which has an epiphysis for its posterior extremity. The centers make their appearance in the following order: calcaneus at the sixth month of fetal life; <!-- PAGE NUM="276" -->talus, about the seventh month; cuboid, at the ninth month; third cuneiform, during the first year; first cuneiform, in the third year; second cuneiform and navicular, in the fourth year. The epiphysis for the posterior extremity of the calcaneus appears at the tenth year, and unites with the rest of the bone soon after puberty. The posterior process of the talus is sometimes ossified from a separate center, and may remain distinct from the main mass of the bone, when it is named the <I>os trigonum.</I></TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="6"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;6</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>metatarsal bones</B> are each ossified from <I>two</I> centers: one for the body, and one for the head, of the second, third, fourth, and fifth metatarsals; one for the body, and one for the base, of the first metatarsal. <A NAME="txt65">&nbsp;</A><A HREF="/107/65.html#note65">65</A> Ossification commences in the center of the body about the ninth week, and extends toward either extremity. The center for the base of the first metatarsal appears about the third year; the centers for the heads of the other bones between the fifth and eighth years; they join the bodies between the eighteenth and twentieth years.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="7"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;7</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>

<TR><TD>&nbsp;&nbsp;The <B>phalanges</B> are each ossified from <I>two</I> centers: one for the body, and one for the base. The center for the body appears about the tenth week, that for the base between the fourth and tenth years; it joins the body about the eighteenth year.</TD><TD VALIGN= TOP ALIGN=RIGHT><FONT SIZE="-2"><A NAME ="8"><I>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;8</I></A></FONT></TD></TR>


</TABLE>
